The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates the six valid moods of the third figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six valid moods of the third figure by its premise types and state its conclusion.

Core Idea
Six moods, each with a unique combination of quantity and quality, yield valid conclusions in the third figure.

After establishing the conditions, the author lists the six productive moods of the third figure. Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its two premises, and the conclusion is always particular. The list moves from the most straightforward (both universal affirmative) to more complex combinations involving negative and particular premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood number
The passage lists six moods in order. For each, note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the major and minor premises.
The minor premise is mentioned first in the description, e.g., 'from two universal affirmatives' means both premises are universal and affirmative.
2
Determine the conclusion type
All conclusions in the third figure are particular. If both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative; if one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative.
For example, mood 2 has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, giving a particular negative conclusion.
3
Match the example given
Each mood is illustrated with a concrete example using variables B (middle), C (minor term), A (major term). For instance, mood 1: 'Every B is C, every B is A, so some C is A'.
The example helps to internalize the pattern.
4
Understand the proof methods
The passage briefly mentions two proof methods for each mood: reductio (khalf) and reduction to the first figure (radd). These validate the mood.
The details of these methods are explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما الشكل الثالث: فشرطه إيجاب الصغرى – وإلا لحصل الاختلاف - وكلّيّة إحدى مقدمتيه - وإلا لكان البعض المحكوم عليه بالأصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالأكبر، فلم تجب التعدية.
وضروبة الناتجة ستة:
الأول: من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: «كل (ب ج) وكلّ (ب ا) فبعض (ج)» بالخلف - و هو ضم نقيض النتيجة إلى الصغرى لينتج نقيض الكبرى - وبالرد إلى الأوّل بعكس الصغرى.
الثاني: من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب ج) ولا شيء من (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف وبعكس الصغرى.
الثالث: من موجبتين والكبرى كلية ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: «بعض ب (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف وبعكس الصغرى، وبفرض موضوع الجزئية (د)، فـ«كلّ (دب) وكلّ (ب) (ا) فكل (دا)» ثم نقول: «كل (د ج) وكلّ (دا) فبعض (ج ا)» وهو المطلوب.
الرابع: من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) ج) ولا شيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف وبعكس الصغرى والافتراض.
الخامس: من موجبتين والصغرى كلية ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كـلّ ب ج) وبعض (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف، وبعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة والافتراض.
السادس: من موجبة كلّيّة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: كلّ ب (ج) وبعض (ب) ليس (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف والافتراض إن كانت السالبة مركبة.
أقول: يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثالث بحسب كيفية المقدمات إيجاب الصغرى، وبحسب الكمية كلية إحدى المقدمتين.
أما إيجاب الصغرى: فلأنها لو كانت سالبة فالكبرى إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، وأيا ما كان يحصل الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج: أما إذا كانت موجبة فكقولنا: «لاشيء من الإنسان بفرس وكـلّ إنسان حيوان - أوناطق»، فالحق في الأول الإيجاب، وفي الثاني السلب. وأما إذا كانت سالبة فكما إذا بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا ولاشيء من الإنسان بصهال أو حمار والصادق في الأول الإيجاب، وفي الثاني السلب. وأما كلّيّة إحدى المقدمتين فلأنهما لو كانتا جزئيتين احتمل أن يكون البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأصغر، فلم يجب تعدية الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، كقولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعضه فرس والحكم على بعض الحيوان بالفرسية لا يتعدّى إلى البعض المحكوم عليه بالإنسانية.
وباعتبار هذين الشرطين تحصل الضروب ستة، لأن اشتراط إيجاب الصغرى حذف ثمانية أضرب - كما في الأوّل - واشتراط كلية إحداهما حذف ضربين آخرين وهما الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الموجبة الجزئية.
He said: As for the third figure, its condition is the affirmation of the minor—otherwise difference would occur—and the universality of one of its two premises—otherwise the part judged by the minor would not be the part judged by the major, so transitivity would not hold. Its productive moods are six: First: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' by reductio—which is joining the contradictory of the conclusion to the minor to produce the contradictory of the major—and by reduction to the first figure by conversion of the minor. Second: from two universals with the major negative, produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by reductio and by conversion of the minor. Third: from two affirmatives with the major universal, produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' by reductio and by conversion of the minor, and by assuming the subject of the particular as D, so 'Every D is B and every B is A, so every D is A', then we say: 'Every D is C and every D is A, so some C is A' which is what is required. Fourth: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by reductio, by conversion of the minor, and by supposition. Fifth: from two affirmatives with the minor universal, produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C and some B is A, so some C is A' by reductio, and by conversion of the major and making it the minor, then conversion of the conclusion, and by supposition. Sixth: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C and some B is not A, so some C is not A' by reductio and by supposition if the negative is compound. I say: For the production of the third figure, the condition regarding the quality of the premises is the affirmation of the minor, and regarding quantity is the universality of one of the two premises. As for the affirmation of the minor: because if it were negative, then the major is either affirmative or negative, and in either case difference occurs causing non-production: if the major is affirmative, as in our saying: 'No human is a horse and every human is an animal—or rational'—the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. And if the major is negative, as if we replace the major with our saying: 'No human is a neighing animal or a donkey'—and the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. And as for the universality of one of the two premises: because if both were particular, it is possible that the part of the middle judged by the major is not the part of the middle judged by the minor, so transitivity of the judgment from the middle to the minor does not necessarily hold, as in our saying: 'Some animal is human and some animal is horse'—the judgment on some animal as being a horse does not transfer to the part judged as being human. And with consideration of these two conditions, six moods are obtained, because the condition of affirming the minor removes eight moods—as in the first [figure]—and the condition of universality of one of them removes two other moods, namely the two particular majors with the particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyya explains the conditions and moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first states that the third figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and at least one premise to be universal. If these conditions are not met, no valid conclusion follows. Then he enumerates the six productive moods, each with a concrete example using variables (e.g., 'Every B is C') and methods of proof: either by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), where the contradictory of the conclusion is assumed together with the minor to produce the contradictory of the major, or by reduction to the first figure through conversion or supposition. For the third mood, he also uses a hypothetical term D to convert a particular premise into a universal. The commentary then explains the rationale behind the conditions: if the minor is negative, the premises can be arranged so that both conclusions (affirmative and negative) would be equally plausible (as in the example with 'human', 'horse', and 'animal'), leading to 'ikhtilaf' (difference) which prevents sound inference. If both premises are particular, the middle term may not connect the same individuals, so transitivity fails. He ends by noting that these two conditions reduce the possible 16 premise-pairs down to 6 productive moods. This passage is a typical sharh expansion, providing both the compressed matn and the logical justification.

Key Terms
ضروب
موجبتين كليتين
سالبة جزئية
الكبرى كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the conclusion of the second mood of the third figure?
A) Particular affirmative
B) Particular negative
C) Universal affirmative
D) Universal negative

Key Takeaway
The third figure has exactly six valid moods, each with a particular conclusion, and they are distinguished by the quantity and quality of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which mood of the third figure is being used in the following argument: 'Every raisin drink is intoxicating, some raisin drink is nabidh, therefore some nabidh is intoxicating.' Then check if the premises satisfy the conditions (affirmative minor, one universal).
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism on any fiqh topic using mood 4 (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major).

The Syllogism

Proof Methods: Reductio and Reduction to First Figure
Author outlines proof methods for the third-figure moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two proof methods (reductio and reduction to the first figure) to validate any third-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Reductio assumes the contradictory of the conclusion; reduction converts premises to the first figure, which is self-evidently valid.

After listing the six moods, the author briefly mentions two methods for proving them: al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) and al-radd ilā al-awwal (reduction to the first figure). The commentary expands on these, showing how they work with examples. This lesson explains both methods and their logical foundations.

Guided Path
1
Understand reductio (al-khalf)
To prove a mood, assume the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one premise to derive the contradictory of the other premise. This shows that if the premises are true, the conclusion must be true.
The passage defines khalf as 'joining the contradictory of the conclusion to the minor to produce the contradictory of the major'.
2
Follow a khalf example
For mood 1 (both universal affirmative), assume the conclusion 'some C is A' is false, so its contradictory 'no C is A' is true. Combine with minor 'every B is C' to get 'no B is A', which contradicts the major 'every B is A'. Hence the conclusion must hold.
This is a standard reductio structure.
3
Understand reduction to the first figure (al-radd ilā al-awwal)
Convert one or both premises to transform the syllogism into a first-figure form, which is immediately valid. The first figure requires the middle to be subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
The passage uses conversion (aks) of the minor to achieve this.
4
Apply reduction to mood 1
For mood 1, convert the minor 'every B is C' to 'some C is B'. Then we have: 'some C is B' (minor) and 'every B is A' (major). This is a first-figure syllogism (Ferio? Actually it's a valid first-figure mood: minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative, conclusion particular affirmative? Check: The middle is B, so the argument is 'some C is B, every B is A, so some C is A' – that is a valid first-figure mood (Darii). Hence the original third-figure mood is valid.
The passage mentions 'by conversion of the minor' for mood 1.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take mood 4 (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major) from the passage: 'Some B is C, no B is A, therefore some C is not A'. Prove it using reductio: assume the contradictory 'every C is A' and combine with the minor to derive a contradiction with the major. Write out the steps.
?
Convert the premises of mood 3 (some B is C, every B is A) to reduce it to the first figure. Use the method of supposition mentioned (assume D as the subject of the particular).

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for the Third Figure and Six Valid Moods
Author states the two conditions for the third figure and lists the resulting six valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism and enumerate the six resulting moods.

Core Idea
The third figure requires an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise; only six of sixteen combinations survive.

In the third figure of the categorical syllogism, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The author lays down two indispensable conditions—the minor must be affirmative, and one premise must be universal—and then shows how these conditions reduce the possible moods from sixteen to six. Understanding these constraints is essential for constructing sound syllogisms in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Locate in the Arabic where the author states: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) one of the two premises must be universal. These are the two conditions for any third-figure syllogism to be productive.
The conditions are stated after 'أقول: يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثالث بحسب كيفية المقدمات إيجاب الصغرى، وبحسب الكمية كلية إحدى المقدمتين.'
2
Test the first condition with examples
The author justifies the affirmative minor condition: if the minor were negative, whether the major is affirmative or negative, the premises would yield contradictory results for different instantiations. Examine the examples provided with 'man', 'horse', 'animal', etc.
Notice that from a negative minor, you cannot derive a single conclusion that covers all cases.
3
Test the second condition with examples
The author justifies the universality condition: if both premises are particular, the middle term may refer to different subsets in each premise, so no transfer of judgment occurs. Study the example: 'Some animal is human, some animal is horse' – no conclusion about humans and horses follows.
The key insight is the lack of a guaranteed overlap when both premises are particular.
4
Count the valid moods
Starting from 16 possible premise combinations (four types for each premise), eliminate the 8 where the minor is negative, then eliminate the 2 where both premises are particular. Verify that exactly 6 remain.
The elimination mirrors the first figure: 'as in the first' refers to a similar count.
5
Recognize the proof method
The author mentions that some moods are proved by reductio (khalf) and assumption (iftirad), especially when the negative is compound. Understand that this is a standard technique for demonstrating validity.
These methods are used when direct Aristotlean proof is not possible.

القياس
2.1
السادس من موجبة كلّيّة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: كلّ ب (ج) وبعض (ب) ليس (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)) بالخلف والافتراض إن كانت السالبة مركبة.
أقول: يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثالث بحسب كيفية المقدمات إيجاب الصغرى، وبحسب الكمية كلية إحدى المقدمتين.
أما إيجاب الصغرى : فلأنها لو كانت سالبة فالكبرى إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، وأيا ما كان يحصل الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج : أما إذا كانت موجبة فكقولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بفرس وكـلّ إنسان حيوان - أوناطق»، فالحق في الأول الإيجاب، وفي الثاني السلب. وأما إذا كانت سالبة فكما إذا بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا ولاشيء من الإنسان بصهال أو حمار والصادق في الأول الإيجاب، وفي الثاني السلب. وأما كلّيّة إحدى المقدمتين فلأنهما لو كانتا جزئيتين احتمل أن يكون البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأصغر ، فلم يجب تعدية الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، كقولنا : بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعضه فرس والحكـم علـى بعـض الحـيـوان بالفرسية لا يتعدّى إلى البعض المحكوم عليه بالإنسانية.
وباعتبار هذين الشرطين تحصل الضروب ستة، لأن اشتراط إيجاب الصغرى حذف ثمانية أضرب - كما في الأوّل - واشتراط كلية إحداهما حذف ضربين ،آخرين وهما الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الموجبة الجزئية.
The sixth [mood] from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major produces a particular negative, as we say: Every B is C, and some B is not A, so some C is not A — by reductio and assumption if the negative is compound. I say: For the third figure to produce, the minor must be affirmative, and one of the two premises must be universal. As for the affirmation of the minor: because if it were negative, then the major is either affirmative or negative, and whichever it is, a difference occurs causing non-production: if it is affirmative, as we say: 'Nothing of man is a horse, and every man is an animal — or rational', then the truth in the first is affirmation, and in the second is negation. And if it is negative, as if we replace the major with our saying: 'Nothing of man is a neigh or a donkey', and the truth in the first is affirmation, and in the second is negation. As for the universality of one of the two premises: because if they were both particular, it is possible that the part of the middle subject of the major is not the part of the middle subject of the minor, so the judgment from the middle to the minor is not necessarily transmitted, as in our saying: Some animal is man and some animal is horse, and the judgment on some animal of horseness does not extend to that part judged to be human. And considering these two conditions, six moods result, because the condition of the affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods — as in the first — and the condition of the universality of one of them eliminates two other moods, namely the two particular majors with the particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for valid syllogisms in the third figure. The author begins by stating the sixth mood (likely from a prior enumeration) as an example: universal affirmative minor and particular negative major produce a particular negative. He then lays down two necessary conditions for any third-figure syllogism: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) one of the two premises must be universal. He justifies the first condition by showing that if the minor were negative, regardless of the quality of the major, the premises would yield contradictory results for different instantiations, leading to non-production. For instance, if the minor is negative (e.g., 'No man is a horse') and the major is affirmative (e.g., 'Every man is an animal'), then from 'No man is a horse' and 'Every man is an animal' one cannot conclude universally about animals — some animals are horses, some are not. Similarly, if the major is negative, the same problem arises. He justifies the second condition by noting that if both premises are particular, the middle term may refer to different subsets in each premise, so the judgment cannot be transferred from the middle to the minor term. For example, 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' do not allow us to conclude anything about humans and horses. After establishing these conditions, he counts the valid moods: originally there are 16 possible combinations (from four premise types times four), but requiring an affirmative minor eliminates 8 (the ones with a negative minor), and requiring one universal eliminates 2 more (the ones with both particulars), leaving 6 valid moods. This enumeration matches the standard account of the third figure.

إيجاب الصغرى
كلية إحدى المقدمتين
الخلف والافتراض

The third figure of categorical syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise and at least one universal premise, reducing possible moods to six.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
First Two Moods of the Third Figure: Reductio and Conversion Proofs
Author explains first two moods of third figure with proofs and reason for particular conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the validity of the first two moods of the third figure using reductio ad absurdum and conversion, and explain why they yield only particular conclusions.

Core Idea
The third figure only produces particular conclusions because the minor term can be more general than the major.

In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. This passage details the first two moods—both universal premises, one affirmative and one negative—and their proofs. You will learn two methods to reduce these moods to the first figure: reductio and conversion. The author also clarifies why these moods cannot yield universal conclusions, a fundamental constraint that applies to all moods of this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood components
For the first mood, the premises are 'Every B is C' (minor) and 'Every B is A' (major). The middle term is B; the minor term is C (subject of conclusion) and major term is A (predicate of conclusion). The conclusion is 'Some C is A'. Note the figure is third (middle term subject in both premises). For the second mood, the major premise is negative: 'No B is A'.
The mood names are Darapti (AAA-3) and Felapton (EAO-3).
2
Apply reductio proof for first mood
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion: 'No C is A'. Combine with the minor premise 'Every B is C' to get 'No B is A' (by first figure, Celarent). But this contradicts the major premise 'Every B is A'. Hence the assumption is false, so 'Some C is A' must be true.
Reductio relies on assuming the opposite of the conclusion.
3
Apply conversion proof for first mood
Convert the minor premise 'Every B is C' by accidental conversion to 'Some C is B'. Now we have 'Some C is B' and 'Every B is A' in the first figure (Darii), yielding 'Some C is A' directly.
Conversion works only because the minor is universal affirmative.
4
Explain why no universal conclusion
The minor term (C) may be more general in scope than the major term (A). For example, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational' would give 'Some animal is rational', not 'Every animal is rational', because not all animals are rational. Similarly for negative cases. Since the first two moods are the strongest for affirmation and negation, if they cannot yield universals, no other mood can.
This is a key insight about the third figure's limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا : «كل (ب ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا) بوجهين :
أحدهما الخلف، وطريقه في هذا الشكل أن يجعل نقيض النتيجة الكلية (ن : لكليته كبرى - إذ هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية – وصغرى ينتج القياس لإيجابها صغرى، فينتظم منهما قياس من الشكل الأول، لماينا في الكبرى، فيقال : لولم يصدق «بعض ( ج ) » لصدق «لاشيء من (ج 1) » و «كل (ب (ج) ولاشيء من (ج 1) » ينتج «لاشيء من (ب )» وكان الكبرى «كل ب ا هذا خلف.
وثانيهما عكس الصغرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الأول وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة بعينها.
الثاني من كلّيتين والكبرى سالبة، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : «كـلّ (ب) ج) ولاشيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف، ويعكس الصغرى كما سلف في الضرب الأول بلافرق.
وإنما لم ينتج هذان الضربان كلّيّة لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر، وامتناع إيجاب الأخص لكل أفراد الأعم أوسلبه عنها، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان وكلّ إنسان ناطق» أو «لاشيء من الإنسان بفرس»؛ وإذا لم ينتجا الكلية لم ينتجه شيء من الضروب الباقية، لأن الضرب الأوّل أخص الضروب المنتجة للإيجاب والضرب الثاني أخص الضروب المنتجة للسلب وعدم إنتاج الأخص مستلزم لعدم إنتاج الأعم.
The first [mood] from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as our saying: 'Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' in two ways: One of them is reductio, and its method in this figure is to make the contradictory of the universal conclusion (note: because of its universality it is major? — since this figure only produces particulars) and a minor which the syllogism makes a minor due to its affirmation, so a syllogism from the first figure is composed, from what is in the major, so it is said: If 'some C is A' were not true, then 'nothing from C is A' would be true, and 'every B is C' and 'nothing from C is A' produce 'nothing from B is A', but the major was 'every B is A', this is a contradiction. And the second way is conversion of the minor to return to the first figure and produce exactly the required conclusion. The second [mood] from two universal [premises] and the major negative produces a particular negative, as our saying: 'Every B is C and nothing from B is A, so some C is not A' by reductio, and he converts the minor as earlier in the first mood with no difference. And these two moods do not produce a universal because it is possible that the minor term is more general than the major, and it is impossible to affirm the more specific for all individuals of the more general or to deny it of them, as our saying: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' or 'Nothing from human is a horse'; and if they do not produce the universal, nothing from the remaining moods produces it, because the first mood is the most specific of the productive moods for affirmation, and the second mood is the most specific of the productive moods for negation, and the non-production of the more specific entails the non-production of the more general.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage details the first two moods of the third figure, their proofs, and explains why they yield only particular conclusions. The first mood (both premises universal affirmative) yields a particular affirmative conclusion. The author gives two proof methods: (1) reductio (khalf): assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion (i.e., that no C is A) and combine it with the minor (every B is C) to derive a contradiction with the major (every B is A). (2) Conversion of the minor: convert 'every B is C' to 'some C is B' (by accidental conversion), then in the first figure we have 'some C is B' and 'every B is A' giving 'some C is A'. The second mood (both universal, major negative) yields a particular negative. Its proof uses reductio: assume the contradictory (every C is A) with the minor (every B is C) gives 'every B is A', contradicting the major (no B is A). Conversion also works: convert the minor to 'some C is B' and combine with 'no B is A' to get 'some C is not A' in the first figure. Then the author explains why these moods do not produce universal conclusions: because the minor term (C in the example) may be more general than the major (e.g., 'animal' is more general than 'human'), and it is impossible to assert or deny a specific property (e.g., rationality) of every individual of a more general class (since not all animals are rational). Therefore, the conclusion must be particular. The author notes that since these are the strongest moods for affirmation and negation, if they fail to give universal conclusions, no other mood will.

?

Use reductio to prove its validity by assuming the contradictory and deriving a contradiction with the premises.

The Six Valid Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates and proves the six valid moods of the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six valid moods of the second figure and identify the type of conclusion (particular affirmative or negative) for each mood.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only particular conclusions due to its structural limitation.

In this passage, Qutb al-Din al-Razi presents the six valid moods of the second figure, extending the classification from al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its two premises, and the author shows that they yield only particular conclusions—never universal. Understanding these moods is essential for validating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
List the six mood pairs
From the passage, extract each of the six premise combinations and their corresponding conclusions. Notice the pattern: the first two moods have universal premises, the rest have at least one particular premise.
The six moods are explicitly enumerated: (1) two universal affirmatives → particular affirmative; (2) universal affirmative + universal negative → particular negative; (3) particular affirmative + universal affirmative → particular affirmative; (4) particular affirmative + universal negative → particular negative; (5) universal affirmative + particular affirmative → particular affirmative; (6) universal affirmative + particular negative → particular negative.
2
Identify the conclusion type for each
For each mood, note whether the conclusion is particular affirmative (بعض C A) or particular negative (بعض C ليس A). All six are particular.
3
Understand why no universal conclusion
The author argues using the principle of akhass (most specific): the first mood is the most specific for affirmation, the second for negation. Since these fail to produce a universal, no other mood can.
The concrete example given: 'كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق' shows that a universal conclusion (e.g., 'every animal is rational') would be false.
4
Recognize the three proof methods
Although not the focus, note that each mood is proved by khalf (reductio), aks al-sughra (conversion of minor), or iftirad (supposition). These methods are detailed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بوجهين: أحدهما الخلف، وطريقه في هذا الشكل أن يجعل نقيض النتيجة الكلية (لكليته كبرى - إذ هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية – وصغرى) فينتظم منهما قياس من الشكل الأول، فيقال: لولم يصدق «بعض (ج) (ا)» لصدق «لا شيء من (ج) (ا)» و«كل (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ج) (ا)» ينتج «لا شيء من (ب) (ا)» وكانت الكبرى «كل (ب) (ا)» هذا خلف. وثانيهما عكس الصغرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الأول وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة بعينها. الثاني من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف، ويعكس الصغرى كما سلف في الضرب الأول بلا فرق. وإنما لم ينتج هذان الضربان كلية لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر، وامتناع إيجاب الأخص لكل أفراد الأعم أو سلبه عنها، كقولنا: «كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق» أو «لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس»؛ وإذا لم ينتجا الكلية لم ينتجه شيء من الضروب الباقية، لأن الضرب الأول أخص الضروب المنتجة للإيجاب والضرب الثاني أخص الضروب المنتجة للسلب وعدم إنتاج الأخص مستلزم لعدم إنتاج الأعم. الثالث من موجبتين والكبرى كلية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف وبعكس الصغرى - وهو ظاهر - والافتراض: وهو أن يفرض موضوع الجزئية (د) فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (ج)، فتضم المقدمة الأولى إلى كبرى القياس لينتج من الشكل الأول كل (د) (ا)، ثم تجعلها كبرى للمقدمة الثانية لينتج من أول هذا الشكل بعض (ج) (ا) وهو المطلوب. الرابع من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالطرق الثلاثة - والكل ظاهر. الخامس من موجبتين والصغرى كلية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وبعض (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف والافتراض: وهو فرض موضوع الكبرى (د) فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (ا)، فيجعل المقدمة الأولى صغرى وصغرى الأصل كبرى، فـ«كل (د) (ب) وكل (ب) (ج)» ينتج من الشكل الأول كل (د) (ج) وتجعلها صغرى للمقدمة الثانية هكذا: «كل (د) (ج) وكل (د) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» وهو المطلوب. ويعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة؛ لا بعكس الصغرى لأن الكبرى جزئية، والجزئية لا تصلح لكبروية الشكل الأول. السادس من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وبعض (ب) ليس (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف والافتراض في الكبرى - إن كانت السالبة مركبة ليتحقق وجود الموضوع.
The first mood: from two universal affirmatives yields a particular affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' in two ways. One of them is reductio, and its method in this figure is to take the contradictory of the universal conclusion (because of its universality as major – since this figure produces only particular – and minor) so from them a syllogism of the first figure is formed, and it is said: if 'some C is A' were not true, then 'no C is A' would be true, and 'every B is C and no C is A' yields 'no B is A', while the major premise was 'every B is A'; this is a contradiction. The second way is conversion of the minor to reduce it to the first figure, producing the very desired conclusion. The second mood: from two universal premises with the major negative yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by reductio, and he converts the minor as in the first mood without difference. These two moods do not produce a universal because it is possible for the minor term to be more general than the major, and it is impossible to affirm the more specific of every individual of the more general or to negate it from them, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' or 'No human is a horse'; and if these two do not produce a universal, nothing from the remaining moods produces it, because the first mood is the most specific of the moods that produce affirmation and the second mood is the most specific of the moods that produce negation, and non-production of the most specific entails non-production of the more general. The third mood: from two affirmatives with the major universal yields a particular affirmative, as our statement: 'Some B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' by reductio and conversion of the minor – which is obvious – and supposition: that is, one supposes the subject of the particular to be D, so every D is B and every D is C, then one combines the first premise with the major premise of the syllogism to produce from the first figure every D is A, then one makes it the major premise for the second premise to produce from the first of this figure some C is A, which is the desired. The fourth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Some B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by the three methods – all obvious. The fifth mood: from two affirmatives with the minor universal yields a particular affirmative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and some B is A, so some C is A' by reductio and supposition: that is, one supposes the subject of the major to be D, so every D is B and every D is A, then one makes the first premise minor and the original minor major, so 'every D is B and every B is C' produces from the first figure every D is C, and one makes it the minor premise for the second premise thus: 'every D is C and every D is A, so some C is A', which is the desired. And one converts the major and makes it minor then converts the result; not by conversion of the minor because the major is particular, and the particular is unfit to be major in the first figure. The sixth mood: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and some B is not A, so some C is not A' by reductio and supposition in the major – if the negative is composite to ensure existence of the subject.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, classifying and proving the six valid moods (darab) of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani) of categorical syllogism. The author systematically lists each mood with its premise pair, conclusion, and methods of proof: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), conversion of the minor premise (aks al-sughra), and supposition (al-iftirad). The first mood (two universal affirmatives) yields particular affirmative; the second (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major) yields particular negative. The author then explains why these two moods cannot produce universal conclusions: it is possible for the minor term (asghar) to be more general (a‘amm) than the major (akbar), as in 'every human is an animal and every human is rational' – here 'human' is more specific than 'animal' , so we cannot conclude 'every animal is rational'. Similarly for negatives. Since these are the most specific productive moods for affirmation and negation, their failure to produce universals implies that none of the remaining moods (which involve particular premises) can produce universals either. The third mood (particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major) yields particular affirmative; proof uses reductio, conversion of minor, and supposition – taking a particular instance D that satisfies the minor, then using it to derive the conclusion via first-figure syllogisms. The fourth mood (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major) yields particular negative via all three methods. The fifth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major) yields particular affirmative; the method here uses supposition of the major's subject D, then constructs two first-figure syllogisms. The author notes that conversion of the minor is not possible here because the major is particular and cannot serve as major in the first figure. The sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major) yields particular negative; the author warns that the negative premise must be composite (i.e., asserting existence) for the supposition method to work. Throughout, the examples use variables (b, j, a, d) and occasionally concrete terms like 'human', 'animal', 'horse'.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb): A mood of a syllogism, i.e., a specific combination of quantity and quality of the two premises in a given figure. [Note: Stable across tradition; used consistently for the valid combinations of premises in each figure.]
خلف (khalf): Reductio ad absurdum: proving the conclusion by assuming its contradictory and deriving a contradiction with the given premises.
عكس الصغرى (aks al-sughra): Conversion of the minor premise (swapping the subject and predicate) to transform a second-figure syllogism into a first-figure one.
افتراض (iftirad): Supposition: introducing a new term (D) as the subject of the particular premise to create a universal premise, then using first-figure syllogisms to derive the conclusion. [Note: This method is specially used in the second figure and may not appear in later simpler textbooks.]
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thani): The second figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is predicate in both premises (M-P, S-M).
أخص (akhass): More specific; used to argue that the most specific moods (those with two universal premises) are the most productive, so if they fail to produce universals, more general moods (with particular premises) will also fail.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly describes the conclusion of the first mood of the second figure (two universal affirmatives)?
A) Universal affirmative
B) Particular affirmative
C) Universal negative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
The second figure has six valid moods, all producing particular conclusions; this is established through three proof methods and the principle of akhass.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a stone', determine which mood of the second figure this corresponds to and state the conclusion.
?
Using the principle of akhass, explain why the second figure cannot produce a universal conclusion even from two universal premises.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Proof Methods in the Second Figure: Khalf, Aks, and Iftirad
Author proves each mood using three methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three proof methods – reductio (khalf), conversion of minor (aks), and supposition (iftirad) – to demonstrate the validity of a second-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Three distinct logical methods transform a second-figure syllogism into a first-figure proof.

Qutb al-Din al-Razi not only lists the six moods but also demonstrates how to prove each using three methods: reductio ad absurdum (assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction), conversion of the minor premise (to turn it into a first-figure syllogism), and supposition (introducing a new term D to handle particular premises). These methods are essential tools for a logician to verify the validity of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand khalf (reductio)
Assume the opposite of the conclusion. Combine it with one premise to deduce a statement that contradicts the other premise, thereby proving the original conclusion.
Example: For 'كل (ب) (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)' , assume 'لا شيء من (ج) (ا)'. Then from 'كل (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ج) (ا)' we get 'لا شيء من (ب) (ا)', contradicting 'كل (ب) (ا)'.
2
Understand aks al-sughra (conversion of minor)
Convert the minor premise (e.g., 'some B is C' becomes 'some C is B') to obtain a first-figure syllogism that directly yields the desired conclusion.
This works for moods where the minor premise is universal affirmative (converts to particular affirmative) or universal negative (converts to universal negative).
3
Understand iftirad (supposition)
When a premise is particular, introduce a new term D that is the subject of that particular premise. Use D to create universal premises and then construct first-figure syllogisms to derive the conclusion.
For example, for mood 3 (بعض ب ج, كل ب ا → بعض ج ا), suppose D is the thing that is B and C. Then from 'كل د ب' and 'كل ب ا' we get 'كل د ا', and from 'كل د ب' and 'كل ب ج' we get 'كل د ج'. Then from 'كل د ج' and 'كل د ا' we get 'بعض ج ا'.
4
Compare the methods
Note that khalf works for all moods, aks works when the minor premise is universal, and iftirad works when a premise is particular.
The author explicitly states for each mood which methods apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بوجهين: أحدهما الخلف، وطريقه في هذا الشكل أن يجعل نقيض النتيجة الكلية (لكليته كبرى - إذ هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية – وصغرى) فينتظم منهما قياس من الشكل الأول، فيقال: لولم يصدق «بعض (ج) (ا)» لصدق «لا شيء من (ج) (ا)» و«كل (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ج) (ا)» ينتج «لا شيء من (ب) (ا)» وكانت الكبرى «كل (ب) (ا)» هذا خلف. وثانيهما عكس الصغرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الأول وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة بعينها. الثاني من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف، ويعكس الصغرى كما سلف في الضرب الأول بلا فرق. وإنما لم ينتج هذان الضربان كلية لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر، وامتناع إيجاب الأخص لكل أفراد الأعم أو سلبه عنها، كقولنا: «كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق» أو «لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس»؛ وإذا لم ينتجا الكلية لم ينتجه شيء من الضروب الباقية، لأن الضرب الأول أخص الضروب المنتجة للإيجاب والضرب الثاني أخص الضروب المنتجة للسلب وعدم إنتاج الأخص مستلزم لعدم إنتاج الأعم. الثالث من موجبتين والكبرى كلية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) (ج) وكل (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف وبعكس الصغرى - وهو ظاهر - والافتراض: وهو أن يفرض موضوع الجزئية (د) فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (ج)، فتضم المقدمة الأولى إلى كبرى القياس لينتج من الشكل الأول كل (د) (ا)، ثم تجعلها كبرى للمقدمة الثانية لينتج من أول هذا الشكل بعض (ج) (ا) وهو المطلوب. الرابع من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) (ج) ولا شيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالطرق الثلاثة - والكل ظاهر. الخامس من موجبتين والصغرى كلية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وبعض (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» بالخلف والافتراض: وهو فرض موضوع الكبرى (د) فكل (د) (ب) وكل (د) (ا)، فيجعل المقدمة الأولى صغرى وصغرى الأصل كبرى، فـ«كل (د) (ب) وكل (ب) (ج)» ينتج من الشكل الأول كل (د) (ج) وتجعلها صغرى للمقدمة الثانية هكذا: «كل (د) (ج) وكل (د) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» وهو المطلوب. ويعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة؛ لا بعكس الصغرى لأن الكبرى جزئية، والجزئية لا تصلح لكبروية الشكل الأول. السادس من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا: «كل (ب) (ج) وبعض (ب) ليس (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بالخلف والافتراض في الكبرى - إن كانت السالبة مركبة ليتحقق وجود الموضوع.
The first mood: from two universal affirmatives yields a particular affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' in two ways. One of them is reductio, and its method in this figure is to take the contradictory of the universal conclusion (because of its universality as major – since this figure produces only particular – and minor) so from them a syllogism of the first figure is formed, and it is said: if 'some C is A' were not true, then 'no C is A' would be true, and 'every B is C and no C is A' yields 'no B is A', while the major premise was 'every B is A'; this is a contradiction. The second way is conversion of the minor to reduce it to the first figure, producing the very desired conclusion. The second mood: from two universal premises with the major negative yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by reductio, and he converts the minor as in the first mood without difference. These two moods do not produce a universal because it is possible for the minor term to be more general than the major, and it is impossible to affirm the more specific of every individual of the more general or to negate it from them, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' or 'No human is a horse'; and if these two do not produce a universal, nothing from the remaining moods produces it, because the first mood is the most specific of the moods that produce affirmation and the second mood is the most specific of the moods that produce negation, and non-production of the most specific entails non-production of the more general. The third mood: from two affirmatives with the major universal yields a particular affirmative, as our statement: 'Some B is C and every B is A, so some C is A' by reductio and conversion of the minor – which is obvious – and supposition: that is, one supposes the subject of the particular to be D, so every D is B and every D is C, then one combines the first premise with the major premise of the syllogism to produce from the first figure every D is A, then one makes it the major premise for the second premise to produce from the first of this figure some C is A, which is the desired. The fourth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Some B is C and no B is A, so some C is not A' by the three methods – all obvious. The fifth mood: from two affirmatives with the minor universal yields a particular affirmative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and some B is A, so some C is A' by reductio and supposition: that is, one supposes the subject of the major to be D, so every D is B and every D is A, then one makes the first premise minor and the original minor major, so 'every D is B and every B is C' produces from the first figure every D is C, and one makes it the minor premise for the second premise thus: 'every D is C and every D is A, so some C is A', which is the desired. And one converts the major and makes it minor then converts the result; not by conversion of the minor because the major is particular, and the particular is unfit to be major in the first figure. The sixth mood: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major yields a particular negative, as our statement: 'Every B is C and some B is not A, so some C is not A' by reductio and supposition in the major – if the negative is composite to ensure existence of the subject.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, classifying and proving the six valid moods (darab) of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani) of categorical syllogism. The author systematically lists each mood with its premise pair, conclusion, and methods of proof: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), conversion of the minor premise (aks al-sughra), and supposition (al-iftirad). The first mood (two universal affirmatives) yields particular affirmative; the second (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major) yields particular negative. The author then explains why these two moods cannot produce universal conclusions: it is possible for the minor term (asghar) to be more general (a‘amm) than the major (akbar), as in 'every human is an animal and every human is rational' – here 'human' is more specific than 'animal' , so we cannot conclude 'every animal is rational'. Similarly for negatives. Since these are the most specific productive moods for affirmation and negation, their failure to produce universals implies that none of the remaining moods (which involve particular premises) can produce universals either. The third mood (particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major) yields particular affirmative; proof uses reductio, conversion of minor, and supposition – taking a particular instance D that satisfies the minor, then using it to derive the conclusion via first-figure syllogisms. The fourth mood (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major) yields particular negative via all three methods. The fifth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major) yields particular affirmative; the method here uses supposition of the major's subject D, then constructs two first-figure syllogisms. The author notes that conversion of the minor is not possible here because the major is particular and cannot serve as major in the first figure. The sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major) yields particular negative; the author warns that the negative premise must be composite (i.e., asserting existence) for the supposition method to work. Throughout, the examples use variables (b, j, a, d) and occasionally concrete terms like 'human', 'animal', 'horse'.

Key Terms
خلف (khalf): Reductio ad absurdum.
عكس الصغرى (aks al-sughra): Conversion of the minor premise.
افتراض (iftirad): Supposition of a new term for the subject of a particular premise. [Note: This method is specially used in the second figure.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

The three proof methods – khalf, aks al-sughra, and iftirad – provide a rigorous way to verify the validity of any second-figure syllogism.

The Principle of Akhass: Why Second Figure Yields Only Particulars
Author argues why second figure yields only particular conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain and apply the principle that if the most specific moods (with universal premises) do not produce universal conclusions, then no mood in the second figure can.

Core Idea
The failure of the most specific moods to produce universals proves that all moods yield only particular conclusions.

A key insight in Qutb al-Din's exposition is the argument from 'akhass' (most specific). He argues that since the first two moods, which have both premises universal, produce only particular conclusions, the remaining moods, which have at least one particular premise, are even less likely to produce universals. This logical principle is crucial for understanding the limits of the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first two moods as the most specific
The first mood (two universal affirmatives) and the second mood (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major) are the most specific because they have the strongest premises (both universal).
The author calls the first mood 'أخص الضروب المنتجة للإيجاب' (most specific for affirmation) and the second 'أخص الضروب المنتجة للسلب' (most specific for negation).
2
Understand why they fail to produce universals
The minor term (S) can be more general than the major term (P). For example, 'every human is an animal, every human is rational' – human is more specific than animal, so we cannot conclude 'every animal is rational'.
The author explicitly says: 'لم ينتج هذان الضربان كلية لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر'.
3
Apply the akhass principle
If the most specific moods cannot produce universals, then the less specific moods (with particular premises) definitely cannot. This is a logical principle: if the strongest case fails, weaker cases also fail.
The author states: 'وإذا لم ينتجا الكلية لم ينتجه شيء من الضروب الباقية'.
4
Test with a concrete example
Take a mood with a particular premise, e.g., 'some human is a musician, every human is an animal' → 'some animal is a musician'. This is particular, not universal.
Even if both premises were universal, as in the first mood, we still get particular. So any weaker mood will also be particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does the author say that the first two moods are the most specific for producing affirmation and negation?

D) Because they use supposition.

Key Takeaway
The principle of akhass shows that if the strongest moods fail to produce universal conclusions, then no mood in the second figure can, establishing that only particular conclusions are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the argument 'Every scholar is knowledgeable, every scholar is pious', explain why you cannot conclude 'Every knowledgeable person is pious' (i.e., a universal affirmative) based on the akhass principle.
?
Construct a concrete Islamic example using terms like 'human', 'animal', 'rational' to show why the second figure cannot produce a universal conclusion.

Fourth Figure Conditions and First Four Moods
Conditions and first four productive moods of fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantity and quality conditions for a valid fourth-figure syllogism and verify whether a given mood (AA, IA, EA, AE) is productive.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity depends on specific quantity/quality conditions plus conversion operations.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogism has unique conditions and eight productive moods. This lesson focuses on the first four moods, showing how reversing premise order and converting the conclusion yield valid results. Understanding these conversions is crucial for evaluating arguments presented in non-standard forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the fourth figure
The author states that for the fourth figure, either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one universal. If they differ without universality, no conclusion follows.
This condition ensures the middle term is properly distributed.
2
Understand the two conversion operations
'Aks al-tartib (عكس الترتيب) reverses the order of premises; 'aks al-natija (عكس النتيجة) converts the conclusion by swapping subject and predicate.
These operations transform a fourth-figure syllogism into a first-figure one for evaluation.
3
Apply the condition to the first mood (AA)
Both premises are universal affirmative, meeting the condition. After reversing order and converting the conclusion, we get a particular affirmative.
Example: Every B is J and every A is B → after operations: some J is A.
4
Apply the condition to the third mood (EA)
Both premises universal, minor negative, major affirmative. Condition satisfied. It yields a universal negative conclusion.
Example: Nothing of B is J and every A is B → nothing of J is A.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وضروبه الناتجة ثمانية:

Conditions of Production and the Productive Moods in the Fourth Figure

He said: As for the fourth figure, its condition according to quantity and quality is the affirmation of both premises with universality of the minor, and their difference in quality with the universality of one of them, and the difference that necessitates non-production does not occur.

And its productive moods are eight:

The first: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J and every A is B, so some J is A', by reversing the order then reversing the conclusion.

The second: from two affirmatives with the major particular, it produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J and some A is B, so some J is A', as previously mentioned.

The third: from two universals with the minor negative, it produces a universal negative, as in our saying: 'Nothing of B is J and every A is B, so nothing of J is A', as previously mentioned.

The fourth: from two universals with the minor affirmative, it produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every ...'

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author then lists the first four of its eight productive moods, providing examples and noting the conversion operations needed. The fourth figure has specific conditions regarding quantity and quality: either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one of them universal. If they differ without one being universal, no conclusion follows. The eight moods are enumerated; the first four are given here. The first mood (AA) produces a particular affirmative conclusion by reversing the order and then converting the conclusion. The second (IA with major particular) also yields a particular affirmative. The third (EA with minor negative) yields a universal negative. The fourth (AE with minor affirmative) yields a particular negative, though the example is cut short.

Condition for Productivity in the Fourth Figure
Author states the condition for productivity and lists the eight valid moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given pair of premises in the fourth figure satisfies the condition for productivity.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields a conclusion only if premises are both affirmative with minor universal, or differ in quality with one universal.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogism has a specific condition on quantity and quality that must be met for a conclusion to follow. This lesson unpacks that condition and its justification from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in the passage
The author states: 'شرط إنتاج الشكل الرابع... إما إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما' – either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with at least one universal.
2
Understand why the condition is necessary
The author argues that if neither condition holds, three problematic cases arise: both premises negative, both affirmative with particular minor, or differing quality with both particular – each leads to non-productivity.
Non-productivity means no necessary conclusion follows.
3
Apply the condition to example pairs
Test pairs like both negative ('No human is a horse, no donkey is a human') – no conclusion follows because the truth can be either true or false as illustrated.
The author uses examples with human, horse, donkey, and whinnying thing.
4
Verify with the given justification
The author shows that when both premises are negative, the conclusion can be either true (e.g., 'No whinnying thing is a human') or false (e.g., 'No human is a horse, no donkey is a human' yields 'No donkey is a horse' which is false), so no necessary conclusion.

Not by conversion of the minor premise, because the particular does not occur in the major of the first figure. And not by conversion of the major, because it does not accept conversion, and if its conversion were supposed, it would not be suitable as a minor in the first figure. These moods are placed in these ranks only because the first is the most specific of the productive moods for affirmation and the second is the most specific of the productive moods for negation, and the most specific is nobler, and the third and fourth are given precedence over the last two because they contain the major of the first figure. As for the fourth figure, its condition regarding quantity and quality is either the affirmation of both premises with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them, and there is no difference that causes non-productivity. Its productive moods are eight: The first: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying 'Every B is C and every A is B, so some C is A' by transposition of order then conversion of the conclusion. The second: from two affirmatives with a particular major, produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying 'Every B is C and some A is B, so some C is A' due to what passed. The third: from two universal premises with a negative minor, produces a universal negative, as in our saying 'No B is C and every A is B, so no C is A' due to what passed. The fourth: from two universal premises with an affirmative minor, produces a particular negative, as in our saying 'Every B is C and no A is B, so some C is not A' by conversion of both premises. The seventh: from a universal affirmative minor and a negative particular major, produces a particular negative, as in our saying 'Every B is C and some A is not B, so some C is not A' by conversion of the major to reduce to the third [figure]. The eighth: from a universal negative minor and an affirmative particular major, produces a particular negative, as in our saying 'No B is C and some A is B, so some C is not A' by transposition of order then conversion of the conclusion. The statement: The condition for production of the fourth figure regarding quality and quantity is one of two things: either affirmation of both premises with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them. That is because if neither of them obtained, one of three things would follow: either negation of both premises, or their affirmation with particularity of the minor, or their difference in quality with particularity of both. In all estimations, the difference that necessitates non-productivity occurs. As for when both are negative, then our saying 'No human is a horse and no donkey is a human' would be true, and the truth is negation, or 'No whinnying thing is a human' and the truth is affirmation.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth figure of categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-rabi'), its condition for productivity, and its eight productive moods. The author first explains why certain moods are ranked as they are, then specifies the condition: either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one universal. He then lists all eight moods, each with an example using the letters ج، ب، أ as terms, and indicates the reduction method to the first figure. The first two moods produce affirmative conclusions; the rest produce negative ones. The moods are reduced by conversion (عكس) or transposition (عكس الترتيب) or both. Finally, the author justifies the condition by showing that if neither of the two conditions holds, non-productivity results, illustrated with examples from everyday objects (human, horse, donkey, whinnying thing). The examples are chosen to show that when both premises are negative, the conclusion can be either true or false, so no necessary conclusion follows.

The Eight Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author states the condition for productivity and lists the eight valid moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the eight valid moods of the fourth figure and its reduction method to the first figure.

Core Idea
All eight moods of the fourth figure reduce to the first figure via conversion and transposition.

The fourth figure has eight moods that produce conclusions, each with a specific combination of premises and a reduction method. This lesson walks through the first two affirmative moods and the six negative moods, focusing on their reduction steps.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the moods by conclusion type
Moods 1-2 produce particular affirmative conclusions; Moods 3-8 produce negative conclusions (Mood 3 is universal negative, the rest are particular negative).
2
Examine Mood 1 and Mood 2 (affirmative) and their reductions
Mood 1: both universal affirmatives (AA) – reduction by transposition then conversion of conclusion. Mood 2: major universal affirmative, minor particular affirmative (AI) – reduction similar. Example: 'Every B is C, every A is B' yields 'Some C is A'.
The reduction steps ensure the syllogism becomes valid in the first figure.
3
Examine the negative moods (3-8) and note the reduction method for each
Mood 3: universal premises, minor negative (EA) – reduction to first figure (not specified but similar). Mood 4: universal premises, minor affirmative (AE) – reduction by conversion of both premises. Mood 5: particular affirmative minor, universal negative major (IE) – reduction similar. Mood 6: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major (OA) – reduction by conversion of minor to reduce to second figure. Mood 7: universal affirmative minor, particular negative major (AI with negative major) – reduction by conversion of major to reduce to third figure. Mood 8: universal negative minor, particular affirmative major (EI) – reduction by transposition then conversion of conclusion.
All moods ultimately reduce to the first figure, confirming their validity.
4
Trace the reduction of Mood 1 step-by-step
Original premises: 'كل (ب ج)' and 'كل (اب)'. First, transpose: 'كل (اب)' becomes major, 'كل (ب ج)' becomes minor. Now in first figure: 'كل (اب)' (major: every A is B) and 'كل (ب ج)' (minor: every B is C) yields 'كل (ا ج)' (every A is C) by first figure. Then convert conclusion 'كل (ا ج)' to 'بعض (ج ا)' (some C is A) by conversion of the universal affirmative to particular affirmative. Thus original conclusion 'بعض (ج ا)' is valid.

This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth figure of categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-rabi'), its condition for productivity, and its eight productive moods. The author first explains why certain moods are ranked as they are, then specifies the condition: either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one universal. He then lists all eight moods, each with an example using the letters ج، ب، أ as terms, and indicates the reduction method to the first figure. The first two moods produce affirmative conclusions; the rest produce negative ones. The moods are reduced by conversion (عكس) or transposition (عكس الترتيب) or both. Finally, the author justifies the condition by showing that if neither of the two conditions holds, non-productivity results, illustrated with examples from everyday objects (human, horse, donkey, whinnying thing). The examples are chosen to show that when both premises are negative, the conclusion can be either true or false, so no necessary conclusion follows.

The Fourth Figure: Condition for Productivity and the First Mood
Author states the condition for fourth figure productivity and gives the first mood example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the fourth figure's productivity and reduce its first productive mood to the first figure via conversion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure produces only when either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or they differ in quality with one universal.

In this passage, the author tackles the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, establishing exactly when it yields a necessary conclusion. He then walks through the first productive mood—two universal affirmatives—showing how it reduces to the familiar first figure. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying the fourth figure in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternative conditions
Read the first sentence: the condition is either both premises affirmative with the minor universal, or different quality with one premise universal. Note that these are two distinct sufficient conditions.
2
Examine the three non-productive cases
The author lists three cases that must be avoided: both negatives, both affirmatives with particular minor, and both particular with differing quality. For each, he gives a counterexample to show no necessary conclusion follows.
The examples use everyday terms like 'man', 'horse', 'donkey', 'animal'.
3
Count the productive moods
From the condition, only 8 of the possible 16 moods are productive. The author explains which are eliminated: 4 from both negatives, 2 from both affirmatives with particular minor, 2 from both particular with differing quality.
4
Work through the first productive mood
The first mood is two universal affirmative premises yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The author shows how to reduce it to the first figure by reversing the premise order and then converting the universal affirmative conclusion to a particular affirmative.
The example in variables: 'Every B is J, every A is B' gives 'Every A is J', which converts to 'Some J is A'.
5
Understand why the conclusion is particular
The author notes that a universal conclusion is impossible because the minor term could be broader than the major. The example 'Every man is an animal, every rational is a man' yields only 'Some animal is rational', not 'Every animal is rational'.
This illustrates the logical point using an Islamic example (man, animal, rational).

القول: شرط إنتاج الشكل الرابع بحسب الكيفية والكمية أحد الأمرين، وهو إما إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف كلية إحداهما، وذلك لأنّه لولا أحدهما لزم أحد الأمور الثلاث : إما سلب المقدمتين، أو إيجابهما مع جزئية الصغرى، أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع جزئيتهما، وعلى التقادير يتحقق الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج.
أما إذا كانتا سالبتين فلصدق قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بفرس، ولاشيء من الحمار بإنسان والحق السلب، أو «لاشيء من الصاهل بإنسان» والحق الإيجاب.

وأما إذا كانتا مختلفتين بالكيف مع كونهما جزئيتين فلأن الموجبة إن كانت صغرى صدق قولنا : بعض الناطق إنسان وبعض الحيوان ليس بناطق، أو بعض الفرس ليس بناطق»، و الصادق في الأول الإيجاب وفي الثاني السلب وإن كانت كبرى صدق بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس وبعض الحيوان إنسان»، والحق الإيجاب أو بعض الناطق إنسان» والحق السلب.

الأول: من موجبتين كلّيتين ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا : «كل (ب ج) وكلّ (اب) فبعض (ج 1) » بعكس الترتيب ثم عكس النتيجة.
فإنا إذا عكسنا الترتيب ارتد إلى الشكل الأول هكذا : «كل (اب) وكل (ب) ج) » ينتج «كل (اج) » وهو ينعكس إلى «بعض (ج 1)» وهو المطلوب.
ولا ينتج كلياً لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر، وامتناع حمل الأخص على كل أفراد الأعم، كقولنا : كلّ إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق إنسان، مع أن الحق «بعض الحيوان ناطق».
Statement: The condition for production of the fourth figure in terms of quality and quantity is one of two things: either the affirmation of both premises with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them. This is because if neither of these holds, one of three things necessarily follows: either both premises are negative, or both are affirmative with a particular minor, or they differ in quality while both are particular. In all these cases, the difference that necessitates non-production is realized. As for when both are negative, then our statement 'Nothing of man is a horse, and nothing of the donkey is a man' is true, and the truth is negativity, or 'Nothing of the neighing one is a man' and the truth is affirmative. As for when both are affirmative and the minor is particular, then our statement 'Some animal is a man, and every rational is an animal' is true, with the truth of affirmation, or 'Every horse is an animal' with the truth of negation. As for when they differ in quality while both are particular, then if the affirmative is the minor, our statement 'Some rational is a man, and some animal is not rational' or 'Some horse is not rational' is true, and the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second negation; if the affirmative is the major, 'Some man is not a horse, and some animal is a man' is true, and the truth is affirmation, or 'Some rational is a man' and the truth is negation. Its productive variants according to this condition are eight: due to the dropping of four types because of the barrenness of the two negatives, two types because of the barrenness of the two affirmatives with a particular minor, and two others because of the barrenness of the two particular differing in quality. The first: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as in our statement 'Every B is J, and every A is B, so some J is A' by reversing the order then reversing the result. For if we reverse the order, it reduces to the first figure thus: 'Every A is B, and every B is J' produces 'Every A is J', which is converted to 'Some J is A', and that is desired. It does not produce a universal because it is possible that the minor term is more general than the major, and it is impossible to predicate the more specific to every individual of the more general, as in our statement 'Every man is an animal, and every rational is a man', while the truth is 'Some animal is rational'.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism to produce a necessary conclusion. The author first states that the condition is one of two: either both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal, or the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one of them is universal. If neither condition is met, then one of three undesirable situations occurs: both premises negative, both affirmative with particular minor, or both particular and differing in quality. In each case, no valid conclusion follows. The author then provides examples of each invalid combination using everyday terms like 'man', 'horse', 'donkey', and 'animal' to illustrate that the premises can be true while the conclusion fails. After establishing the condition, the author announces that there are eight productive moods for the fourth figure, explaining that four moods are eliminated because both premises negative yield nothing, two because both affirmative with particular minor yield nothing, and two because particular premises differing in quality yield nothing. The first productive mood is then given: two universal affirmative premises produce a particular affirmative conclusion. The proof involves converting the order of the premises to reduce to the first figure, then converting the result. The author also notes that the conclusion is only particular, not universal, because the minor term may be broader than the major, and the more specific cannot be predicated of every instance of the broader term. A concrete example is given: 'Every man is an animal, every rational is a man' — this yields 'Some animal is rational', not 'Every animal is rational'. This demonstrates the logical point using an Islamic kalam example (the concepts of man, animal, rational).

The Eight Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Enumerates the eight productive moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight productive moods of the second figure of categorical syllogism and reduce each to the first figure using conversion of order (aks al-tartib) and propositional conversion where necessary.

Core Idea
The second figure yields eight productive moods; all are reduced to the first figure by transposition and conversion.

The second figure of categorical syllogism has the middle term as predicate in both premises. Not all combinations of premise types produce valid conclusions. The author enumerates exactly eight moods that do, and shows how each reduces to the first figure by swapping premises (aks al-tartib) and sometimes converting propositions. This lesson walks through each mood, explaining the conditions for productivity and the reduction procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood composition
For each mood, note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of both premises. The author lists eight moods based on these combinations.
Use the author's condition: both premises cannot be negative, both cannot be affirmative with a particular minor, and both cannot be different in quality when both are particular.
2
Check if the mood is among the eight productive ones
Compare the composition with the list: 1) two universal affirmatives, 2) two affirmatives with particular major, 3) two universals with minor negative, 4) two universals with minor affirmative, 5) particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, 6) particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, 7) universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, 8) [missing from text].
The eighth mood is not preserved in the source; it would be the dual of the seventh or similar.
3
Perform reduction by converting the order (aks al-tartib)
Swap the two premises so that the minor premise becomes the major and vice versa. This transforms the syllogism into the first figure.
For example, moods 1-5 and 7 use direct aks al-tartib. Mood 6 requires converting the minor premise first to return to the second figure, then proceeding.
4
Apply propositional conversion if needed
After transposition, the first figure may require an additional conversion (aks) of the conclusion to obtain the final result. For instance, mood 1 produces a universal affirmative in the first figure, then converts to a particular affirmative.
Understand that conversion changes the subject and predicate while preserving truth under specific rules (e.g., universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative).
5
Verify the validity and explain why it's not universal when relevant
The author shows why certain moods yield only particular conclusions: the minor term may be more general than the major, making a universal claim false. For example, 'Every human is animal' and 'Every rational is human' does not imply 'Every animal is rational'.
This step reinforces the logical reason behind the limitation, not just procedural knowledge.
