As for when both are affirmative and the minor is particular: because it is true to say 'Some animal is human and every rational is animal' with the truth of affirmation, or 'Every horse is animal' with the truth of negation. As for when they differ in quality while both are particular: because if the affirmative is minor, it is true to say 'Some rational is human and some animal is not rational', or 'Some horse is not rational', and the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second negation; if it is major, it is true to say 'Some human is not horse and some animal is human', and the truth is affirmation; or 'Some rational is human' and the truth is negation. Its productive moods according to this condition are eight: because four moods drop due to the barrenness of both negatives, two moods for the barrenness of both affirmatives with particularity of the minor, and two others for the barrenness of the two different particulars. First: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as our saying 'Every B is C and every A is B, so some C is A' by conversion of order then conversion of the result. For if we convert the order, it returns to the first figure thus: 'Every A is B and every B is C' produces 'Every A is C' which converts to 'Some C is A', which is required. And it does not produce a universal because it is possible that the minor is more general than the major, and it is impossible to predicate the more specific of every individual of the more general, as our saying 'Every human is animal and every rational is human', while the truth is 'Some animal is rational'. Second: from two affirmatives with the major particular produces a particular affirmative, as our saying 'Every B is C and some A is B, so some C is A' by conversion of order also as before. Third: from two universals with the minor negative produces a universal negative, as our saying 'Nothing of B is C and every A is B, so nothing of C is A' by conversion of order also as before. Fourth: from two universals with the minor affirmative produces a particular negative, as our saying 'Every B is C and nothing of A is B, so some C is not A' by converting both premises to return to the first figure thus: 'Some C is B and nothing of B is A, so some C is not A', which is required. And it does not produce a universal because of the possibility of the generality of the minor, as our saying 'Every human is animal and nothing of horse is human' while the truth is 'Some animal is not horse'. Fifth: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as our saying 'Some B is C and nothing of A is B, so some C is not A' by converting both premises as before. Sixth: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major produces a particular negative, as our saying 'Some B is not C and every A is B, so some C is not A' by converting the minor to return to the second figure and it produces the mentioned result itself. Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods of the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author first sets out the conditions under which moods are productive: both premises cannot be negative, both cannot be affirmative with a particular minor, and both cannot be different in quality when both are particular. From these conditions, eight moods remain. The author then lists each mood with its example and explains how it is reduced to the first figure by conversion of order and/or conversion of propositions. The eighth mood is missing from the text. The commentary on each mood shows the logical reasoning behind the validity of each mood in the second figure.

Valid moods of the fourth figure
Eight valid moods of the fourth figure enumerated with reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the eight valid moods of the fourth figure and explain how they reduce to the first figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's moods are valid but less natural; reduction to the first figure confirms them.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism may seem less intuitive, but it has eight productive moods. In this lesson, you will learn how each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its premises and conclusion, and how they can be reduced to the first figure. Understanding these moods equips you to handle syllogisms where the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
The fourth figure has the middle term as predicate of the major premise and subject of the minor premise. In the examples, the middle term (ب) appears as predicate in the first premise and subject in the second.
The order of premises is swapped compared to the first figure.
2
Determine the mood by premise quantity and quality
Each mood is characterized by the type of the two premises (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) in the order major then minor. Locate the description of each mood in the passage.
For example, the first mood: universal affirmative major, universal affirmative minor, yields particular affirmative conclusion.
3
Verify reduction to the first figure
The author shows how to convert premises or swap order to reduce to the first figure. Follow the reduction steps provided: e.g., for the fourth mood, convert both premises to get a first-figure syllogism.
The reduction method often involves 'aks al-tartīb (reversing order) or conversion of propositions.
4
Check the conclusion type
The conclusion is always particular in the fourth figure, never universal. Note why: in the fourth mood, a universal negative is invalid because of counterexample (every human is animal, no horse is human → not every animal is horse).
The author provides a concrete example to explain the limitation.
5
Analyze the arrangement rationale
The moods are ordered not by productivity but by logical nobility: universal affirmative first, then shared characteristics (affirmative premises, easy reduction). Examine the criteria used.
This ordering helps in memorization and understanding their logical status.

الثاني : من موجبتين والكبرى جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا : كل (ب) (ج) وبعض (ا) (ب) فبعض (ج) (ا) بعكس الترتيب أيضاً كما مر. الثالث: من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ينتج سالبة كلية، كقولنا : لاشيء من (ب) (ج) وكل (ا) (ب) فلاشيء من (ج) (ا) بعكس الترتيب أيضاً كما مر. الرابع : من كليتين والصغرى موجبة، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : كل (ب) (ج) ولاشيء من (ا) (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا) بعكس المقدمتين ليرجع إلى الشكل الأوّل هكذا : «بعض (ج) (ب) ولاشيء من (ب) (ا) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» وهو المطلوب. ولا ينتج كلياً لاحتمال عموم الأصغر، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان ولاشيء من الفرس بإنسان» مع أنّ الصادق «ليس بعض الحيوان فرساً». الخامس: من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا بعض (ب) (ج) ولاشيء من (ا) (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا) بعكس المقدمتين كما مر. السادس : من سالبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : «بعض (ب) ليس (ج) وكل (ا) (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)، بعكس الصغرى ليرتد إلى الشكل الثاني وينتج النتيجة المذكورة بعينها. السابع : من موجبة كلّيّة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : كلّ (ب) (ج) وبعض (ا) ليس (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا) بعكس الكبرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الثالث وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. الثامن : من سالبة كلّيّة صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : «لاشيء من (ب) (ج) وبعض (ا) (ب) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» بعكس الترتيب، ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول ثم عكس النتيجة. وترتيب هذه الضروب ليس باعتبار إنتاجها، لأنها لبعدها عن الطبع لم يعتد بإنتاجها ؛ بل باعتبار أنفسها ؛ فلابد من تقديم الأول، لأنه من موجبتين كليتين، والإيجاب الكلّي أشرف الأربع ؛ وقدم الثاني أيضاً وإن كان الثالث والرابع من كلّيّتين - والكلّي أشرف وإن كان سلباً من الجزئي وإن كان إيجاباً - لمشاركته للأوّل في إيجاب المقدمتين وفي أحكام الاختلاط كما ستعرفه، ثم الثالث لارتداده إلى الشكل الأول بعكس الترتيب، ثم الرابع لكونه أخص من الخامس، ثم الخامس على السادس لارتداده إلى الشكل الأول بعكس المقدمتين، ثم السادس والسابع على الثامن لاشتمالهما على الإيجاب الكلّي دونه، وقدم السادس على السابع لارتداده إلى الشكل الثاني دون السابع.
The second: from two affirmatives with a particular major, it yields a particular affirmative, as we say: Every B is C, and some A is B, so some C is A, also by reversing the order as before. The third: from two universals with a negative minor, it yields a universal negative, as we say: No B is C, and every A is B, so no C is A, also by reversing the order as before. The fourth: from two universals with an affirmative minor, it yields a particular negative, as we say: Every B is C, and no A is B, so some C is not A, by converting both premises to return to the first figure, thus: 'Some C is B, and no B is A, so some C is not A' which is the desired conclusion. And it does not yield a universal because of the possibility of the minor being universal, as we say: 'Every human is an animal, and no horse is a human' whereas the truth is 'Not every animal is a horse'. The fifth: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, it yields a particular negative, as we say: Some B is C, and no A is B, so some C is not A, by converting both premises as before. The sixth: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, it yields a particular negative, as we say: 'Some B is not C, and every A is B, so some C is not A', by converting the minor to revert to the second figure and produce that same conclusion. The seventh: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, it yields a particular negative, as we say: Every B is C, and some A is not B, so some C is not A, by converting the major to revert to the third figure and produce the desired conclusion. The eighth: from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major, it yields a particular negative, as we say: 'No B is C, and some A is B, so some C is not A' by reversing the order to revert to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. And the arrangement of these moods is not according to their productivity, because due to their distance from nature they are not considered productive; rather according to themselves. So it is necessary to advance the first, because it is from two universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative is the noblest of the four; and he advanced the second also, even though the third and fourth are from two universals – and the universal is nobler even if negative than the particular even if affirmative – because it shares with the first in the affirmation of both premises and in the rules of mixture as you will learn. Then the third because it reverts to the first figure by reversing the order. Then the fourth because it is more specific than the fifth. Then the fifth over the sixth because it reverts to the first figure by converting both premises. Then the sixth and the seventh over the eighth because they contain the universal affirmative while it does not. And he advanced the sixth over the seventh because it reverts to the second figure while the seventh does not.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the eight productive moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author describes each mood in terms of quantity and quality of its premises and the conclusion, and illustrates each with an example using the letters A, B, C. After listing the moods, he explains the rationale for their arrangement. He notes that the arrangement is not based on productivity (since all produce valid conclusions) but on intrinsic logical nobility. The first mood (two universal affirmatives) is placed first as the noblest. Various criteria such as universality versus particularity, affirmative versus negative, and ease of reduction to the first figure are used to order the remaining moods. This discussion is typical of post-Avicennan logic where the fourth figure is recognized separately. The examples use variable terms (A, B, C) as placeholders; the author also provides a concrete example for the fourth mood: 'Every human is an animal, no horse is a human' leading to the correct conclusion 'Not every animal is a horse', showing why a universal negative is not valid.

Proving Fourth Figure Moods with Khalf and Iftiraḍ
Author begins explaining proof methods for the fourth figure moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the khalf and iftiraḍ methods to prove categorical syllogisms in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
Khalf and iftiraḍ are proof strategies for moods that resist direct conversion.

In the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, some moods are not directly reducible to the first figure. Classical logicians devised two methods: reductio (khalf) and supposition (iftiraḍ). This passage explains how the first five moods are proved by khalf, while the second and fifth are proved by iftiraḍ. Understanding these techniques allows you to validate any syllogism in the fourth figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proof methods
The author names two methods: al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) and al-iftiraḍ (supposition). Note which moods they apply to.
The first five moods use khalf; the second and fifth use iftiraḍ.
2
Understand khalf (reductio)
In khalf, assume the contradictory of the conclusion (naqīḍ al-natīja) and combine it with one premise. This should produce a result that, when converted, yields the contradictory of the other premise, showing the assumption is false.
This method proves validity by showing that denying the conclusion leads to a logical contradiction.
3
Understand iftiraḍ (supposition)
In iftiraḍ, posit a hypothetical individual (here called D) that instantiates the particular premise. For example, if the premise is 'some A are B', assume D is such that D is A and D is B. Then from universal premises (e.g., 'every D is A', 'every D is B') you can derive a categorical conclusion.
This method converts a syllogism with a particular premise into one with universal premises, making it analyzable in the first figure.
4
Map the methods to specific moods
From the passage: the first five moods are proved by khalf, the second and fifth by iftiraḍ. The second mood is used as an example to illustrate iftiraḍ, and the fifth is analogous.
Refer to the standard list of fourth figure moods to identify which are second and fifth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ويمكن بيان الخمسة الأول بالخلف، وهو ضم نقيض النتيجة إلى إحدى المقدمتين لينتج ما ينعكس إلى نقيض الأخرى. والثاني والخامس بالافتراض، ولنبين ذلك في الثاني ليقاس عليه الخامس: وليكن البعض الذي هو (ا) (ب) (د) فكل (د) (ا) وكل (د) (ب)، فنقول: «كل
He said: And it is possible to explain the first five by reductio (al-khalf), which is conjoining the contradictory of the conclusion with one of the premises to produce something that converts to the contradictory of the other. And the second and the fifth by supposition (al-iftiraḍ), and let us explain that in the second so that the fifth may be measured upon it: let the particular that is A B be D, so every D is A and every D is B, then we say: 'Every...'
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage introduces two methods for proving the validity of the moods of the fourth figure: reductio ad absurdum (khalf) and supposition (iftiraḍ). The first five moods are proved by khalf: assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and combining it with one premise yields the contradictory of the other premise. The second and fifth moods are proved by iftiraḍ: postulating a particular instance D that satisfies the minor premise, then constructing a syllogism. The author begins to illustrate with the second mood, but the text breaks off. In iftiraḍ, D is the individual that makes the particular premise true; from that one can derive universal statements to complete a categorical syllogism in the first figure. This method avoids the need for conversion when the premises are not directly convertible.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khalf): Reductio ad absurdum: proving a syllogism by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deducing a contradiction. [Note: Standard term in Arabic logic; no significant shift.]
الافتراض (al-iftirāḍ): Supposition: a proof method that posits a hypothetical individual (D) to instantiate a particular premise and then uses universal premises to derive the conclusion. [Note: Used in post-Avicennan logic for moods that involve particular premises; earlier logicians sometimes used conversion instead.]
نقيض النتيجة (naqīḍ al-natīja): The contradictory opposite of the conclusion of a syllogism. [Note: Standard term.]

Eighth Mood and Ordering of the Fourth Figure
Describes the eighth mood and ordering rationale of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the eighth mood of the fourth figure and justify the ordering of its moods based on nobility and reduction criteria.

Core Idea
Moods are ordered by logical nobility and reduction ease, not by productivity.

The fourth figure's eighth mood uses a universal negative minor and particular affirmative major to produce a particular negative conclusion. But why are the moods arranged from first to eighth? Al-Qutbi explains that the order is based on the nobility of premises (universal affirmative being noblest) and the ease of reduction to the first or second figure. This lesson unpacks both the mechanics of the eighth mood and the principles behind the ordering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eighth mood's components
Notice the minor premise is universal negative ('Nothing of B is C'), the major is particular affirmative ('Some A is B'), and the conclusion is particular negative ('Some C is not A').
The conclusion is derived after reduction steps.
2
Trace the reduction process
Reverse the order of the premises (swap minor and major) to convert to the first figure, then perform conversion on the conclusion to get the final result.
This two-step reduction shows the method for all fourth-figure moods.
3
Identify the ordering criteria
Note the author's two criteria: nobility (universal affirmative > universal negative > particular affirmative > particular negative) and ease of reduction (direct reduction to first figure is better).
Productivity is ignored because fourth-figure moods are considered remote from nature.
4
Apply the criteria to a sample mood
For example, the first mood (two universal affirmatives) is placed first because universal affirmative is the noblest. The third mood is placed before the fourth because it reduces directly to the first figure.
This reasoning applies to all seven moods beyond the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : من سالبة كلّيّة صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : «لاشيء من (ب) ج) وبعض (اب) فبعض (ج) ليس (۱)» بعكس الترتيب، ليرتد إلى الشكل الأول ثم عكس النتيجة. وترتيب هذه الضروب ليس باعتبار إنتاجها، لأنها لبعدها عن الطبع لم يعتد بإنتاجها ؛ بل باعتبار أنفسها ؛ فلابد من تقديم الأول، لأنه من موجبتين كليتين، والإيجاب الكلّي أشرف الأربع ؛ وقدم الثاني أيضاً وإن كان الثالث والرابع من كلّيّتين - والكلّي أشرف وإن كان سلباً من الجزئي وإن كان إيجاباً - لمشاركته للأوّل في إيجاب المقدمتين وفي أحكام الاختلاط كما ستعرفه، ثم الثالث لارتداده إلى الشكل الأول بعكس الترتيب، ثم الرابع لكونه أخص من الخامس، ثم الخامس على السادس لارتداده إلى الشكل الأول بعكس المقدمتين، ثم السادس والسابع على الثامن لاشتمالهما على الإيجاب الكلّي دونه، وقدم السادس على السابع لارتداده إلى الشكل الثاني دون السابع.
The eighth: from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major produces a particular negative, as we say: 'Nothing of (B) is (C) and some (A) is (B)' then 'some (C) is not (A)' by reversal of order to return to the first figure and then conversion of the conclusion. And the ordering of these moods is not due to their productivity, because they are remote from nature and not considered productive; rather due to themselves. So the first must be presented, because it is from two universal affirmatives, and universal affirmative is the noblest of the four; and the second is also presented even though the third and fourth are from two universal premises—and the universal is nobler even if negative than the particular even if affirmative—due to its sharing with the first in affirmative premises and in rules of mixing as you will know. Then the third because it reduces to the first figure by reversal of order. Then the fourth because it is more specific than the fifth. Then the fifth over the sixth because it reduces to the first figure by reversal of both premises. Then the sixth and seventh over the eighth because they involve the universal affirmative without it. And the sixth is offered before the seventh because it reduces to the second figure without the seventh.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains the eighth mood of the fourth figure and then discusses the rationale behind the ordering of the moods of the fourth figure. The author first states the eighth mood: a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major yield a particular negative conclusion. The method to derive this is to reverse the order of premises to convert to the first figure, then convert the conclusion. Next, the author explains that the ordering of the moods (first to eighth) is not based on productivity but on logical nobility and ease of reduction. The first mood (two universal affirmatives) is first because universal affirmative is the noblest. The second mood is placed second due to sharing affirmative premises with the first. The third reduces directly to the first figure. The fourth is more specific (particular conclusion) than the fifth. The fifth reduces by reversing both premises. The sixth and seventh are placed above the eighth because they contain a universal affirmative, and the sixth reduces to the second figure while the seventh does not.

Proving Syllogistic Validity by Khalf (Reductio)
Explains the method of reductio ad absurdum to prove the first five moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum) to prove the validity of a categorical syllogism in any mood.

Core Idea
Khalf proves an argument by showing its negation yields a contradiction with a premise.

The passage explains how to prove the validity of the first five moods of the fourth figure using khalf, or reductio ad absurdum. Instead of constructing a direct syllogism, we assume the opposite of the conclusion and combine it with one premise to derive a contradiction with the other premise. This method is essential for validating syllogisms that are not directly reducible to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its conclusion
Determine whether the mood to be proved has an affirmative or negative conclusion; this affects how you set up the khalf syllogism.
The first two moods have affirmative conclusions; the next three have negative conclusions.
2
Take the contradictory of the conclusion
Form the contradictory proposition (the negation) of the conclusion. If the conclusion is affirmative, its contradictory is a universal negative; if negative, its contradictory is a particular affirmative.
The contradictory must be exact: 'some ... not' versus 'none ...'.
3
Combine the contradictory with one original premise
For affirmative conclusions: make the contradictory (universal negative) the major premise, and the original minor (affirmative) the minor. For negative conclusions: make the contradictory (particular affirmative) the minor, and the original major (universal) the major. This forms a first-figure syllogism.
The arrangement ensures the middle term is properly positioned for the first figure.
4
Derive the new conclusion and convert if needed
The first-figure syllogism yields a conclusion; apply conversion (if needed) to obtain a proposition that directly contradicts the other original premise.
Conversion rules: 'no B is A' converts to 'no A is B'; 'some B is A' converts to 'some A is B'.
5
Observe the contradiction
The derived proposition contradicts one of the original premises (either the major or minor). This shows that the assumption (the contradictory of the original conclusion) is false, so the original conclusion must be true.
The contradiction is between the converted result and the other premise not used in the khalf syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: يمكن بيان إنتاج الضروب الخمسة الأول بالخلف، وهو أن يضم نقيض النتيجة إلى إحدى المقدمتين لينتج ما ينعكس إلى نقيض الأخرى. أما في الضربين المنتجين للإيجاب، فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لكونه كلياً - كبرى، وصغرى القياس - لإيجابها - صغرى، فينتظمان على هيأة الشكل الأول - كما مر في الخلف المستعمل في الشكل الثالث - وتحصل نتيجة تنعكس إلى ماينا في الكبرى؛ فلو لم يصدق «بعض (ج) ليس (ا)» لصدق «لاشيء من (ج) (ا)» ، فنجعلها كبرى لصغرى القياس، هو «كل (ب) ج)» لينتج «لاشيء من (ب) (ا)» وتنعكس إلى «لاشيء من (ا) (ب)» وهو يضاد كبرى الضرب الأوّل ويناقض كبرى الضرب الثاني. وأما في الضروب المنتجة للسلب : فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لإيجابه صغرى، وكبرى القياس لكلّيتها كبرى - كما عملنا في الضرب الأول من الشكل الثاني - لينتجا من الشكل الأول نتيجة تنعكس إلى ما ينافي الصغرى؛ مثلاً لولم يصدق «لاشيء من (ج) (ا)» لصدق «بعض (ج) (ا)» نجعلها صغرى لكبرى القياس، وهو «كل (ا) (ب)» لينتج «بعض (ج) (ب)» فـ «بعض (ب) (ج)» وقدكان صغرى القياس «لاشيء من (ب) (ج)» هذا خلف.
I say: The productivity of the first five moods can be demonstrated by khalf, which is to attach the contradictory of the conclusion to one of the two premises so that it produces something that converts into the contradictory of the other. As for the two moods that produce affirmative, the contradictory of the conclusion, being universal, is made the major, and the minor of the syllogism, due to its affirmation, is made the minor, so they become arranged in the form of the first figure—as was done in the khalf used in the third figure—and a result is obtained that converts into the opposite of what is in the major. So if 'some (C) is not (A)' is not true, then 'nothing of (C) is (A)' is true. So we make it the major of the minor premise of the syllogism, which is 'all (B) is (C)' so it produces 'nothing of (B) is (A)' and it converts into 'nothing of (A) is (B)' which contradicts the major of the first mood and is the contradictory of the major of the second mood. And as for the moods that produce negative: the contradictory of the conclusion, being affirmative, is made the minor, and the major of the syllogism, being universal, is made the major—as we did in the first mood of the second figure—so they produce from the first figure a result that converts into the opposite of the minor. For example, if 'nothing of (C) is (A)' is not true, then 'some (C) is (A)' is true. We make it the minor of the major premise of the syllogism, which is 'all (A) is (B)' so it produces 'some (C) is (B)' then 'some (B) is (C)' but the minor premise of the syllogism was 'nothing of (B) is (C)'. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum) to prove that the first five moods of the fourth figure are valid. The author first defines khalf: one takes the contradictory of the desired conclusion and combines it with one of the original premises to produce a conclusion that, when converted, contradicts the other premise. He then distinguishes two cases: when the mood produces an affirmative conclusion, and when it produces a negative conclusion. For affirmative conclusions, the contradictory of the conclusion (which is universal negative) becomes the major premise, and the original minor premise (which is affirmative) becomes the minor, forming a first figure syllogism. The result, after conversion, contradicts the major premise of the original syllogism. For negative conclusions, the contradictory of the conclusion (which is particular affirmative) becomes the minor, and the original major premise (which is universal) becomes the major, again producing a first figure syllogism whose result, after conversion, contradicts the original minor premise. An example is given for the negative case: if the conclusion 'nothing of (C) is (A)' is false, then its contradictory 'some (C) is (A)' is true. Combining this with the major premise 'all (A) is (B)' yields 'some (C) is (B)', which converts to 'some (B) is (C)', but the original minor was 'nothing of (B) is (C)'—a direct contradiction. This establishes validity.

Key Terms
الخلف

D) It is converted.

Key Takeaway
Khalf proves syllogistic validity by showing that assuming the opposite leads to contradiction with a premise, a method widely used in Islamic logic and legal theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the third mood of the fourth figure (a categorical syllogism with negative conclusion) and construct its khalf proof step by step, showing which premise is contradicted.
?
Using a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicating; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Prove this directly, then show how khalf would work if we wanted to prove the conclusion indirectly.

Proof by Iftirad for Fourth-Figure Moods
Demonstrates the validity of the second and fifth moods of the fourth figure using hypothetical assumption (iftirad).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by iftirad (hypothetical assumption) for the second mood of the fourth figure, using an assumed individual to handle the particular premise.

Core Idea
Iftirad transforms a particular premise into a universal by assuming an individual, enabling standard syllogistic deduction.

The second mood of the fourth figure has a particular premise, which cannot be used directly in universal syllogisms. Logicians devised the method of iftirad (hypothetical assumption) to overcome this: they assume an individual that satisfies the particular premise, then reason with universal premises. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of the second mood
The second mood has major: 'Every B is C' (universal affirmative) and minor: 'Some A is B' (particular affirmative). The conclusion is 'Some C is A'.
This is the fourth figure second mood (e.g., Bamalip in some notations).
2
Assume an individual D that satisfies the particular premise
Since the minor says 'Some A is B', there exists at least one individual, call it D, that is both A and B. So we assume 'Every D is A' and 'Every D is B'.
This assumption is hypothetical—it does not need to be a real existent; it's a logical supposition.
3
First syllogism: from major and assumed universal
From 'Every B is C' (major) and 'Every D is B' (assumed), apply first figure (Barbara) to get 'Every D is C'. Convert per accidens to 'Some C is D'.
Conversion from universal to particular is valid.
4
Second syllogism: from result and second assumed universal
From 'Some C is D' (from step 3) and 'Every D is A' (assumed), apply first figure (Darii) to get 'Some C is A'—the desired conclusion.
Darii requires a particular minor and universal major.
5
Generalize to the fifth mood
The fifth mood (major particular, minor universal) is proved analogously by swapping the roles of the particular premises.
The same iftirad method works for any mood with a particular premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يمكن بيان الضرب الثاني والخامس بالافتراض : ولنبين ذلك في الثاني ليقاس عليه الخامس: وليكن البعض الذي هو (ا) (ب) (د) فكل (د) (ا) وكل (د) (ب)، فنقول: «كل (ب) (ج) وكلّ (د) (ب) فبعض (ج) (د) » ثم نقول: «بعض (ج) (د) وكل (د) (ا) فبعض (ج) (ا)» وهو المطلوب.
And likewise the second and fifth can be demonstrated by hypothesis. Let us demonstrate that in the second so that the fifth may be analogized to it. Let the particular which is (A) (B) be (D). So every (D) is (A) and every (D) is (B). We say: 'Every (B) is (C) and every (D) is (B), so some (C) is (D).' Then we say: 'Some (C) is (D) and every (D) is (A), so some (C) is (A).' And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the validity of the second mood of the fourth figure using the method of iftirad (hypothetical assumption). The second mood consists of a universal affirmative major (all B is C) and a particular affirmative minor (some A is B) producing a particular affirmative conclusion (some C is A). The proof assumes a hypothetical individual (D) that falls under both A and B, since the minor premise states some A is B. Then we reason: every D is A and every D is B. From the major premise (every B is C) and every D is B, we get some C is D (by third figure). Then from some C is D and every D is A, we get some C is A (by first figure). This yields the conclusion. The same method applies to the fifth mood, which is analogous. This hypothetical method is an alternative to khalf and relies on introducing an individual term.

Key Terms
الافتراض (al-iftirad): A method of proof by assuming an individual instance (fard) that satisfies the existential import of a particular premise. [Note: Also called 'al-fard' in some texts; a standard tool in logic to handle particular propositions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) To prove the conclusion by contradiction.
D) To convert the fourth figure to the first.

Key Takeaway
Iftirad uses a hypothetical individual to transform a particular premise into a universal, enabling standard categorical syllogisms.

Proving Syllogistic Moods by Khalf
Author explains how to demonstrate productivity of first five moods using reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a khalf (reductio) proof for a given categorical syllogism mood and explain the steps.

Core Idea
Khalf proves a conclusion by showing its negation contradicts the premises.

In this passage, al-Qutb al-Tahtani explains how to demonstrate that certain moods of the second and third figures are productive using the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum). Instead of directly deducing the conclusion, you assume its opposite and combine it with one premise to derive a contradiction with the other premise. This lesson unpacks that procedure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion and its contradictory
Determine which proposition is the original conclusion. If it is affirmative (e.g., 'Some C is A'), its contradictory is a universal negative ('No C is A'). If negative, its contradictory is a particular affirmative ('Some C is A').
The contradictory is always the proposition that cannot be true simultaneously with the conclusion.
2
Choose the premise to pair with the contradictory
For affirmative conclusions, use the contradictory as the major premise and the original minor premise as the minor. For negative conclusions, use the contradictory as the minor premise and the original major premise as the major.
This setup ensures the resulting pair fits the first figure.
3
Arrange in the first figure
Place the chosen premises into a first-figure form (middle term subject of minor and predicate of major). The resulting syllogism will be one of the valid first-figure moods (e.g., Celarent for affirmative case).
The first figure is used because its moods are directly recognized as valid.
4
Derive the intermediate conclusion
Apply the first-figure mood to obtain an immediate conclusion. For affirmative conclusions, the intermediate conclusion is a universal negative; for negative conclusions, it is a particular affirmative.
This intermediate conclusion is the direct result of the khalf syllogism.
5
Convert and identify the contradiction
Convert the intermediate conclusion if necessary (e.g., swap subject and predicate). The converted proposition will oppose or contradict the other original premise, showing that the assumption leads to an absurdity.
The contradiction forces the original conclusion to be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: يمكن بيان إنتاج الضروب الخمسة الأول بالخلف، وهو أن يضم نقيض النتيجة إلى إحدى المقدمتين لينتج ما ينعكس إلى نقيض الأخرى.
أما في الضربين المنتجين للإيجاب، فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لكونه كلياً - كبرى، وصغرى القياس - لإيجابها - صغرى، فينتظمان على هيأة الشكل الأول - كما مر في الخلف المستعمل في الشكل الثالث - وتحصل نتيجة تنعكس إلى ماينا في الكبرى؛ فلو لم يصدق «بعض (ج 1) » لصدق «لاشيء من (ج )» ، فنجعلها كبرى لصغرى القياس، هو «كـلّ (ب) ج) » لينتج «لاشيء من (ب) () وتنعكس إلى «لاشيء من (اب) وهو يضادٌ كبرى الضرب الأوّل ويناقض كبرى الضرب الثاني.
وأما في الضروب المنتجة للسلب : فيجعل نقيض النتيجة لإيجابه صغرى، وكبرى القياس لكلّيتها كبرى - كما عملنا في الضرب الأول من الشكل الثاني - لينتجا من الشكل الأول نتيجة تنعكس إلى ما ينافي الصغرى ؛ مثلاً لولم يصدق لاشيء من (ج ) » لصدق «بعض (ج )» نجعلها صغرى لكبرى القياس، وهـو كـل (اب)» لينتج «بعض (ج ب» فـ «بعض (ب) (ج) وقدكان صغرى القياس «لاشيء من (ب ج)»
هذا خلف.
I say: It is possible to demonstrate the productivity of the first five moods by khalf, which is to join the contradictory of the conclusion to one of the two premises to produce what converts to the contradictory of the other. As for the two moods that produce affirmation, one makes the contradictory of the conclusion, because it is universal, a major premise, and the minor premise of the syllogism—because it is affirmative—a minor premise; they are arranged in the form of the first figure—as passed in the khalf used in the third figure—and a result is obtained that converts to what contradicts the major premise. For if 'Some (ج) is (ا)' were not true, 'Nothing of (ج) is (ا)' would be true, so we make it a major for the minor premise of the syllogism, which is 'Every (ب) is (ج)', to produce 'Nothing of (ب) is (ا)', and it converts to 'Nothing of (ا) is (ب)', and it opposes the major of the first mood and contradicts the major of the second mood. As for the moods that produce negation: one makes the contradictory of the conclusion, because it is affirmative, a minor premise, and the major premise of the syllogism, because of its universality, a major premise—as we did in the first mood of the second figure—so that they produce from the first figure a result that converts to what contradicts the minor premise. For example, if 'Nothing of (ج) is (ا)' were not true, 'Some (ج) is (ا)' would be true; we make it a minor for the major premise of the syllogism, which is 'Every (ا) is (ب)', to produce 'Some (ج) is (ب)', so 'Some (ب) is (ج)', but the minor premise of the syllogism was 'Nothing of (ب) is (ج)'. This is khalf.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains how to prove that certain moods of the syllogism are productive by using the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum). The author first states the general principle: you take the contradictory of the conclusion and add it to one of the premises; from this combination you derive a result that, when converted, contradicts the other premise, showing that the original conclusion must be true. Then he explains the procedure for the two moods that produce affirmative conclusions. In these moods, the contradictory of the conclusion is a universal negative (e.g., 'Nothing of (ج) is (ا)'), which is taken as the major premise; the minor premise of the original syllogism (which is affirmative) is kept as the minor. They form a first-figure syllogism (as in the khalf used in the third figure). The result is a negative proposition that converts to a universal negative contradicting the original major premise. For example, if the conclusion 'Some (ج) is (ا)' is denied, then 'Nothing of (ج) is (ا)' is assumed, combined with 'Every (ب) is (ج)' to get 'Nothing of (ب) is (ا)', which converts to 'Nothing of (ا) is (ب)', contradicting the original major premise. Next, the author explains the procedure for the moods that produce negative conclusions. In these, the contradictory of the conclusion is an affirmative particular (e.g., 'Some (ج) is (ا)'), which is taken as the minor premise; the universal major premise of the original syllogism is kept as the major. This forms a first-figure syllogism that yields a result converting to a proposition that contradicts the original minor premise. For instance, if the conclusion 'Nothing of (ج) is (ا)' is denied, then 'Some (ج) is (ا)' is assumed, combined with 'Every (ا) is (ب)' to produce 'Some (ج) is (ب)', which converts to 'Some (ب) is (ج)', but the original minor premise was 'Nothing of (ب) is (ج)'—a contradiction. The passage ends by stating 'This is khalf'.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khalf): The method of reductio ad absurdum in logic: assuming the negation of the desired conclusion and showing it leads to a contradiction with the premises. [Note: In later logical works, khalf is often used interchangeably with burhan al-khalfi, but the basic sense remains stable.]
النقيض (al-naqiḍ): The contradictory of a proposition; two propositions that cannot both be true and cannot both be false. [Note: No significant shift in meaning across the tradition.]
الضروب الخمسة الأول (al-ḍurūb al-khamsah al-ūlā): The first five moods of the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism, which are productive according to the author. [Note: Different logicians may classify moods differently; here it likely refers to the moods of the second and third figures considered before the ones that require conversion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is taken as the minor premise.
B) It is taken as the major premise because it is universal.
C) It is taken as the middle term.
D) It is ignored.

Key Takeaway
The khalf method proves a conclusion by assuming its negation and deriving a contradiction with the given premises, forcing the conclusion to be true.

Demonstrating the Second Mood by Supposition (Iftirad)
Demonstration of the second mood via iftirad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the iftirad proof for the second mood of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Iftirad transforms a particular premise into universals by supposing a common part.

In this lesson, we learn how the second mood of the categorical syllogism can be proven using the method of supposition (iftirad). The author demonstrates how introducing a hypothetical term allows us to derive universal premises and then combine them to reach the desired conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Assume a second-mood syllogism with premises 'Every B is C' and 'Every D is B' (or similar) producing 'Some C is D'? Actually the text's second mood is not fully specified; we focus on the iftirad process.
The original mood is not given explicitly; the iftirad method works for any mood.
2
Suppose a new term D
Take the part common to A and B in the original syllogism and call it D. Derive 'Every D is A' and 'Every D is B'.
D is a hypothetical term representing a subset.
3
Combine D with original premises
Use 'Every D is B' with the original minor 'Every B is C' to get 'Some C is D' (via first figure with existential import).
The text uses first figure: All B is C, All D is B yields Some C is D by conversion.
4
Final combination
Take 'Some C is D' as minor for 'Every D is A' to get 'Some C is A' via first figure.
Thus the desired conclusion is obtained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يمكن بيان الضرب الثاني والخامس بالافتراض :
أما بيانه في الثاني : فهو أن يفرض البعض الذي هو (اب) (د) ف «كل (دا) وكل (د ب » فنضم «كل (د ب) – كبرى – إلى صغرى القياس ونقول : «كل (ب ج) وكل (د ب)» ينتج من أول هذا الشكل بعض (ج) (د) » نجعلها صغرى لـ «كل (دا)» لينتج من الشكل الأول بعض (ج ۱)» وهو المطلوب.
وأما بيانه في الخامس فهو أن يفرض البعض الذي هو (ب ج) (د)، ف «كلّ (دب) وكلّ (دج) » ثم نقول : «كل (دب) ولاشيء من (اب)» ينتج من الشكل الثاني : لاشيء من (د (ا)»، نجعلها كبرى لـ«كل (د ج)» لينتج من الثالث بعض (ج) ليس (ا)»، وهو المطلوب.
واعلم أن محصل الافتراض أن يؤخذ مقدمـة مـن مـقـدمتي القياس ويحمل وصفا موضوعها ومحمولها على ذات الموضوع، فتحصل مقدمتان كليتان، وإن كانت مقدمة القياس جزئية - لاعتبار سائر أفراد ذلك البعض وتسميتها به.
فإن قلت : ربما لا يتعدّد ذات الموضوع، بل يكون منحصراً في فرد واحد، فلا تحصل كلية لاقتضاء الكل تعدد الأفراد.
فنقول : حينئذ تحصل قضيّتان شخصيّتان وقد سمعت أن الشخصيات في الانتاج بمنزلة الكليات على أن ذلك لا يكون إلا نادراً. ثم لا شك أن أحد الوصفين هو الحد الأوسط في القياس، فيكون إحدى مقدمتي الافتراض محمولها الحد الأوسط، فتنتظم هذه المقدمة الافتراضية مع المقدمة الأخرى القياسية، وينتج نتيجة إذا انضمت إلى المقدمة الأخرى الافتراضية تحصل النتيجة المطلوبة.
And similarly, the second and fifth moods can be demonstrated by supposition:
As for its demonstration in the second: it is that one supposes the part that is (اب) is (د), so 'Every (د) is (ا) and every (د) is (ب)' then we append 'Every (د) is (ب)' as major to the minor of the syllogism and say: 'Every (ب) is (ج) and every (د) is (ب)' produces from the first of this figure 'Some (ج) is (د)' — we make it minor for 'Every (د) is (ا)' to produce from the first figure 'Some (ج) is (ا)', which is the desired conclusion.
As for its demonstration in the fifth: it is that one supposes the part that is (ب ج) is (د), so 'Every (د) is (ب) and every (د) is (ج)' then we say: 'Every (د) is (ب) and Nothing of (ا) is (ب)' produces from the second figure 'Nothing of (د) is (ا)', we make it major for 'Every (د) is (ج)' to produce from the third figure 'Some (ج) is not (ا)', which is the desired conclusion.
And know that the essence of supposition is that one takes a premise from the two premises of the syllogism and applies the description of its subject and predicate to the essence of the subject, so two universal premises are obtained, even if the premise of the syllogism is particular—by considering all individuals of that part and naming them by it.
If you say: Perhaps the essence of the subject is not multiple, but is confined to a single individual, so universality is not obtained because universality requires multiplicity of individuals.
Then we say: In that case two singular propositions are obtained, and you have heard that singulars are in productivity like universals, and that happens only rarely. Then there is no doubt that one of the two descriptions is the middle term in the syllogism, so one of the two premises of the supposition has the middle term as its predicate, so this suppositional premise is combined with the other syllogistic premise, and a result is produced, and if it is joined with the other suppositional premise, the desired result is obtained.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage explains the method of iftirad (supposition) for proving the productivity of the second and fifth moods of the syllogism. The author first demonstrates the second mood: one supposes a new term (د) that encompasses a part common to (ا) and (ب). From this, two universal premises are derived: 'Every (د) is (ا)' and 'Every (د) is (ب)'. Then 'Every (د) is (ب)' is taken as a major premise and combined with the original minor 'Every (ب) is (ج)' to produce 'Some (ج) is (د)' from the first figure. This result is then taken as a minor for 'Every (د) is (ا)', yielding 'Some (ج) is (ا)', which was the desired conclusion. Next, for the fifth mood, one supposes a term (د) covering the part common to (ب) and (ج). From this we get 'Every (د) is (ب)' and 'Every (د) is (ج)'. Then 'Every (د) is (ب)' is combined with the original major 'Nothing of (ا) is (ب)' in the second figure to produce 'Nothing of (د) is (ا)'. This result becomes the major for 'Every (د) is (ج)' in the third figure, yielding 'Some (ج) is not (ا)', the desired conclusion. The author then summarizes the essence of iftirad: one takes one premise of the original syllogism and applies the descriptions of its subject and predicate to the essence of the subject, thereby obtaining two universal premises (even if the original premise was particular, by considering all individuals of that part). An objection is raised: what if the essence of the subject is only one individual? In that case, the two premises would be singular, but singular propositions function like universals in syllogistic productivity, though such cases are rare. Finally, the author notes that one of the two descriptions in the supposition is the middle term of the original syllogism, so one suppositional premise has the middle term as its predicate; when combined with the other original premise, a result is produced, and adding the other suppositional premise yields the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
الافتراض
الشخصيات
الحد الأوسط

Key Takeaway
Iftirad proves the second mood by introducing a hypothetical term that generates universal premises, then reduces the syllogism to two first-figure steps.

Demonstrating the Fifth Mood by Supposition (Iftirad)
Demonstration of the fifth mood via iftirad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the iftirad method to prove the fifth mood of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Iftirad for the fifth mood uses a suppositional term to convert a negative particular into a valid deduction.

This lesson extends the iftirad method to the fifth mood. The author shows how supposing a new term representing a part common to B and C allows us to derive a negative conclusion through a series of syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify original syllogism
Assume a fifth-mood syllogism with premises like 'Nothing of A is B' and 'Every B is C'? Actually the text's fifth mood uses: Nothing of A is B (major) and Some C is B? Not clear. We follow the iftirad: suppose D common to B and C.
The original mood is not fully given; focus on the iftirad steps.
2
Suppose a new term D
Take the part common to B and C in the original syllogism and call it D. Derive 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C'.
D is a hypothetical term representing a subset.
3
Combine D with original major
Use 'Every D is B' with the original major 'Nothing of A is B' in the second figure to get 'Nothing of D is A'.
Second figure: No A is B, All D is B implies No D is A.
4
Final combination
Take 'Nothing of D is A' as major for 'Every D is C' in the third figure to get 'Some C is not A'.
Third figure: No D is A, All D is C implies Some C is not A (by Ferison).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يمكن بيان الضرب الثاني والخامس بالافتراض :
أما بيانه في الثاني : فهو أن يفرض البعض الذي هو (اب) (د) ف «كل (دا) وكل (د ب » فنضم «كل (د ب) – كبرى – إلى صغرى القياس ونقول : «كل (ب ج) وكل (د ب)» ينتج من أول هذا الشكل بعض (ج) (د) » نجعلها صغرى لـ «كل (دا)» لينتج من الشكل الأول بعض (ج ۱)» وهو المطلوب.
وأما بيانه في الخامس فهو أن يفرض البعض الذي هو (ب ج) (د)، ف «كلّ (دب) وكلّ (دج) » ثم نقول : «كل (دب) ولاشيء من (اب)» ينتج من الشكل الثاني : لاشيء من (د (ا)»، نجعلها كبرى لـ«كل (د ج)» لينتج من الثالث بعض (ج) ليس (ا)»، وهو المطلوب.
واعلم أن محصل الافتراض أن يؤخذ مقدمـة مـن مـقـدمتي القياس ويحمل وصفا موضوعها ومحمولها على ذات الموضوع، فتحصل مقدمتان كليتان، وإن كانت مقدمة القياس جزئية - لاعتبار سائر أفراد ذلك البعض وتسميتها به.
فإن قلت : ربما لا يتعدّد ذات الموضوع، بل يكون منحصراً في فرد واحد، فلا تحصل كلية لاقتضاء الكل تعدد الأفراد.
فنقول : حينئذ تحصل قضيّتان شخصيّتان وقد سمعت أن الشخصيات في الانتاج بمنزلة الكليات على أن ذلك لا يكون إلا نادراً. ثم لا شك أن أحد الوصفين هو الحد الأوسط في القياس، فيكون إحدى مقدمتي الافتراض محمولها الحد الأوسط، فتنتظم هذه المقدمة الافتراضية مع المقدمة الأخرى القياسية، وينتج نتيجة إذا انضمت إلى المقدمة الأخرى الافتراضية تحصل النتيجة المطلوبة.
And similarly, the second and fifth moods can be demonstrated by supposition:
As for its demonstration in the second: it is that one supposes the part that is (اب) is (د), so 'Every (د) is (ا) and every (د) is (ب)' then we append 'Every (د) is (ب)' as major to the minor of the syllogism and say: 'Every (ب) is (ج) and every (د) is (ب)' produces from the first of this figure 'Some (ج) is (د)' — we make it minor for 'Every (د) is (ا)' to produce from the first figure 'Some (ج) is (ا)', which is the desired conclusion.
As for its demonstration in the fifth: it is that one supposes the part that is (ب ج) is (د), so 'Every (د) is (ب) and every (د) is (ج)' then we say: 'Every (د) is (ب) and Nothing of (ا) is (ب)' produces from the second figure 'Nothing of (د) is (ا)', we make it major for 'Every (د) is (ج)' to produce from the third figure 'Some (ج) is not (ا)', which is the desired conclusion.
And know that the essence of supposition is that one takes a premise from the two premises of the syllogism and applies the description of its subject and predicate to the essence of the subject, so two universal premises are obtained, even if the premise of the syllogism is particular—by considering all individuals of that part and naming them by it.
If you say: Perhaps the essence of the subject is not multiple, but is confined to a single individual, so universality is not obtained because universality requires multiplicity of individuals.
Then we say: In that case two singular propositions are obtained, and you have heard that singulars are in productivity like universals, and that happens only rarely. Then there is no doubt that one of the two descriptions is the middle term in the syllogism, so one of the two premises of the supposition has the middle term as its predicate, so this suppositional premise is combined with the other syllogistic premise, and a result is produced, and if it is joined with the other suppositional premise, the desired result is obtained.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage explains the method of iftirad (supposition) for proving the productivity of the second and fifth moods of the syllogism. The author first demonstrates the second mood: one supposes a new term (د) that encompasses a part common to (ا) and (ب). From this, two universal premises are derived: 'Every (د) is (ا)' and 'Every (د) is (ب)'. Then 'Every (د) is (ب)' is taken as a major premise and combined with the original minor 'Every (ب) is (ج)' to produce 'Some (ج) is (د)' from the first figure. This result is then taken as a minor for 'Every (د) is (ا)', yielding 'Some (ج) is (ا)', which was the desired conclusion. Next, for the fifth mood, one supposes a term (د) covering the part common to (ب) and (ج). From this we get 'Every (د) is (ب)' and 'Every (د) is (ج)'. Then 'Every (د) is (ب)' is combined with the original major 'Nothing of (ا) is (ب)' in the second figure to produce 'Nothing of (د) is (ا)'. This result becomes the major for 'Every (د) is (ج)' in the third figure, yielding 'Some (ج) is not (ا)', the desired conclusion. The author then summarizes the essence of iftirad: one takes one premise of the original syllogism and applies the descriptions of its subject and predicate to the essence of the subject, thereby obtaining two universal premises (even if the original premise was particular, by considering all individuals of that part). An objection is raised: what if the essence of the subject is only one individual? In that case, the two premises would be singular, but singular propositions function like universals in syllogistic productivity, though such cases are rare. Finally, the author notes that one of the two descriptions in the supposition is the middle term of the original syllogism, so one suppositional premise has the middle term as its predicate; when combined with the other original premise, a result is produced, and adding the other suppositional premise yields the desired conclusion.

Iftirad proves the fifth mood by introducing a hypothetical term that allows a second-figure negative conclusion, which then combines with a third-figure syllogism to yield the desired negative particular.

The Essence of Iftirad: Universal Premises from Particulars
Explanation of the essence of iftirad and handling of singulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical basis of iftirad and handle the objection regarding singular propositions.

Core Idea
Iftirad derives universal premises by considering all individuals of a part, even if the original premise is particular.

After demonstrating the method, the author explains the essence of iftirad: it takes one premise of the syllogism and applies the descriptions of its subject and predicate to the essence of the subject, obtaining two universal premises even if the original premise was particular. An objection about singular terms is raised and resolved by noting that singular propositions function like universals in syllogistic productivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal of iftirad
Iftirad aims to generate universal premises from a particular premise by considering all individuals of a part of the subject.
The method works by taking a premise and applying its descriptions to the essence of the subject.
2
Understand the objection
If the subject's essence is confined to a single individual, then the resulting propositions are singular, not universal.
Singular propositions have only one subject.
3
Resolve the objection
The author states that singular propositions are treated as universals in syllogistic productivity, and such cases are rare.
This is a specific doctrine in some logical schools.
4
Recognize the middle term
One of the two descriptions in the supposition is the middle term of the original syllogism, linking the suppositional premises to the original ones.
This ensures the final syllogism is valid.

Critique of Supposition's Figure Requirements
Author criticizes the claim that supposition always requires a first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of the standard view on supposition and provide counterexamples from the second, third, and fifth moods.

Core Idea
Supposition does not always require a first-figure syllogism; the figures can vary by mood.

The method of supposition (iftirad) is used to prove syllogistic moods. Many logicians assumed that one of the two syllogisms in a supposition must always be in the first figure. In this passage, the author challenges that assumption with concrete counterexamples. Let's walk through his critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the standard view
The standard view claims that in supposition, one syllogism must be in the first figure, and the other in the figure whose mood is being demonstrated. Restate this view in your own words.
2
Locate the first counterexample
The author says that in the fifth mood (of the figure under discussion), one syllogism is from the second figure and the other from the third. Which figures are used here? Note that neither is the first figure.
The exact figure of the fifth mood is not specified; the key is that the two syllogisms are from the second and third figures.
3
Examine the second counterexample
The author argues that the supposition for the second mood can be formulated with the first syllogism in the first figure and the second in the third, which he claims is clearer. Why might this be more apparent?
This shows flexibility: the standard view would require the second syllogism to be in the second figure, but the author shows an alternative.
4
Critique of universal vs. particular premises
The author points out an inconsistency: in conversions they use supposition for universals, but in syllogisms they use it only for particulars. He says supposition in the second and third figures is not complete with a universal premise because one syllogism lacks conditions or is wrongly arranged. Identify why that might be.
5
Assess the fourth figure exception
The author admits that supposition in the fourth figure does work with universal premises, as in the major of the first mood and minor of the fourth mood. How does this exception fit into his overall critique?
The critique is not absolute; the author acknowledges where the standard view holds.

ففي الافتراض قياسان ، وزعم القوم أن أحدهما لابد أن يكون على نظم الشكل الأول، والآخر على نظم ذلك الشكل المطلوب إنتاجه، وهو ليس بصحيح على الإطلاق، لأن الافتراض في خامس هذا الشكل ليس كذلك، بل أحد القياسين فيه من الشكل الثاني والآخر من الشكل الثالث، والافتراض في ثانيه أيضاً لا يجب أن يقرر كما قرروه، فإنه يمكن أن يبين بحيث يكون القياس الأوّل من الشكل الأول والثاني من الثالث على أن الاستنتاج من الأول والثالث أظهر وأبين من الاستنتاج من الرابع والأول.
ثم إنك تراهم يفترضون في باب العكوس في الكليات ولا يفترضون في باب الأقيسة إلا في الجزئيات، وهو أيضاً ليس بمستقيم مطلقاً، بل الافتراض في الشكل الثاني والثالث لايتم في المقدمة الكلية، لأن أحد قياسيه أمر غير مشتمل على شرائط الإنتاج أو مرتب على هيأة الضرب المطلوب إنتاجه.

So in supposition there are two syllogisms, and the folk claim that one of them must be in the arrangement of the first figure, and the other in the arrangement of that figure whose production is sought, but this is not correct absolutely, because the supposition in the fifth of this figure is not like that; rather one of the two syllogisms in it is from the second figure and the other from the third figure. And the supposition in its second also is not necessarily to be formulated as they formulated it, for it can be demonstrated such that the first syllogism is from the first figure and the second from the third, while the deduction from the first and third is more apparent and clear than the deduction from the fourth and first. Then you see them applying supposition in the chapter of conversions in universals and not applying supposition in the chapter of syllogisms except in particulars, and this too is not valid absolutely; rather supposition in the second and third figures is not complete in the universal premise, because one of its two syllogisms is something that does not include the conditions of production or is arranged on the form of the mood whose production is sought. And as for supposition in the fourth figure, it is complete in the universal premise, as in the major of the first mood and the minor of the fourth mood. And you must consider and test with what we have given you of the universal law.

Commentary
This passage critiques the commonly held view regarding the structure of the two syllogisms used in the method of supposition. Some authors claimed that one of the two syllogisms must always be in the first figure, and the other in the figure whose mood is being demonstrated. The commentator argues this is not always true: for example, in the fifth mood of this figure, one syllogism is in the second figure and the other in the third. Similarly, the supposition for the second mood can be formulated differently, with the first syllogism in the first figure and the second in the third, which is clearer. He further criticizes the practice of only applying supposition to particular premises in syllogisms, while applying it to universal premises in the chapter on conversions. He asserts that supposition in the second and third figures cannot be completed with a universal premise because one of the syllogisms lacks the conditions for production or is arranged in a non-standard form. However, in the fourth figure, supposition works perfectly with universal premises, as seen in the major of the first mood and the minor of the fourth mood. The passage ends with an exhortation to test this using the universal law provided.

Resolving the Dispute on Fourth Figure Moods: The Special Premise Condition
Author resolves dispute on fourth figure moods by adding a condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the 'special premise condition' to determine whether a fourth figure mood is productive, and explain how this condition resolves the classical dispute.

Core Idea
A simple condition on the negative premise resolves a centuries-old dispute on fourth figure moods.

In this short passage, the author Qutbi engages with a classical disagreement among logicians about which moods of the fourth figure yield valid conclusions. Earlier scholars limited productive moods to five, citing a problem with the remaining three when both premises are 'simple' (two affirmative or two negative). Qutbi introduces a condition: the negative premise must be one of the 'two special premises.' With this clarification, the disputed moods become productive, and the disagreement dissolves. This lesson unpacks how a precise condition can resolve ambiguity in syllogistic theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classical dispute
Earlier scholars (al-mutaqaddimun) held that only the first five moods of the fourth figure are productive. They argued that the last three moods are non-productive because of a disagreement about syllogisms composed of two 'simple' premises (likely two universal affirmatives or two universal negatives).
The 'last three' moods are typically those with two universal premises or two particular premises in the fourth figure.
2
Understand the author's condition
Qutbi stipulates that the negative premise in the disputed moods must be 'one of the two special premises' (إحدى الخاصتين). In the context of the fourth figure, these special premises are often understood as the particular negative (O) or the universal negative (E) when they occupy a specific role.
The exact identification of 'special premises' varies among logicians, but the key is that Qutbi imposes a restriction on which premise can be negative.
3
See how the condition resolves the dispute
By requiring the negative premise to be one of the special premises, the earlier objection loses its force. The syllogisms with two simple premises become productive when this condition is met, thus expanding the set of valid moods in the fourth figure.
This resolution is typical in logical commentaries: a precise condition eliminates ambiguity and reconciles conflicting views.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Consider a fourth figure syllogism with premises: 'All A is B' and 'No B is C.' The second premise is a universal negative (E). According to Qutbi, if this negative premise is one of the special premises, then the mood is productive, yielding 'Some A is not C' or 'No A is C' depending on other factors.
Check which premises are 'special' in your logical tradition to confirm productivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۹ - الاختلاف في الضروب الناتجة من الشكل الرابع
قال: والمتقدمون حصروا الضروب الناتجة في الخمسة الأول، وذكروا لعدم إنتاج الثلاثة الأخيرة الاختلاف في القياس من بسيطتين، ونحن نشترط كون السالبة فيها من إحدى الخاصتين فيسقط ماذكروه من الاختلاف.
99 - The difference in the productive moods of the fourth figure. He said: The earlier scholars limited the productive moods to the first five, and mentioned for the non-productivity of the last three a difference regarding the syllogism consisting of two simple [premises]. And we stipulate that the negative [premise] in them be from one of the two special [premises], so what they mentioned of difference falls away.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This short section addresses a disagreement about which moods of the fourth figure are productive. The earlier scholars limited the productive moods to the first five, and they cited a disagreement regarding the non-productivity of the last three moods, which involve syllogisms composed of two simple premises (probably two universal affirmatives or two negatives?). The author here stipulates a condition: the negative premise in these moods must be one of the two special premises (likely meaning it must be a particular negative or something specific). By imposing this condition, he eliminates the basis of the disagreement, implying that with this condition, those last three moods become productive. This is a typical move in logical commentaries where the author resolves a classical dispute by adding a clarification or condition.

Key Takeaway

Critique of Supposition in Syllogisms
Author criticizes logicians' claim about two suppositional syllogisms in first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the flaws in the logicians' method of supposition (iftiraḍ) in categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Supposition does not always require the first figure; the author shows it can involve the second, third, and fourth figures.

In this passage, our author takes aim at a cornerstone of Aristotelian syllogistic: the method of supposition (iftiraḍ) used to prove the validity of syllogistic moods. The logicians claimed that supposition always yields two syllogisms, one in the first figure and one in the desired figure. Our author demonstrates that this is not universally true, using examples from the first, second, third, and fourth figures. This critique not only refines the method but also reveals deeper issues about the universality of logical rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The logicians claim that in supposition, one of the two syllogisms must be in the first figure and the other in the figure whose conclusion is desired.
2
Find the counterexample from the fifth mood of the first figure
The author points out that in the fifth mood of the first figure, the two suppositional syllogisms are from the second and third figures, not the first and desired.
3
Analyze the author's alternative for the second mood
He shows that for the second mood, the supposition can be arranged with the first syllogism from the first figure and the second from the third, making the inference more evident.
4
Examine the critique of inconsistency in supposition usage
The author notes that logicians use supposition for universal propositions in converses but only for particulars in syllogisms, which is not sound.
5
Evaluate the special case of the fourth figure
Supposition does succeed with a universal premise in the fourth figure, as in specific moods.

In supposition, there are two syllogisms. The people claim that one of them must be in the arrangement of the first figure, and the other in the arrangement of that figure whose conclusion is desired. But this is not absolutely correct, because the supposition in the fifth [mood] of this figure is not like that; rather, one of its two syllogisms is from the second figure and the other from the third figure. Also, the supposition in its second [mood] need not be determined as they determined it, for it can be demonstrated such that the first syllogism is from the first figure and the second from the third, and that the inference from the first and third is more evident and clearer than the inference from the fourth and first.
Moreover, you see them using supposition in the chapter of converses for universal propositions, but they do not use supposition in the chapter of syllogisms except for particulars. This also is not absolutely sound. Rather, supposition in the second and third figures does not succeed with a universal premise, because one of its two syllogisms either does not include the conditions for productivity or is arranged in the form of the mood whose conclusion is desired.
As for supposition in the fourth figure, it does succeed with a universal premise, as in the major premise of the first mood and the minor premise of the fourth mood. And upon you is to consider and test [this] by what we have given you of the universal principle.

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' method of supposition (iftaḍ) in syllogisms. The author begins by noting that the logicians claim that in supposition there are two syllogisms, one of which must be in the form of the first figure and the other in the form of the figure whose conclusion is desired. The author refutes this claim by pointing to the fifth mood of the first figure, where the two suppositional syllogisms are actually from the second and third figures. Then he addresses the second mood of the first figure, arguing that the suppositional arrangement is not fixed as they claim; one can demonstrate it with the first syllogism from the first figure and the second from the third, and that the inference from the first and third is more evident than from the fourth and first. Next, the author criticizes the inconsistency of the logicians: they use supposition for universal propositions in the chapter of converses, but only for particulars in the chapter of syllogisms. He argues that this is not universally sound, because in the second and third figures, supposition does not succeed with a universal premise due to the lack of conditions for productivity. Finally, he notes that supposition in the fourth figure does succeed with a universal premise, as in the major of the first mood and the minor of the fourth mood, and concludes by inviting the reader to test this using the universal principle provided. The author uses examples from the figures of categorical syllogisms, which are standard in Islamic logic.

The Fourth Figure's Productive Moods: Refuting the Ancients' Limit
Author refutes ancients' claim that only five moods of fourth figure are productive.

Learning Objective
Explain why the three last moods of the fourth figure are productive when the negative premise is from the two specials, and refute the objection that they produce a difference.

Core Idea
The three last moods of the fourth figure are not sterile if we restrict the negative premise.

The ancients held that only the first five moods of the fourth figure produce a valid conclusion, and that the last three are sterile because they yield both an affirmative and a negative conclusion. The author challenges this by stipulating a condition: the negative premise must be from 'the two specials' (al-khāṣṣatayn). This lesson unpacks the dispute, the examples of difference, and how the condition resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ancients' claim
Find the exact claim in the Arabic: that only the first five moods are productive, and the last three are sterile due to 'difference' (al-ikhtilāf).
Difference means the same premises can yield both an affirmative and a negative conclusion, indicating invalidity.
2
Examine the examples of difference
Look at the examples for the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods. For each, the author shows how both an affirmative and a negative conclusion can be derived, which seems to prove the ancients' point.
The examples use terms like 'animal', 'human', 'horse', 'rational being'. In the sixth mood: 'Not every animal is a human' and 'every horse is an animal' can produce both conclusions.
3
Understand the author's condition
The author states: 'we stipulate that the negative in them be from one of the two specials.' This means the negative premise must be a particular negative that is convertible (yielding a valid syllogism).
The two specials (al-khāṣṣatayn) are particular negative premises that, when converted, produce a valid result in the fourth figure.
4
See how the condition resolves the difference
The author argues that the demonstration of difference only holds if the syllogism is composed of simple premises. By restricting the negative to the two specials, the difference no longer arises, and the moods become productive.
The footnote from Sharh al-Mutali' explains that a particular negative premise, when converted, produces with the universal affirmative whether it is minor or major.
5
Verify with a concrete example
Test the condition with an Islamic science example. For instance: 'Not every being is a jinn, and every angel is a being.' Under the condition, this yields a valid conclusion without difference.
Replace abstract terms with those from Islamic theology to see the condition in action.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال: والمتقدمون حصروا الضروب الناتجة في الخمسة الأول، وذكروا لعدم إنتاج الثلاثة الأخيرة الاختلاف في القياس من بسيطتين، ونحن نشترط كون السالبة فيها من إحدى الخاصتين فيسقط ماذكروه من الاختلاف.

القول: المتقدمون كانوا يحصرون الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل في الخمس الأول، وكان عندهم أنّ الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة عقيمة لتحقق الاختلاف فيها.
أما في الضرب السادس فلصدق قولنا : «ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان وكلّ فرس حيوان» والحق السلب، أو كل ناطق حيوان» والحق الإيجاب.
