أما إذا كانت صغرى ارتد القياس بعكسها إلى رابع الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت كبرى يرتد بعكسها إلى سادس الشكل الثالث، وينتجان المطلوب
99 - The difference in the productive moods of the fourth figure. He said: The ancients confined the productive moods to the first five, and they mentioned, for the non-productivity of the last three, the difference in the syllogism composed of two simple [premises]. But we stipulate that the negative [premise] in them be from one of the two special [premises], so what they mentioned of the difference collapses. The commentary: The ancients used to confine the productive moods in this figure to the first five, and they held that the last three moods are sterile because of the realization of difference in them. As for the sixth mood, it is because our statement 'Not every animal is a human and every horse is an animal' is true, and the conclusion is negative? Or 'every rational [being] is an animal' and the conclusion is affirmative. As for the seventh, it is because our statement 'Every human is rational and some horse is not human' is true, and the conclusion is negative? Or 'some animal is not human' and the conclusion is affirmative. As for the eighth, like our statement 'No human is a horse and some rational being is a human' or 'some animal is a human'. And the author alluded to his answer: that the demonstration of difference in these moods is only established if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but we stipulate for their productivity that the negative used in them be from one of the two special [premises], so those objections do not apply. (1) He said in Sharh al-Mutali': Know that the particular negative does not produce with the universal affirmative in this figure where it is not converted, but if it is converted – as in the two specials – it produces with it, whether it is minor or major. If it is minor, the syllogism returns by its conversion to the fourth of the second figure, and if it is major, it returns by its conversion to the sixth of the third figure, and both produce the desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute in Islamic logic regarding the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. The ancients held that only the first five moods are productive, and that the three last moods are sterile because they lead to a difference in the conclusion – i.e., they can produce both an affirmative and a negative conclusion, which is a flaw. The author responds by setting a condition: the negative premise in these moods must be from 'the two specials' (al-khaṣṣatayn). By this condition, the difference mentioned by the ancients is eliminated, and the moods become productive. The commentary provides concrete examples for each of the three last moods. For the sixth mood, they give: 'Not every animal is a human and every horse is an animal' – this shows the claim of difference. For the seventh: 'Every human is rational and some horse is not human'. For the eighth: 'No human is a horse and some rational being is a human'. The author's answer is that the demonstration of difference only holds if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but with the condition, the objections do not apply. The footnote from Sharh al-Mutali' explains that a particular negative premise in the fourth figure does not produce with a universal affirmative unless it is converted – and when converted as in the two specials, it does produce. The examples are drawn from the logical tradition, using terms like 'animal', 'human', 'horse', 'rational being'.

The three last moods of the fourth figure are not sterile; they are productive under the condition that the negative premise is from the two specials (al-khāṣṣatayn).

Productive moods in the second figure: the role of special negative conversion
Explains why the 6th, 7th, and 8th moods of the second figure are productive under the special negative condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which the 6th, 7th, and 8th moods of the second figure become productive, and apply the conversion of the particular negative to test their validity.

Core Idea
The particular negative's conversion unlocks productive moods in the second figure.

Early logicians thought only five moods of the second figure were productive. However, later logicians discovered that the 6th, 7th, and 8th moods can be productive if the negative premise is a 'special' particular negative that can be converted. This lesson explains the condition and the reduction process, showing how a simple adjustment turns seemingly invalid syllogisms into valid ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three problematic moods
Note the 6th, 7th, and 8th moods of the second figure. They involve combinations like particular negative + universal affirmative, universal affirmative + particular negative, and universal negative + particular affirmative. The early logicians thought them unproductive.
These are the moods that early logicians excluded.
2
Understand why they were considered unproductive
The early logicians argued that because the premises differ in quality (one negative, one affirmative), the conclusion's quality does not match the required truth. For example, from 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every horse is animal', the expected conclusion 'No horse is human' does not follow.
The 'difference' refers to the mismatch between the conclusion and what is logically required.
3
Learn the special condition for productivity
The author states that if the negative premise is one of the 'خاصتان' (two special types) — specifically a particular negative that can be converted by contraposition — then the syllogism becomes productive. This conversion allows reduction to valid moods.
The conversion is not simple conversion; it is a special operation (likely عكس النقيض).
4
Follow the reduction process
If the negative is minor, convert it and the syllogism reduces to the 4th mood of the second figure. If the negative is major, it reduces to the 6th mood of the third figure. Both are known to be productive. The 8th mood, after swapping premises and converting, yields a productive first‑figure syllogism.
Al-Abhari discovered this conversion, which earlier logicians missed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول: المتقدمون كانوا يحصرون الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل في الخمس الأول، وكان عندهم أنّ الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة عقيمة لتحقق الاختلاف فيها.
أما في الضرب السادس فلصدق قولنا : «ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان وكلّ فرس حيوان» والحق السلب، أو كل ناطق حيوان» والحق الإيجاب.
وأما في السابع فلانه يصدق قولنا : «كل إنسان ناطق وبعض الفرس ليس بإنسان» والحق السلب، أو بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان» والحق الإيجاب.
وأما في الثامن فكقولنا : لاشيء من الإنسان بفرس وبعض الناطق إنسان أو بعض الحيوان إنسان».
وأشار المصنف إلى جوابه بأن بيان الاختلاف في هذه الضروب إنما يتم إذا كان القياس مركباً من المقدمات البسيطة، لكنا نشترط في إنتاجها أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيها من إحدى الخاصتين فلا تنتهض تلك النقوض عليها.
(۱) قال في شرح المطالع : اعلم أن السالبة الجزئية إنما لاتنتج مع الموجبة الكلية في هذا الشكل حيث لم تنعكس، أما إذا انعكست - كما في الخاصتين - أنتجت معها، سواء كانت صغرى أو كبرى.
أما إذا كانت صغرى ارتد القياس بعكسها إلى رابع الشكل الثاني، وإن كانت كبرى يرتد بعكسها إلى سادس الشكل الثالث، وينتجان المطلوب.
واعلم أنّ إنتاجها بناء على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية الخاصة كنفسها، لأن السادس والسابع إنما يرتدّان إلى الثاني والثالث بعكسها، والثامن إنما ينتج لوكان بحيث إذا بدل مقدمتاه يحصل من الشكل الأوّل سالبة خاصة تنعكس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة، ولم يظهر للمتقدمين انعكاسها، و اتفق لبعض الأفاضل من المتأخرين) أن وقف عليه فبين ذلك.
بعينه، وإن الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية إنما لم ينتج إذا لم يكن إحدى الخاصتين، وأما إذا كانت أنتجت لأنا إذا بدلناهما ارتد إلى الشكل الأول وأنتج سالبة جزئية خاصة، وهي تنعكس إلى المطلوب.
(۱) هو أثير الدين المفضل الأبهري كما صرح به الخواجة نصير الدين - قده - في منطق التجريد، راجع الجوهر النضيد : ١٥٦.
The statement: The earlier [logicians] used to restrict the productive moods in this figure to the first five, and according to them the last three moods were unproductive due to the realization of difference [of quality] in them. As for the sixth mood, because our statement 'Some animal is not human and every horse is animal' is true while the truth is the negative [conclusion], or 'every rational is animal' while the truth is the affirmative [conclusion]. As for the seventh, because our statement 'Every human is rational and some horse is not human' is true while the truth is the negative, or 'some animal is not human' while the truth is the affirmative. As for the eighth, like our statement 'No human is a horse and some rational is human' or 'some animal is human'. And the author [of the original text] pointed to his answer: that the demonstration of difference in these moods is only complete if the syllogism is composed of simple premises; however we stipulate in their production that the negative used in them be one of the two special [types]; thus those objections are not effective against them. (1) He said in Sharh al-Matali': Know that the particular negative only does not produce [a conclusion] with the universal affirmative in this figure when it is not converted; but when it is converted—as in the two special [types]—it produces with it, whether it is minor or major. If it is minor, the syllogism is reduced by its conversion to the fourth of the second figure; if it is major, it is reduced by its conversion to the sixth of the third figure, and both produce the desired result. And know that its production is based on the conversion of the particular negative being like itself, because the sixth and seventh are only reduced to the second and third by its conversion; and the eighth only produces if its two premises are swapped to produce from the first figure a particular negative that converts to the desired conclusion; and its conversion was not apparent to the earlier [logicians], but it happened that one of the later eminent scholars [Athiraddin al-Abhari] came upon it and explained it. Precisely, and that the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative only does not produce if it is not one of the two special [types]; but if it is, it produces because when we swap them it reduces to the first figure and produces a particular negative which converts to the desired [conclusion]. (1) He is Athiraddin al-Mufaddal al-Abhari as stated by Khwaja Nasir al-Din (may he be sanctified) in Mantiq al-Tajrid; refer to al-Jawhar al-Nadid: 156.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the solar epistle (al-Risala al-Shamsiyya) concerning the productive moods of the second figure. The author first reports the position of the early logicians: they limited the productive moods in the second figure to five, considering the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods unproductive because the conclusion's quality differs from what is required (i.e., the 'difference' in truth-value between the premises and the desired conclusion). He then illustrates each problematic mood with an example. For the sixth mood: the premises 'Some animal is not human' (particular negative) and 'Every horse is animal' (universal affirmative) should yield 'No horse is human' but actually yield 'Some horse is not human' (which is false if all horses are not human? Actually the example shows the problem) — the text says 'the truth is the negative' meaning the true conclusion should be negative, but the premises give an affirmative. Similarly for the seventh: universal affirmative and particular negative. For the eighth: universal negative and particular affirmative. The early logicians thought these are invalid. Then the author (al-Mutawalli? Actually 'المصنف' likely refers to the author of the original matn being commented on, perhaps Qutb al-Din al-Razi? The commentary is by someone else) replies that the objection only holds if the syllogism uses simple premises (i.e., without conversion), but we require that the negative premise be one of the 'خاصتان' (the two special types) — specifically, the particular negative that can be converted (i.e., the particular negative that is true and its converse is also true under certain conditions). By this condition, the syllogisms become productive. The commentary then explains the mechanics: the particular negative, when converted (i.e., obverted or contraposed? Actually 'انعكاس' here means conversion by contraposition or simple conversion? In logic, the particular negative (e.g., 'Some A is not B') does not convert simply (to 'Some B is not A') because that would be invalid; but the 'خاصتان' likely refer to a special type of conversion called 'عكس النقيض' (conversion by contraposition) where the premise is transformed. The text says that if the negative is one of the two special types, it can be converted and then the syllogism reduces to a valid form. A footnote from Sharh al-Matali' clarifies: the particular negative does not produce with the universal affirmative unless it is converted; when converted, it reduces to either the fourth mood of the second figure or the sixth mood of the third figure, both of which are productive. The commentary concludes by noting that this discovery was made by a later scholar, Athiraddin al-Abhari, who found the conversion rule that the earlier logicians missed.

انعكاس السالبة الجزئية

Conversion of the Particular Negative and Mood Productivity
Analysis of conversion-driven productivity in second/third figure moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how conversion of the particular negative enables the reduction of certain syllogistic moods to the first figure and identify which moods depend on this operation.

Core Idea
The particular negative converts to itself, unlocking otherwise unproductive moods.

This passage reveals a subtle but crucial logical operation: the conversion of the particular negative (al-sāliba al-juz'iyya al-khāṣṣa). Unlike the universal negative (E) which converts simply, or the particular affirmative (I) which converts per accidens, the particular negative (O) converts to itself – swapping subject and predicate yields an equivalent proposition, e.g., 'Some S are not P' becomes 'Some P are not S'. This unique property allowed later logicians to reduce certain moods of the second and third figures – notably the sixth, seventh, and eighth – to the first figure, thereby demonstrating their validity. In this lesson, you will trace how this conversion works and why it was historically significant.

Guided Path
1
Recall conversion rules for the four categorical propositions
Review the standard conversions: A (universal affirmative) converts per accidens (Some P are S), E (universal negative) converts simply (No P is S), I (particular affirmative) converts simply (Some P are S), and O (particular negative) does not convert in Aristotelian logic. However, in the Islamic logical tradition, the particular negative is held to convert to itself – i.e., 'Some S are not P' is equivalent to 'Some P are not S'.
This is a distinctive feature of Islamic logic; it differs from the Aristotelian position that O does not convert.
2
Identify the sixth and seventh moods
From the standard list of moods in the second and third figures, the sixth and seventh moods (e.g., in the second figure: Camestres and Festino) reduce to the first figure through conversion. The author states that their reduction depends on the conversion of the particular negative.
If you are unfamiliar with the mood names, focus on the logical structure: these moods contain a particular negative premise.
3
Analyze the eighth mood condition
The eighth mood (likely a third-figure mood) produces a conclusion only if, after swapping its premises, the resulting first-figure syllogism yields a particular negative conclusion. This particular negative then converts to the desired conclusion. Thus, the conversion is applied twice: first to reduce to first figure, then to obtain the final proposition.
The phrase 'إذا بدل مقدمتاه' indicates reversing the order of premises, not converting them immediately.
4
Work through a concrete example
Take a specific second-figure mood: 'All A are B; some C are not B; therefore some C are not A' (Festino). Convert the minor premise 'some C are not B' to 'some B are not C'. Now the syllogism becomes: All A are B; some B are not C; therefore some C are not A? Wait, that's not a first figure. Actually, Festino reduces via conversion of the major? The passage specifically deals with particular negative conversion. Better to follow the text: the sixth and seventh moods reduce by converting the particular negative. For the eighth mood, after swapping premises, the first figure yields a particular negative which is then converted. Let's illustrate: Suppose the eighth mood is a third-figure mood with premises: 'No A is B' and 'Some C is B'? Not sure. Instead, we can show a generic example: 'Some animals are not rational; every human is rational; therefore some humans are not animals?' That doesn't work. Let's skip this step and rely on the text's explanation.
The exact identity of the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods is not essential; the key is the role of conversion.
5
Connect to the historical discovery
The author notes that earlier logicians were unaware of this conversion, until a later scholar clarified it. This underscores that logical truths can be discovered progressively, and the later figure is an expansion of the earlier tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس واعلم أنّ إنتاجها بناء على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية الخاصة كنفسها، لأن السادس والسابع إنما يرتدّان إلى الثاني والثالث بعكسها، والثامن إنما ينتج لوكان بحيث إذا بدل مقدمتاه يحصل من الشكل الأوّل سالبة خاصة تنعكس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة، ولم يظهر للمتقدمين انعكاسها، و اتفق لبعض الأفاضل من المتأخرين) أن وقف عليه فبين ذلك. بعينه، وإن الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية إنما لم ينتج إذا لم يكن إحدى الخاصتين، وأما إذا كانت أنتجت لأنا إذا بدلناهما ارتد إلى الشكل الأول وأنتج سالبة جزئية خاصة، وهي تنعكس إلى المطلوب.
The syllogism. Know that its productivity is based on the conversion of the particular negative proposition, like itself, because the sixth and seventh [moods] are reduced to the second and third [figures] through its conversion, and the eighth [mood] produces only if, when its premises are reversed, a first figure syllogism yields a particular negative which converts to the desired conclusion. The early scholars did not discover its conversion, and it happened that some later scholars came upon it and clarified it. Exactly, and the combination of a universal negative minor with a particular affirmative major does not produce if it is not one of the particular ones; but if it does produce, then because when we reverse them, it reduces to the first figure and produces a particular negative, which converts to the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage discusses the productivity of certain moods in the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism. The author explains that this productivity depends on the conversion (عكس) of the particular negative proposition (السالبة الجزئية الخاصة). He notes that the particular negative converts to itself, meaning that swapping its subject and predicate yields an equivalent proposition. The sixth and seventh moods (from the standard list of moods in the second and third figures) are reduced to the second and third figures through this conversion. The eighth mood produces a conclusion only if, after reversing its two premises, we obtain a first-figure syllogism that yields a particular negative conclusion. This conclusion is then converted to give the desired conclusion. The author informs us that earlier logicians were unaware of this conversion, but a later scholar discovered it and clarified it. He then addresses the combination of a universal negative minor premise and a particular affirmative major premise. This combination does not produce a conclusion unless it falls under a certain condition—namely, it must involve a particular negative proposition. When it does produce, the premises are reversed, reducing to the first figure and giving a particular negative, which converts to the required conclusion. The key point is the central role of the conversion of the particular negative in enabling the reduction of these moods.

Key Terms
انعكاس (in'ikās): The operation of converting a proposition by swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth value; here specifically conversion of the particular negative. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition as an immediate inference.]
سالبة جزئية خاصة (sāliba juz'iyya khāṣṣa): A particular negative proposition (e.g., 'Some S are not P'); its conversion is unique because it converts to itself. [Note: In some later texts, the term 'خاصة' may be omitted, but the concept remains the same.]
إنتاج (intāj): The property of a syllogistic mood to yield a necessary conclusion; productivity. [Note: Stable across the tradition as a technical term for validity in syllogism.]

Condition of Actuality for Minor Premise in First Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Commentary explains condition of actuality for minor premise in first figure mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first figure mixed syllogism is valid by checking if the minor premise is actual rather than possible.

Core Idea
In first figure mixed syllogisms, the minor premise must be actual for the conclusion to follow.

This passage from al-Qutbi's commentary explains a crucial condition for validity in modal syllogisms: when premises have different modalities, the first figure requires that the minor premise be actual. If it is merely possible, the judgment does not transfer from the middle term to the minor term. We will examine why this condition is necessary and look at an example to solidify the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition stated in the matn
The matn says: 'شرطه بحسب الجهة فعلية الصغرى' (its condition regarding modality is the actuality of the minor premise). Write that down as the core rule.
2
Understand why actuality is required
The commentary explains: the major premise indicates that everything that is actually the middle term is judged by the major term. If the minor premise is possible, the minor term is not actually the middle term, so the judgment remains potential and does not transfer.
This is because the middle term must be actually predicated of the minor term for the syllogism to work.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Example: 'Every donkey is rideable' (major, actual) and 'Zayd is possibly a donkey' (minor, possible). The conclusion 'Zayd is rideable' does not follow because Zayd is not actually a donkey.
The counterexample shows the necessity of actuality.
4
Contrast with al-Farabi's alternative view
Sharif's marginal note mentions that al-Farabi accepted sufficient possibility. If so, the counterexample fails because the major premise would then be 'Everything ridden is Zayd's horse', which is false. This highlights that the condition depends on one's stance on descriptive terms actually existing.
This shows there is scholarly disagreement on the role of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الفصل الثاني في المختلطات أما الشكل الأوّل فشرطه بحسب الجهة فعلية الصغرى. القول: المختلطات هي الأقيسة الحاصلة من خلط الموجبة بعضها مع بعض، وعند اعتبار الجهات في المقدمات يعتبر لإنتاج الأشكال شرائط : أما الشكل الأول فشرطه باعتبار الجهة فعلية الصغرى، فإنّها لو كانت ممكنة لم يجب تعدّي الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، لأن الكبرى تدلّ أن كل ماهو أوسط بالفعل محكوم عليه بالأكبر، والأصغر ليس تما أوسط بالفعل - بل بالإمكان - فجاز أن يبقى بالقوة ولا يخرج منها إلى الفعل، فلم يتعد الحكم من الأوسط إليه. مثلاً يصدق في الفرض المذكور كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان (۱) اشتراط ذلك مبني على أن المعتبر في الوصف العنواني أن يكون بالفعل بحسب الخارج. وأما إذا اكتفي بمجرد الإمكان كما هو مذهب الفارابي، فالممكنة تنتج في صغرى الشكل الأول، وكذا في صغرى الشكل الثالث، والنقض المذكور هاهنا وهناك مندفع ، إذ لا تصدق حينئذ المقدمة القائلة «كل مركوب زيد فرس» (شریف)
He said: The second chapter on mixed syllogisms. As for the first figure, its condition according to modality is the actuality of the minor premise. The discourse: Mixed syllogisms are the syllogisms resulting from mixing affirmative [premises] with each other, and when considering modalities in premises, conditions are considered for the productivity of figures. As for the first figure, its condition with respect to modality is the actuality of the minor premise, because if it were possible, the judgment would not necessarily transfer from the middle term to the minor term, since the major premise indicates that everything that is actually the middle term is judged by the major term, and the minor term is not actually the middle term — rather possibly — so it remains in potentiality and does not emerge into actuality, so the judgment does not transfer from the middle to it. For example, in the mentioned supposition, 'Every donkey is rideable' and 'Zayd is a donkey possibly' is true. (1) This condition is based on the fact that what is considered in the descriptive term is that it be actual with respect to the external. But if one is satisfied with mere possibility, as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, then the possible premise produces in the minor of the first figure, and likewise in the minor of the third figure, and the mentioned refutation here and there is rebutted, for then the premise 'Everything ridden is a horse of Zayd' is not true. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage opens the second chapter on mixed syllogisms (المختلطات), which are syllogisms with premises of different modalities. The matn states that for the first figure, the condition regarding modality is that the minor premise must be actual (فعلية الصغرى). The commentary explains that mixed syllogisms are those formed by mixing affirmative premises with each other, and when considering modalities, conditions are required for the productivity of each figure. For the first figure, the minor premise must be actual because the major premise indicates that everything that is actually the middle term is judged by the major term. If the minor premise is merely possible, the minor term is not actually the middle term; it is only potentially so. Thus, the judgment does not necessarily transfer from the middle to the minor, and the minor term could remain in potentiality. An example is given: 'Every donkey is rideable' (major, actual) and 'Zayd is possibly a donkey' (minor, possible). The conclusion 'Zayd is rideable' does not follow because Zayd is not actually a donkey. The marginal note by Sharif al-Jurjani points out that this condition rests on the requirement that the descriptive term be actual in the external world. If one accepts possibility as sufficient, as al-Farabi did, then possible premises would produce valid syllogisms in the minor of the first figure and also in the third figure. In that case, the counterexample mentioned above would be rebutted, because the premise 'Everything ridden is Zayd's horse' would not be true. This shows the disagreement between logicians on the role of possibility in syllogistic premises.

جهة

Key Takeaway
In first figure mixed syllogisms, the minor premise must be actual for the conclusion to be guaranteed; a possible minor blocks the transfer of judgment.

Condition for First Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Actuality of Minor Premise
Author explains why minor premise must be actual in first-figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya) in first-figure mixed syllogisms and evaluate the validity of a mixed syllogism using this condition.

Core Idea
The minor premise must be actual, not merely possible, for the middle term's judgment to transfer to the minor term.

In modal logic, mixed syllogisms combine premises of different modalities. The first figure has a special condition: the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this condition and shows why a possible minor premise fails to guarantee the transfer of judgment. You will also encounter a disagreement with al-Farabi, who allowed possible minor premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that for the first figure, the minor premise must be actual in modality. Find this statement in the Arabic.
The term 'fi'liyyat al-sughra' is key.
2
Understand the reasoning
The major premise says 'everything that is actually middle is major'. If the minor is only possible, the minor term is not actually middle, so the judgment does not transfer. Trace this logical chain in the commentary.
The reason hinges on actual instantiation of the middle term.
3
Examine the example
The example: 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse' does not yield 'Every donkey is possibly a horse'. Why? Because the donkey is not actually ridden by Zayd.
The major premise's condition (actual riding) is not satisfied.
4
Consider al-Farabi's alternative
Al-Farabi held that mere possibility suffices for the minor premise. If so, the example's major premise ('Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse') would not be true under his interpretation. Note the implications.
The debate shows that the condition depends on one's metaphysics of actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول: المختلطات هي الأقيسة الحاصلة من خلط الموجبة بعضها مع بعض، وعند اعتبار الجهات في المقدّمات يعتبر لإنتاج الأشكال شرائط : أما الشكل الأول فشرطه باعتبار الجهة فعلية الصغرى، فإنّها لو كانت ممكنة لم يجب تعدّي الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، لأن الكبرى تدلّ أن كل ماهو أوسط بالفعل محكوم عليه بالأكبر، والأصغر ليس تما أوسط بالفعل - بل بالإمكان - فجاز أن يبقى بالقوة ولا يخرج منها إلى الفعل، فلم يتعد الحكم من الأوسط إليه.
مثلاً يصدق في الفرض المذكور كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان العام، وكلّ مركوب زيد فرس بالضرورة»، ولا يصدق «كل حمار فرس بالإمكان العام»، لأن معنى الكبرى أن كل ماهو مركوب زيد بالفعل فهو فرس بالضرورة، والحمار ليس بمركوب زيد بالفعل أصلاً، فالحكم على المركوب بالفعل لا يتعدى إليه.
(۱) اشتراط ذلك مبني على أن المعتبر في الوصف العنواني أن يكون بالفعل بحسب الخارج. وأما إذا اكتفي بمجرد الإمكان كما هو مذهب الفارابي، فالممكنة تنتج في صغرى الشكل الأول، وكذا في صغرى الشكل الثالث، والنقض المذكور هاهنا وهناك مندفع ، إذ لا تصدق حينئذ المقدمة القائلة «كل مركوب زيد فرس» (شریف)
The statement: Mixed syllogisms are the syllogisms resulting from mixing the affirmative ones with each other. And when considering modalities in the premises, conditions are considered for the production of the figures. As for the first figure, its condition with respect to modality is the actuality of the minor premise, because if it were possible, the transfer of the judgment from the middle term to the minor term would not be necessary. This is because the major premise indicates that everything that is actually middle is judged by the major, and the minor term is not actually middle—rather it is possible—so it is possible that it remains in potentiality and does not emerge from it to actuality, so the judgment does not transfer from the middle term to it. For example, in the mentioned hypothesis, 'Every donkey is, in respect of general possibility, ridden by Zayd' and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse' are true, but 'Every donkey is a horse in respect of general possibility' is not true, because the meaning of the major premise is that everything actually ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse, and the donkey is not actually ridden by Zayd at all, so the judgment concerning what is actually ridden does not transfer to it.
(1) This condition is based on the fact that what is considered in the attributive description is that it is actual in the external. But if one is satisfied with mere possibility, as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, then the possible premise produces a conclusion in the minor of the first figure, and likewise in the minor of the third figure, and the objection mentioned here and there is refuted, because then the premise 'Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse' would not be true (Sharif).
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage explains a key condition for mixed syllogisms (mukhtalitat) in the first figure. The author states that mixed syllogisms result from mixing affirmative premises (i.e., from different modalities). For the first figure, the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya) in modality. The reason given is that if the minor premise were merely possible, the middle term would not actually apply to the minor term, so the judgment of the major premise (which depends on actuality of the middle term) would not transfer to the minor term. The author illustrates with an example: suppose the minor premise is 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' (general possibility) and the major premise is 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse'. The conclusion 'Every donkey is possibly a horse' does not follow because the donkey is not actually ridden by Zayd. The marginal note from Sharif adds that this condition assumes the middle term must be actual in reality. However, al-Farabi held that mere possibility suffices, in which case the possible premise would produce a conclusion in the first and third figures. But then the major premise used in the objection would not be true.

Determining Conclusion Modality in First-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Rule for conclusion modality in first-figure mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure mixed syllogism based on the modality of the major premise and the type of minor premise.

Core Idea
In first-figure mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's modality follows the major unless the major is descriptive.

Determining the modality of the conclusion in mixed syllogisms is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic. The author presents a clear rule: if the major premise is not one of the four descriptive types, the conclusion matches the major; otherwise, it follows the minor with certain qualifications removed. This lesson will guide you through applying this rule and understanding its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise's type
Determine whether the major premise is one of the four descriptive types (al-mashrutatayn wa al-'urfiyyatayn) or one of the other nine modal types. This classification is essential for applying the rule.
Descriptive types include the two conditional and two customary propositions.
2
Apply the rule for non-descriptive major
If the major is not descriptive, the conclusion takes the same modality as the major premise. This is the simpler case: the conclusion mirrors the major's modal qualification.
3
Handle descriptive major
If the major is descriptive, the conclusion's modality is taken from the minor premise, but with the qualifications of non-permanence (al-ladawam) and non-necessity (al-ladurura) removed. Additionally, if the major is one of the two general types, specific necessity from the minor is retained; if the major is one of the two specific types, non-permanence is added.
This adjustment ensures the conclusion's modality is correctly aligned with the premises.
4
Consider the actuality condition
Note that the minor premise must be actual (not merely possible) for the syllogism to be productive. This condition eliminates 26 combinations where the minor is possible, leaving 143 productive combinations out of 169 total.
The count helps understand the scope of valid modal syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١٠١ - نتائج المختلطات في الشكل الأول] القال والنتيجة فيه كالكبرى إن كانت غير المشروطتين والعرفيتين، وإلا فكالصغرى محذوفاً عنها قيد اللادوام ،واللاضرورة ، والضرورة المخصوصة بالصغرى إن كانت الكبرى إحدى العامتين ، و بعد ضم اللادوام إليها إن كانت إحدى الخاصتين.
أقول: قد عرفت أنّ الموجهات المعتبرة ثلاث عشرة، فإذا اعتبرناها في الصغرى والكبرى حصل مائة وتسعة وستون اختلاطاً، ضرب ثلاثة عشر في نفسها، لكن اشتراط فعليّة الصغرى أسقط من تلك الجملة ستة وعشرين اختلاطاً، وهي حاصلة من ضرب الممكنتين في ثلاثة عشر، فبقيت الاختلاطات المنتجة مائة وثلاثة وأربعين.
وضابط إنتاجها أن الكبرى إما أن تكون إحدى الوصفيات الأربع - التي هي المشروطتان والعرفيتان - أو غيرها، فإن كانت الكبرى غير الوصفيات الأربع بأن تكون إحدى التسع الباقية فالنتيجة كالكبرى، وإن كانت إحداها فالنتيجة كالصغري ؛ لكن إن كان فيها قيد «اللادوام» أو «اللاضرورة» حذفناه، وكذلك إن وجدنا فيها ضرورة مخصوصة بها - أي غير مشتركة بينها وبين الكبرى - ثمّ يُنظر في الكبرى : إن لم يكن فيها قيد
[101 - Results of Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure] The statement: The conclusion in it is like the major premise if the major is not one of the two conditional or two customary types; otherwise, it is like the minor premise, with the qualification of non-permanence (al-ladawam) and non-necessity (al-ladurura) removed from it, and the specific necessity belonging to the minor if the major is one of the two general types, and after attaching non-permanence to it if the major is one of the two specific types.
I say: You have known that the considered modal propositions are thirteen. So if we consider them in the minor and major, we get one hundred and sixty-nine combinations, multiplying thirteen by itself. But the condition of actuality of the minor removes from that total twenty-six combinations, which result from multiplying the two possible propositions by thirteen. So the productive combinations remaining are one hundred and forty-three.
The rule for their production is that the major is either one of the four descriptive types (al-wasfiyyat) – which are the two conditional and two customary – or not. If the major is not one of the four descriptive types, i.e., it is one of the remaining nine, then the conclusion is like the major. If it is one of them, then the conclusion is like the minor; but if it contains the qualification of non-permanence (al-ladawam) or non-necessity (al-ladurura), we remove it. Likewise, if we find in it a specific necessity – i.e., not shared between the minor and the major. Then we look at the major: if it does not contain a qualification...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage provides the rule for determining the modality of the conclusion in mixed first-figure syllogisms. The author first states a general principle: if the major premise is not one of the four 'descriptive' types (mashrutatayn and 'urfiyyatayn), the conclusion takes the modality of the major. If it is one of these four, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor, but with certain qualifications removed (non-permanence, non-necessity) and with adjustments for specific necessity. Then the author calculates the total number of mixed syllogisms: there are 13 modal propositions; considering all pairs gives 169 combinations. The condition that the minor must be actual eliminates 26 combinations where the minor is possible (2 possible types × 13 = 26), leaving 143 productive combinations. Finally, the author refines the rule: the major is either one of four descriptive types (two conditional, two customary) or one of the other nine. If the major is not descriptive, the conclusion matches the major. If it is descriptive, the conclusion matches the minor after removing certain modal qualifications, and further adjustments depend on whether the major is general or specific.

General Rule for Mixed Modal Syllogisms in the First Figure
Author enumerates mixtures and states the rule for concluding from mixed premises in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for determining the conclusion's modality in a first-figure mixed modal syllogism, given the modalities of the premises.

Core Idea
In the first figure, the conclusion follows the major unless the major is descriptive, then it follows the minor after adjustments.

This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary presents the master rule for mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure. It first calculates the total possible mixtures and then bifurcates based on the type of the major premise. By breaking down the rule step by step, you will learn how to derive the conclusion's modality correctly.

Guided Path
1
Count the possible mixtures
The author starts with 13 considered modalities, giving 169 (13×13) possible mixtures. However, the condition that the minor must be actual (not possible) removes 26 mixtures where both premises are possible, leaving 143 productive mixtures.
This counting shows the scope of the rule.
2
Distinguish two cases based on the major
If the major is not one of the four descriptive types (waṣfiyyāt), the conclusion takes the major's modality. If the major is descriptive, the conclusion takes the minor's modality after adjustments.
The rule depends only on the major's type, not the minor's.
3
Adjust the minor modality when the major is descriptive
First, delete any 'not-permanent' (lā dawām) or 'not-necessary' (lā ḍarūra) qualifiers from the minor, as well as any 'private necessity' (ḍarūra makhṣūṣa). Then, if the major lacks 'not-permanent', the adjusted minor is the conclusion; if the major has 'not-permanent', annex that qualifier to the adjusted minor.
Negative qualifiers are removed because the minor is affirmative and the first figure requires affirmative premises.
4
Justify the first case (non-descriptive major)
The author argues by subsumption: the major asserts a modality for everything that actually has the middle term. Since the minor's subject actually has the middle, it inherits that modality. This is a valid first-figure syllogism.
Formally: Major: Every actually-M is P with modality M. Minor: S is actually M. Conclusion: S is P with modality M.
5
Justify the second case (descriptive major)
A descriptive major ties the major term's permanence to the middle term's permanence. Thus, the conclusion's modality reflects how the minor relates to the middle: if the middle holds permanently of the minor, so does the major; if temporarily, then temporarily; if necessarily, then necessarily (since necessary of necessary is necessary).
This mirrors the rule that the conclusion follows the minor in temporal or necessary aspect.

أقول: قد عرفت أنّ الموجهات المعتبرة ثلاث عشرة، فإذا اعتبرناها في الصغرى والكبرى حصل مائة وتسعة وستون اختلاطاً، ضرب ثلاثة عشر في نفسها، لكن اشتراط فعليّة الصغرى أسقط من تلك الجملة ستة وعشرين اختلاطاً، وهي حاصلة من ضرب الممكنتين في ثلاثه عشر، فبقيت الاختلاطات المنتجة مائة وثلاثة وأربعين. وضابط إنتاجها أن الكبرى إما أن تكون إحدى الوصفيات الأربع - التي هي المشروطتان والعرفيتان - أو غيرها، فإن كانت الكبرى غير الوصفيات الأربع بأن تكون إحدى التسع الباقية فالنتيجة كالكبرى، وإن كانت إحداها فالنتيجة كالصغري ؛ لكن إن كان فيها قيد «اللادوام» أو «اللاضرورة» حذفناه، وكذلك إن وجدنا فيها ضرورة مخصوصة بها - أي غير مشتركة بينها وبين الكبرى - ثمّ يُنظر في الكبرى : إن لم يكن فيها قيد «اللادوام» كما إذا كانت إحدى العامتين - كان المحفوظ بعينه النتيجة، وإن كان فيها قيد «اللادوام كما إذا كانت إحدى الخاصتين - ضممناه إلى المحفوظ، كان المجموع الحاصل منهما جهة النتيجة. أما الأول - وهو أن الكبرى إذا كانت غير الوصفيات الأربع كانت النتيجة كالكبرى - فللاندارج البين، فإنّ الكبرى حينئذ دلّت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط بالفعل فهو محكوم عليه بالأكبر بالجهة المعتبرة في الكبرى، لكن الأصغر مما ثبت له الأوسط بالفعل، فيكون محكوماً عليه بالأكبر بتلك الجهة المعتبرة. وأما الثاني - وهو أن الكبرى إذا كانت إحدى الوصفيات الأربع كانت النتيجة كالصغرى - فإنّ الكبرى تدل على أن دوام الأكبر بدوام الأوسط، ولما كان الأوسط مستديماً للأكبر كان ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر بحسب ثبوت الأوسط له؛ فإن كان ثبوت الأوسط له دائماً كان ثبوت الأكبر له دائماً أيضاً، وإن كان في وقت كان في وقت، وإن كان الأوسط مستديماً للأكبر بالضرورة كما في المشروطتين - كان ضرورة ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر بحسب ضرورة ثبوت الأوسط له ، لأن الضروري للضروري ضروري. وأما حذف لادوام الصغرى ولا ضرورتها فلأن الصغرى لما كانت موجبة كان اللادوام واللاضرورة فيها سالبة والسالبة لامدخل لها في إنتاج هذا الشكل. وأما حذف الضرورة المخصوصة بالصغرى فلأن الكبرى إذا لم يكن فيها ضرورة جاز انفكاك الأكبر عن كلّ ما ثبت له الأوسط، لكن الأصغر
I say: You have known that the considered modal propositions are thirteen. So if we consider them in the minor and major, we obtain one hundred and sixty-nine mixtures, multiplying thirteen by itself. But the condition of actuality of the minor drops from that total twenty-six mixtures, which result from multiplying the two possible (propositions) by thirteen. So the productive mixtures remaining are one hundred and forty-three. The rule for their production is that the major is either one of the four descriptive types – which are the two conditional and the two conventional – or other than them. If the major is not among the four descriptive types, i.e., it is one of the remaining nine, then the conclusion is like the major. If it is one of them, then the conclusion is like the minor; but if it contains the qualifier 'not-permanent' or 'not-necessary', we delete it; likewise if we find in it a private necessity – i.e., not shared between it and the major. Then we look at the major: if it does not contain the qualifier 'not-permanent', as when it is one of the two general types, then what is preserved is exactly the conclusion. If it contains the qualifier 'not-permanent', as when it is one of the two particular types, we annex it to what is preserved, and the sum resulting from both is the modality of the conclusion. As for the first case – that when the major is not among the four descriptive types, the conclusion is like the major – this is due to obvious subsumption. For then the major indicates that everything to which the middle is actually attributed is judged by the major with the modality considered in the major. But the minor is among those to which the middle is actually attributed, so it is judged by the major with that considered modality. As for the second case – that when the major is one of the four descriptive types, the conclusion is like the minor – because the major indicates that the permanence of the major is due to the permanence of the middle. Since the middle is the cause of the permanence of the major, the attribution of the major to the minor is in accordance with the attribution of the middle to it. So if the attribution of the middle to it is permanent, then the attribution of the major to it is also permanent; if it is at a time, then at a time; and if the middle is the cause of the permanence of the major by necessity, as in the two conditionals, then the necessity of the attribution of the major to the minor is according to the necessity of the attribution of the middle to it, because the necessary of the necessary is necessary. As for the deletion of the 'not-permanent' and 'not-necessary' of the minor, it is because when the minor is affirmative, the 'not-permanent' and 'not-necessary' in it are negative, and the negative has no role in the production of this figure. As for the deletion of the private necessity of the minor, it is because when the major has no necessity, it is possible that the major be separate from everything to which the middle is attributed, but the minor...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for producing conclusions from mixed modal syllogisms (where premises have different modalities) in the first figure. The author begins by counting the possible mixtures: with 13 considered modal propositions, mixing minor and major gives 169 possibilities (13×13). However, the condition that the minor premise must be actual (fi‘liyya) eliminates 26 mixtures where both premises are possible (mumkināt), leaving 143 productive mixtures. The central rule is stated: the conclusion's modality depends on whether the major premise is one of the four 'descriptive' (waṣfiyyāt) modalities – the two conditional (mashrūṭatān) and two conventional ('urfiyyatān) – or not. If the major is not descriptive (i.e., one of the remaining nine), the conclusion takes the modality of the major. If it is descriptive, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor, but with certain adjustments: any 'not-permanent' (lā dawām) or 'not-necessary' (lā ḍarūra) qualifiers in the minor are deleted, as is any 'private necessity' (ḍarūra makhṣūṣa) specific to the minor. Then, if the major does not contain 'not-permanent' (as in the two general types, al-‘āmmatān), the preserved minor modality is the conclusion. If the major contains 'not-permanent' (as in the two particular types, al-khāṣṣatān), that qualifier is added to the preserved modality. The author then justifies the first case: if the major is not descriptive, it asserts that all things that actually have the middle term have the major term with a certain modality; since the minor subject actually has the middle, it follows the major. The second case: if the major is descriptive, it indicates that the major's permanence depends on the middle's permanence; thus the minor's attribution of the middle (temporal or necessary) determines the conclusion's modality. The deletion of negative qualifiers from the minor is because the minor is affirmative, so these qualifiers are negative and irrelevant in the first figure's affirmative syllogisms. The deletion of private necessity from the minor is to avoid assuming necessity when the major lacks it, which would allow separation of the major from the middle. The passage ends mid-explanation.

Conclusion Modality in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author explains how conclusion modality is derived with descriptive majors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure mixed modal syllogism given the modalities of the premises, according to the rules for descriptive majors.

Core Idea
Modality of the conclusion is not simply the weaker premise; it depends on the specific type of descriptive major.

Modal syllogisms often combine premises with different modalities. When the major premise is one of the four descriptive types (الوصفيات الأربع), the rules for deriving the conclusion's modality become more complex. This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyyah explains how necessity and permanence interact, and when the necessity of the minor is removed or the non-permanence of the major is added. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh where modal distinctions matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises' modalities
Begin by noting the modality of the minor premise (necessary, permanent, or non-permanent) and the type of the major premise (one of the four descriptive types: general conditioned, special conditioned, general customary, special customary).
The minor is always affirmative in the first figure.
2
Check if the major is descriptive or non-descriptive
If the major is not one of the four descriptive types, simpler rules apply. Here, focus on descriptive majors: the passage explains the rules for these specific cases.
Descriptive majors add conditions about time or custom.
3
Apply the removal and addition rules
Based on the combination, some modalities are removed from the minor (e.g., necessity when the major lacks necessity) and some are added from the major (e.g., non-permanence). Follow the examples: necessary minor with general conditioned yields necessary; with special conditioned yields necessary non-permanent (but invalid syllogism); with general customary yields permanent; with special customary yields permanent non-permanent.
The removal of necessity from the minor occurs because the major disconnects from the middle term.
4
Test for syllogistic validity
Some combinations (e.g., necessary minor + special conditioned major) produce a conclusion but cannot form a valid syllogism with true premises because the syllogism would imply the antecedent without the consequent. This is explained as 'impossible'.
A valid syllogism must guarantee the truth of the conclusion given true premises.
5
Generalize the pattern
For a permanent minor, the conclusion is permanent with general majors, and permanent non-permanent with special majors. Memorize the patterns: general majors preserve the minor's necessity (or permanence) minus removals; special majors add non-permanence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حذف لادوام الصغرى ولا ضرورتها فلأن الصغرى لما كانت موجبة كان اللادوام واللاضرورة فيها سالبة والسالبة لامدخل لها في إنتاج هذا الشكل. وأما حذف الضرورة المخصوصة بالصغرى فلأن الكبرى إذا لم يكن فيها ضرورة جاز انفكاك الأكبر عن كلّ ما ثبت له الأوسط، لكن الأصغر مماثبت له الأوسط فيجوز انفكاك الأكبر عن الأصغر، فلم يتعد ضرورة الصغرى إلى النتيجة. وأما ضمّ لادوام الكبرى فللاندارج البين أيضاً، فإن الكبرى حينئذ تدلّ على أن الأكبر غير دائم لكلّ ما هو أوسط بالفعل، والأصغر مما هو أوسط بالفعل، فيكون الأكبر غير دائم له؛ مثلاً الصغرى الضرورية مع المشروطة العامة تنتج ضرورية ، لأن النتيجة كالصغرى بعينها، ومع المشروطة الخاصة تنتج ضرورية لادائمة لانضمام اللادوام مع الصغرى، لكن القياس الصادق المقدمات لا يتألف منهما، لأن القياس ملزوم للنتيجة، فلو انتظم القياس الصادق المقدّمات منهما لزم صدق الملزوم بدون اللازم - وإنه محال. ومع العرفية العامة ينتج دائمة، لحذف الضرورة التي هي بالصغرى منها، فلم يبق إلا الدوام. المختصة ومع العرفية الخاصة دائمة لادائمة بحذف الضرورة وضم اللادوام، والقياس الصادق المقدمات لا ينتظم منهما أيضاً كما عرفت. والصغرى الدائمة مع إحدى العامتين تنتج دائمة، ومع إحدى الخاصتين دائمة لادائمة، ولا يصدق مقدّمتا القياس منهما أيضاً كما عرفت.
And as for the removal of non-permanence and non-necessity of the minor, it is because when the minor is affirmative, non-permanence and non-necessity in it are negative, and the negative has no entry in the production of this figure. And as for the removal of the necessity specific to the minor, it is because when the major has no necessity, it is possible for the major to disconnect from everything to which the middle is established, but the minor is among those to which the middle is established, so it is possible for the major to disconnect from the minor; thus the necessity of the minor does not extend to the conclusion. And as for adding the non-permanence of the major, it is also due to obvious inclusion, for the major then indicates that the major is not permanent for everything that is actually middle, and the minor is among what is actually middle, so the major is not permanent for it. For example: the necessary minor with the general conditioned produces necessary, because the conclusion is identical to the minor; and with the special conditioned produces necessary non-permanent due to the addition of non-permanence to the minor, but a syllogism with true premises cannot be composed from them, because the syllogism is necessary for the conclusion, so if a syllogism with true premises were regular from them, it would necessitate the truth of the necessary without the necessary — and that is impossible. And with the general customary it produces permanent, due to the removal of the necessity that is in the minor from it, so nothing remains but permanence. And with the special customary it produces permanent non-permanent due to the removal of necessity and the addition of non-permanence, and a syllogism with true premises cannot be regular from them either, as you have known. And the permanent minor with either of the two general types produces permanent, and with either of the two special types produces permanent non-permanent, and both premises of the syllogism cannot be true from them either, as you have known.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyyah explains the rules for determining the modality of the conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when the major premise is one of the four 'descriptive' types (الوصفيات الأربع) or not. The author first discusses the removal (حذف) of non-permanence (اللادوام) and non-necessity (اللاضرورة) from the minor premise. Since the minor is affirmative, these negative modalities in it are negative propositions, and negatives do not contribute to production in the first figure. Next, he explains that the necessity specific to the minor is removed because if the major lacks necessity, the major can disconnect from the middle, and since the minor has the middle, the major may disconnect from the minor, so the minor's necessity does not carry over to the conclusion. Then he addresses adding the non-permanence of the major: the major indicates that the major is not permanent for everything that actually has the middle, and the minor actually has the middle, so the major is not permanent for the minor. The author then provides examples: (1) Necessary minor with general conditioned major yields a necessary conclusion (identical to minor). (2) Necessary minor with special conditioned major yields necessary non-permanent, but a valid syllogism with true premises cannot be formed because the syllogism is necessary for the conclusion, which would entail a true antecedent without the consequent—impossible. (3) With general customary major, the conclusion is permanent (since necessity from minor is dropped). (4) With special customary major, the conclusion is permanent non-permanent. Finally, the permanent minor with either general major yields permanent; with either special major yields permanent non-permanent, and again true premises cannot constitute a valid syllogism. This is a detailed expansion of the matn's rules, using the vocabulary of modal logic.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion's modality in mixed modal syllogisms with descriptive majors is derived by removing necessity from the minor when the major lacks it, and adding non-permanence from the major when it is present, following specific patterns for each descriptive type.

Defense of Necessity in First-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author rebuts an objection concerning the necessity of the major term in a first-figure mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's rebuttal of the objection that conditional necessity on the middle term undermines the conclusion's necessity in a first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Conditional necessity on the middle term does not destroy necessity if the description is essential to the minor.

In modal syllogistic, the necessity of a conclusion can be challenged if the major premise's necessity is conditional on the middle term. The author refutes this by showing that if the description (middle term) is necessary for the minor's essence, then the necessity carries through even when the middle term is omitted from the conclusion. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Defended
The author asserts that in a first-figure mixed syllogism, if the major premise is necessary, then the conclusion is necessary. The middle term is the minor term in the major premise.
The claim is that the major term is necessarily true of the minor.
2
Understand the Objection
The objector says: if the necessity of the major is conditional on the description (the middle term), then when the middle term is omitted from the conclusion, the necessity may not remain. The conclusion would only be necessary as long as the middle term is present, but it is absent.
This would mean the conclusion is not genuinely necessary.
3
Extract the Author's Refutation
The author argues that if the description of the middle term is necessary for the essence of the minor term, then whenever the minor exists, the description exists. And whenever both exist, the major is necessary. Therefore, whenever the minor exists, the major is necessary—no need for the middle term in the conclusion.
The key is that the description is inseparable from the minor's essence.
4
Formalize as a Chain of Conditionals
Represent the reasoning: (1) If (description necessary for minor's essence) then (whenever minor exists, description exists). (2) If (whenever minor exists, description exists) then (whenever minor exists, major is necessary). Therefore, if (description necessary for minor's essence) then (whenever minor exists, major is necessary). This is a valid hypothetical syllogism.
The author affirms the antecedent, so the conclusion follows.

الأوسط هو الأصغر، فيكون الأكبر ضروري الثبوت له - وإن فسرت بالضرورة بشرط الوصف لم تنتج الصغرى الضرورية معها ضرورية كالدائمة لدلالة الكبرى على أن ضرورة الأكبر بشرط وصف الأوسط، فاللازم ليس إلا أن الأكبر ضروري للأصغر بشرط وصف الأوسط، لكن الأوسط واجب الحذف عن النتيجة فجاز أن لا يبقى ضرورة الأكبر. لأنا نقول: وصف الأوسط إذا كان ضرورياً لذات الأصغر فكلّما تحقق الأصغر تحقق ذات الأصغر ووصف الأوسط بالضرورة، وكلّما تحققا ثبت ضرورة الأكبر، فكلّما تحقق الأصغر ثبت ضرورة الأكبر ؛ وهو المطلوب.
The middle is the minor, so the major is necessarily affirmed of it—and if necessity is interpreted as conditional upon the description, then the necessary minor together with it does not yield a necessary conclusion, like the perpetual [minor], because the major indicates that the necessity of the major is conditional upon the description of the middle. So the necessary consequence is only that the major is necessary for the minor conditional upon the description of the middle, but the middle must be omitted from the conclusion, so it is possible that the necessity of the major does not remain. We say: If the description of the middle is necessary for the essence of the minor, then whenever the minor is realized, the essence of the minor and the description of the middle are necessarily realized, and whenever both are realized, the necessity of the major is established; so whenever the minor is realized, the necessity of the major is established; that is what is required.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a defense of a position regarding the necessity condition in first-figure mixed syllogisms. The author begins by stating a claim: 'The middle is the minor, so the major is necessarily affirmed of it.' Then he presents an objection: if necessity is interpreted as conditional upon the description (i.e., the middle term's description), then a necessary minor together with such a conditional necessity does not yield a necessary conclusion, like a perpetual minor, because the major indicates that the necessity of the major is conditional upon the description of the middle. So the only necessary consequence is that the major is necessary for the minor when the description of the middle is present. But since the middle must be omitted from the conclusion, the necessity may not remain. The author then refutes this objection: if the description of the middle is necessary for the essence of the minor, then whenever the minor exists, both the minor's essence and the description of the middle necessarily exist. Whenever both exist, the necessity of the major is established. Therefore, whenever the minor exists, the necessity of the major is established. Thus the conclusion is necessarily true without requiring the middle term in the conclusion. This is a typical logical refinement in modal syllogistic.

الأصغر
الأكبر

C) The major term is necessarily true of the minor even when the middle term is omitted.
D) The necessity of the major depends on the middle term in the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
In first-figure modal syllogisms, if the necessity of the major premise is conditional on the description of the middle term, the conclusion remains necessary provided the description is necessarily attached to the minor term.

Reading the Table of Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure
Table of mixed syllogisms in the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion modality for any valid combination of minor and major premises in the first figure using the given table of mixed syllogisms.

Core Idea
The table of mixed syllogisms is a master key to all valid modal conclusions in the first figure.

Modal logic, which deals with propositions qualified by necessity, perpetuity, conditionality, etc., is essential in Islamic theology for constructing rigorous arguments. In the first figure, not every combination of modal premises yields a conclusion. The author presents a comprehensive table that maps each pair of premise modalities to its resulting conclusion modality. This lesson will teach you how to navigate this table, understand the modal categories, and apply it to theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify rows and columns
The table's rows represent the modality of the minor premise (al-sughra), and the columns represent the modality of the major premise (al-kubra). Locate the row and column headers: e.g., 'al-mashruta al-'amma' (general conditioned) or 'al-daruriyya' (necessary).
The first column header is missing in the OCR, but from the text it begins with 'al-mashruta al-'amma'.
2
Understand the modal abbreviations
Each cell contains the modality of the conclusion. For example, 'da'ima' means perpetual, 'daruriyya' means necessary, 'wujudiyya la-da'ima' means existential non-perpetual. Familiarize yourself with these terms from the key terms list.
The table uses shorthand; refer to the key terms for full definitions.
3
Find a specific combination
Suppose the minor is 'necessary' (al-daruriyya) and the major is 'perpetual' (al-da'ima). Locate the row for 'daruriyya' and the column for 'da'ima'. The cell reads 'da'ima' (perpetual), so the conclusion is perpetual.
The quiz uses this exact combination.
4
Extract patterns and principles
Notice that not all combinations are listed; some cells are empty because they do not yield a valid conclusion. The author hints that the table is based on an underlying rule (al-dabit) from which all results can be derived. Look for patterns, e.g., a necessary minor often yields a perpetual conclusion when combined with certain majors.
Understanding the rule behind the table is more powerful than memorizing each cell.

Two Conditions for Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author lists two conditions for second-figure modal mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a mixed modal syllogism in the second figure to be productive and apply them to determine if a given modal combination is valid.

Core Idea
In the second figure, modal mixing requires either a perpetual minor or a convertible-negative major, and only specific modal combinations are allowed.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has its own constraints when modalities are mixed. The author lays out two conditions that must be met for such syllogisms to yield a conclusion. This lesson unpacks those conditions and shows why certain combinations fail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions from the matn
Find the two statements in the Arabic beginning with 'أما الشكل الثاني فشرطه ... أمران' and separate the two conditions.
The first condition is about the minor being perpetual or the major having convertible negatives; the second is about the possible premise only with specific majors.
2
Understand the rationale via the commentary
The commentator explains why the first condition is necessary: if the minor is not perpetual and the major is not convertible, the specific types (temporal, conditioned) lead to non-productiveness.
Focus on the commentator's phrase about 'اختلاط الصغريين ... غير منتج'.
3
Apply the conditions to sample modal premise pairs
Given a second-figure syllogism with modal premises, check if the minor is perpetual (necessary or perpetual) or if the major is a convertible negative. Then check that the second condition is satisfied (possible only with absolute necessity or two conditioned majors).
Use a Kalam example: 'Every event is contingent (possible), and everything necessary is not contingent' – here minor is possible, major is necessary, so second condition is met? Actually, the major is necessary, not absolute necessity? We need absolute necessity. Adjust example accordingly.

قال: وأما الشكل الثاني فشرطه بحسب الجهة أمران:
احدهما صدق الدوام على الصغرى أوكون الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب.
والثاني: أن لا تستعمل الممكنة إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة أو مع الكبريين المشروطتين.
أقول: يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الجهة أمران، كل واحد منهما أحد الأمرين:
الأول: صدق الدوام على الصغرى - أي كونها ضرورية أو دائمة - أوكون الكبرى من القضايا الست المنعكسة السوالب؛ وذلك لأنه لوانتفيا لكانت الصغرى غير الضرورية والدائمة - وهي إحدى عشرة – والكبرى من القضايا السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب، وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية، لأن المشروطة الخاصة أخص من المشروطة العامة والعرفيتين، والوقتية من السبع الباقية، وأخص الكبريات السبع الوقتية؛ واختلاط الصغريين - أعني المشروطة الخاصة، والوقتية - مع الكبرى الوقتية غير منتج للاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج.
He said: As for the second figure, its condition regarding modality is two things: One of them is that perpetuity is true of the minor, or the major is among the propositions that have convertible negatives. The second: that the possible [premise] is not used except with absolute necessity or with the two conditioned major [premises]. I say: For the productiveness of the second figure regarding modality, two conditions are required, each of which is one of two things: The first: perpetuity is true of the minor – i.e., it is necessary or perpetual – or the major is among the six propositions that have convertible negatives. That is because if both were absent, the minor would be non-necessary and non-perpetual – which are eleven – and the major would be among the seven propositions that do not have convertible negatives; and the most specific of the minor [premises] are the conditioned particular and the temporal, because the conditioned particular is more specific than the conditioned general and the two perceptual [propositions], and the temporal is from the remaining seven; and the most specific of the major [premises] are the seven temporal [propositions]; and the mixing of the two minors – i.e., the conditioned particular and the temporal – with the temporal major is not productive due to the difference necessitating non-productiveness.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a mixed modal syllogism in the second figure to yield a conclusion. The author begins by quoting a statement (likely from the matn) that there are two conditions regarding modality in the second figure: (1) the minor must be perpetual (i.e., necessary or perpetual) or the major must be among propositions that have convertible negatives (i.e., universal negative propositions that can be converted simply); (2) the possible premise (i.e., contingent) must only be used with absolute necessity or with the two conditioned major premises (general conditioned and perceptual? or conditioned and perceptual? The text says 'الكبريين المشروطتين' which likely means two types of conditioned propositions). Then the commentator expands: he says the first condition is that either the minor is necessary/perpetual, or the major is among six types of propositions that have convertible negatives. The reason is that if neither holds, then the minor would be one of eleven non-necessary/non-perpetual types, and the major would be among seven types that do not convert. The most specific of those minor types are the conditioned particular and temporal, and the most specific of those major types are the seven temporal propositions. Mixing those two specific minors with the temporal major does not produce a conclusion because of the difference that necessitates non-productiveness. This is a detailed rule for modal syllogistics in the second figure.

Why Temporal Minors Require Convertible Majors in the Second Figure
Commentary explains why temporal minors with temporal majors fail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a temporal minor combined with a temporal major in the second figure does not produce a conclusion, based on the classification of modal propositions.

Core Idea
When the minor is temporal and the major is also temporal, the difference in temporal reference prevents conversion and thus prevents a conclusion.

The commentary on the first condition delves into the specific types of non-perpetual minors and non-convertible majors. The most restricted case is when both premises are temporal, which always fails. This lesson explores why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of non-perpetual minors and non-convertible majors
From the commentary, the non-perpetual minors are 11 types (including conditioned particular and temporal). The non-convertible majors are 7 types (including temporal). Understand that the most specific minors are conditioned particular and temporal, and the most specific majors are temporal.
The commentator says: 'وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية' and 'وأخص الكبريات السبع الوقتية'.
2
Recognize why mixing temporal with temporal fails
The commentator says: 'واختلاط الصغريين - أعني المشروطة الخاصة، والوقتية - مع الكبرى الوقتية غير منتج للاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج.' The 'difference' (الاختلاف) likely refers to the different temporal conditions: a conditioned particular may hold for a specific duration, while a temporal holds at a specific time; when both are temporal, the temporal reference may not align, preventing the conversion needed for the second figure.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises, and conversion is often required. If the major is temporal and does not convert simply (universal temporal does not convert because it is not universal perpetual?), the syllogism fails.
3
Grasp the logical principle behind the failure
The failure is due to 'modal difference' (اختلاف الجهة). For a second-figure syllogism to be productive, the major must be universal and convert. A temporal universal affirmative, e.g., 'Every A is B at time t', does not convert to 'Some B is A at time t' simply, and certainly a temporal negative may not convert. So when both premises are temporal, the necessary conversion is impossible.
This is a specific instance of the general rule that modal uniformity is needed for conversion in the second figure.

First Condition for Mixed Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author states the first productivity condition for mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the first condition for productive mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure (minor is necessary/perpetual or major is convertible) using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
For a second-figure mixed modal syllogism, either the minor must be perpetual or the major must be convertible.

In modal logic, the second figure requires special conditions when premises mix different modalities. The commentator Qutbi explains the first condition: either the minor premise is necessary (perpetually true) or the major premise belongs to the six types that allow universal negative conversion. This condition ensures the syllogism is productive (yields a valid conclusion). Let's unpack this rule and see why it is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternatives
The condition is an 'or': either the minor is necessary/perpetual (sidq al-dawam) or the major is among the six convertible propositions (al-munʿakisat al-sawālib).
2
Understand the rationale
If neither holds, the minor belongs to eleven non-necessary types and the major to seven non-convertible types. The most specific case (particular conditional minor + temporal major) is shown to be non-productive.
3
Analyze the example
The passage uses an astronomical example: 'Nothing that is eclipsed is bright necessarily during the eclipse' and 'Every moon is bright necessarily at a specific time' do not guarantee a conclusion; when the major is changed to 'Every sun is bright at a specific time', the conclusion fails.
4
Generalize the non-productivity
Since the most specific mixes fail, all less specific mixes also fail (by implication). This proves the condition is necessary.
5
Apply the condition
Test a given mixed syllogism in the second figure by checking whether the minor is necessary/perpetual or the major is convertible.
