شرائط انتاج المختلطات في الشكل الثاني
قال: وأما الشكل الثاني فشرطه بحسب الجهة أمران:
احدهما صدق الدوام على الصغرى أوكون الكبرى من القضايا
المنعكسة السوالب.
والثاني: أن لا تستعمل الممكنة إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة أو مع الكبريين
المشروطتين.
أقول: يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الجهة أمران، كل واحد منهما أحد الأمرين:
الأول: صدق الدوام على الصغرى - أي كونها ضرورية أو دائمة - أوكون الكبرى من القضايا الست المنعكسة السوالب؛ وذلك لأنه لوانتفيا لكانت الصغرى غير الضرورية والدائمة - وهي إحدى عشرة – والكبرى من القضايا السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب، وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والوقتية، لأن المشروطة الخاصة أخص من المشروطة العامة والعرفيتين، والوقتية من السبع الباقية، وأخص الكبريات السبع الوقتية؛ واختلاط الصغريين - أعني المشروطة الخاصة، والوقتية - مع الكبرى الوقتية غير منتج للاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج.
فإنه يصدق قولنا: «لاشيء من المنخسف بمضيء بالضرورة مادام منخسفاً - أو في وقت معين - لا دائماً»، و«كل قمر مضيء بالضرورة في وقت معين لادائماً مع امتناع السلب بالإمكان العام «الصدق كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة»، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: وكل شمس مضيئة في وقت معين لادائماً امتنع الإيجاب، ومتى لم ينتج هذان الاختلاطان لم ينتج سائر الاختلاطات لاستلزام عدم إنتاج الأخص عدم إنتاج الأعم.
Conditions for the productivity of mixed [syllogisms] in the second figure. He said: As for the second figure, its condition with respect to modality is two matters: One of them: the truth of perpetuity for the minor, or that the major is from the propositions that have universal negative conversion. And the second: that the possible not be used except with the absolute necessary or with the two conditioned [propositions] for the major. I say: The condition for productivity in the second figure with respect to modality is two matters, each one of them being one of two things: The first: The truth of perpetuity for the minor – i.e., it is necessary or perpetual – or that the major is from the six propositions that have universal negative conversion. This is because if both were absent, then the minor would be among the non-necessary and non-perpetual propositions – which are eleven – and the major would be among the seven propositions that do not have universal negative conversion. And the most specific of the minors are the particular conditional and the temporal, because the particular conditional is more specific than the general conditional and the two conventional [conditionals], and the temporal [is more specific] than the remaining seven. And the most specific of the majors are the seven temporal [propositions]. And the mixing of the two minors – namely the particular conditional and the temporal – with the temporal major is not productive due to the difference that necessitates non-productivity. For it is true that we say: "Nothing that is eclipsed is bright necessarily as long as it is eclipsed – or in a specific time – not perpetually," and "Every moon is bright necessarily in a specific time not perpetually," while forbidding the universal negative with general possibility, [so] "Every eclipsed [thing] is a moon necessarily" is true. And if we changed the major to our saying: "And every sun is bright in a specific time not perpetually," the affirmative would be prohibited. And whenever these two mixings do not produce, the rest of the mixings do not produce due to the implication that non-productivity of the more specific implies non-productivity of the more general.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the Shamsiyya, explaining the first condition for productive mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure. The author first cites the original text (قال) stating two conditions. In his own commentary (أقول), he focuses on the first condition: either the minor premise is necessary or perpetual (true continuously), or the major premise belongs to the six types that allow universal negative conversion. He explains that if neither condition holds, the minor is from the eleven non-necessary/non-perpetual types and the major from the seven non-converting types. By considering the most specific (أخص) cases—the particular conditional and temporal minors with a temporal major—he shows that such a mixing fails. The example involves astronomical phenomena: 'Nothing that is eclipsed is bright necessarily during the eclipse or at a specific time, not perpetually' and 'Every moon is bright necessarily at a specific time, not perpetually'; from these, the conclusion 'Every eclipsed thing is a moon' is necessarily true. But if the major is changed to 'Every sun is bright at a specific time not perpetually', the conclusion fails. Since the most specific mixings fail, all less specific ones also fail, because non-productivity of the specific entails non-productivity of the general.

Second Condition for Mixed Syllogisms: The Possible Premise
Author states the second condition: the possible premise must be paired with absolute necessary or conditioned majors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the permissible pairings of a possible proposition in the second figure of a mixed modal syllogism and explain the logical reasoning behind the restriction.

Core Idea
The possible minor cannot produce with perpetual majors because what is possible may never be actualized.

In this passage from the Shamsiyya commentary, the author completes the conditions for mixed syllogisms in the second figure, specifically for the possible (contingent) proposition. He explains why the possible minor must be paired only with absolute necessary or conditioned majors, and why pairing with perpetuals is sterile. We'll walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The condition splits into two: when the possible is the minor premise, and when it is the major. Focus on the minor case as argued in detail.
2
Allowed pairings for the possible minor
The text states it may be paired with the absolute necessary or the two conditioned (conventional) majors. These are the only majors that yield a valid conclusion.
The two conditioned majors are subtypes of perpetuals, but their conditionality allows the necessary minor to pin a perpetual predicate.
3
Why perpetuals are excluded
The author argues that mixing the possible minor with the perpetual major is sterile. Example: 'Every Roman is black possibly; no Roman is black perpetually' – no necessary conclusion follows because the possible may never be actualized.
Sterile means no necessary (universal positive or negative) conclusion can be drawn.
4
Generalize to the two conditioned majors
Since the mixing with the perpetual is sterile, and the two conditioned majors are also perpetual in their own way, their mixings are also sterile. Hence they are not allowed when the minor is possible.
5
Confirm the major case
When the possible is the major, only the absolute necessary is allowed. The reasoning is symmetric but not elaborated here.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما الأول فلأنه قد ظهر من الشرط الأوّل أن الممكنة الصغرى لا تنتج مع السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب لعدم صدق الدوام على الصغرى وعدم كون الكبرى من الست المنعكسة السوالب، فلو استعمل الممكنة الصغرى مع غير الضروريات الثلاث لكان اختلاطها مع الدوائم الثلاث - التي هي الدائمة والعرفيتان - لكن اختلاطها مع الدائمة عقيم، لجواز أن يكون الثابت لشيء بإمكان مسلوباً عنه دائماً، كقولنا: «كل رومي فهو أسود بالإمكان ولاشيء من الرومي بأسود دائماً مع امتناع سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: «لاشيء من التركي بأسود دائما امتنع الإيجاب.
ويلزم من عقم هذا الاختلاط عقم اختلاط الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفيتين.
And the second: that the possible not be used with the absolute necessary or with the two conditioned [propositions] for the major; and its gist is that the possible, if it is minor, is not used except with the absolute necessary or the two conditioned [propositions], and if it is major, it is not used except with the absolute necessary. As for the first [case]: because it has appeared from the first condition that the possible minor does not produce with the seven non-converting universals due to lack of truth of perpetuity for the minor and lack of the major being from the six converting universals. So if one used the possible minor with other than the three necessary [propositions], it would be its mixing with the three perpetuals – which are the perpetual and the two conventional [conditionals] – but its mixing with the perpetual is sterile, because it is possible that what is established for a thing as possible is negated from it perpetually, as in our saying: "Every Roman is black possibly, and nothing of the Roman is black perpetually," while forbidding the negation of a thing from itself. And if we changed the major to our saying: "Nothing of the Turk is black perpetually," the affirmative would be prohibited. And from the sterility of this mixing follows the sterility of mixing the possible minor with the two conventional [conditionals].

This passage completes the discussion of the second condition for mixed syllogisms in the second figure. Then he explains why the possible minor cannot be paired with the seven non-converting majors (from the first condition) and, against the remaining three perpetual majors (the perpetual and the two conventional conditionals), shows that the mixing with the perpetual is sterile. He gives an example: 'Every Roman is black possibly, and no Roman is black perpetually.' This does not yield a necessary conclusion. If the major is changed to 'No Turk is black perpetually,' it also fails. Since the mixing with the perpetual fails, the mixings with the two conventional conditionals (which are also perpetual in a sense) also fail.

Combining Possible Premises with Necessary Premises: The Rules
Author states the second condition for using possible premises with necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which major premises can combine with a possible minor premise in a categorical syllogism, and vice versa.

Core Idea
Possible premises can only pair with necessary premises in valid syllogisms.

In modal syllogistics, not every combination of premise modalities yields a valid conclusion. The author here sets out the second condition: a possible minor premise can only be used with an absolute necessary major or with one of the two conditional major premises. Conversely, a possible major premise can only be used with an absolute necessary minor. Understanding this condition is essential for constructing valid modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the author's opening statement: the possible minor is used only with absolute necessary or the two conditional majors; the possible major is used only with absolute necessary. Note the asymmetry.
This is the rule to be explained.
2
Distinguish possible minor vs. possible major
Recognize that the constraints differ: the possible minor has two allowed partner types (absolute necessary and conditional), while the possible major has only one (absolute necessary).
The reason will become clear from the analysis of sterility.
3
List the forbidden combinations
Which majors are forbidden with a possible minor? The author mentions the 'seven non-converse negative' and then specifies the three permanent types: permanent, general customary, special customary. List these.
These are the non-necessary premises.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete syllogism
Take an Islamic example: 'Every human is rational possibly. No human is rational necessarily absolutely.' Is this valid? Check the condition: possible minor with absolute necessary major is allowed, but this example uses a permanent major? Actually it is necessary absolute. So it would be allowed if the conclusion follows. For a valid combination, construct: 'Every animal is sensitive possibly (minor). Everything that is sensitive is animate necessarily absolutely (major).' This satisfies the condition.
Ensure the major is indeed absolute necessary, not permanent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : عدم استعمال الممكنة مع الضرورية المطلقة أو مع الكبريين المشروطتين؛ ومحصله أنّ الممكنة إن كانت صغرى لم تستعمل إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة أو المشروطتين، وإن كانت كبرى لم تستعمل إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة. أما الأول فلأنه قد ظهر من الشرط الأوّل أن الممكنة الصغرى لا تنتج مع السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب لعدم صدق الدوام على الصغرى وعدم كون الكبرى من الست المنعكسة السوالب، فلو استعمل الممكنة الصغرى مع غير الضروريات الثلاث لكان اختلاطها مع الدوائم الثلاث - التي هي الدائمة والعرفيتان - لكن اختلاطها مع الدائمة عقيم، لجواز أن يكون الثابت لشيء بإمكان مسلوباً عنه دائماً، كقولنا : «كل رومي فهو أسود بالإمكان ولاشيء من الرومي بأسود دائماً مع امتناع سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولوبدلنا الكبرى بقولنا : «لاشيء من التركي بأسود دائما امتنع الإيجاب. ويلزم من عقم هذا الاختلاط عقم اختلاط الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفيتين : أما مع العرفية العامة : فلأن الدائمة أخص وعقم الأخص يوجب عقم الأعم. وأما مع العرفية الخاصة فلعدم إنتاج العرفية العامة الممكنة إنتاج اللادوام أيضاً، لأن الأصل لما كان مخالفاً للممكنة في الكيف كان اللادوام موافقاً لها في الكيف، ولا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عن متفقين في الكيف، ومتى لم تنتج العرفية الخاصة مع الممكنة بجزأيها تكون العرفية الخاصة معها عقيمة، إذ المعني بإنتاج القضية المركبة مع قضية أخرى إنتاج أحد جزأيها معها، وبعدم إنتاجها عدم إنتاج جزأيها معها.
The second [condition]: not using the possible [premise] with the absolute necessary or with the two conditional major premises; and its summary is that if the possible is a minor premise, it is not used except with the absolute necessary or the two conditional ones; and if it is a major premise, it is not used except with the absolute necessary. As for the first [part]: because it has appeared from the first condition that the possible minor does not produce with the seven non-converse negative [propositions] because permanence does not apply to the minor and because the major is not among the six converse negatives. So if the possible minor is used with other than the three necessary [propositions], then its mixture would be with the three permanent [propositions]—which are the permanent and the two customary ones. But its mixture with the permanent is sterile, because it is possible that what is affirmed of a thing as possible is negated of it permanently, as in our saying: 'Every Roman is black possibly and no Roman is black permanently' with the impossibility of negating the thing from itself. And if we change the major to our saying: 'No Turk is black permanently', affirmation is prevented. And from the sterility of this mixture follows the sterility of the mixture of the possible minor with the two customary [propositions]: As for the general customary: because the permanent is more specific and the sterility of the more specific necessitates the sterility of the more general. And as for the special customary: because the general customary does not produce with the possible the production of the non-permanent also, because when the original [premise] opposed the possible in quality, the non-permanent agreed with it in quality, and there is no production in this figure from two [premises] agreeing in quality. And when the special customary does not produce with the possible in its two parts, the special customary is sterile with it, since the meaning of the production of a compound proposition with another proposition is the production of one of its parts with it, and by its non-production, the non-production of its two parts with it [follows].
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage discusses the second condition for mixing modal propositions in syllogisms, specifically regarding the possible (mumkina) premise. The author first states the condition: a possible minor premise can only be used with an absolute necessary premise or the two conditional major premises. Similarly, a possible major premise can only be used with an absolute necessary premise. Then the author explains why the possible minor fails with other premises. The key reason is that the possible minor cannot combine with permanent (da'ima) premises because it is possible for something that is possibly true to be permanently false. The author provides an example: 'Every Roman is black possibly' and 'No Roman is black permanently' leads to a contradiction when the same subject is used. Changing the major to 'No Turk is black permanently' also fails. From this sterility, the author deduces that the possible minor also fails with the two customary ('urfiyya) premises—general and special. The general customary is broader and inherits the sterility from the more specific permanent. The special customary fails because the general customary and the possible are opposite in quality (affirmative vs negative) and cannot produce in this figure. The author clarifies that when a compound premise is used, production means the production of one of its parts. If the compound premise does not produce with the possible, then both its parts fail.

Why the Possible Minor Fails with Permanent Premises
Author explains the sterility of combining possible minor with permanent premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical sterility that results from combining a possible minor premise with a permanent major premise, using the author's example.

Core Idea
Mixing possible and permanent premises creates logical sterility.

The author not only states the condition but also proves why certain combinations are sterile. Here we examine the argument that a possible minor with a permanent major leads to impossibility, using the example 'Every Roman is black possibly; no Roman is black permanently.' This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it extends to customary premises.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of sterility
A syllogism is sterile (ʿaqīm) when it does not produce a valid conclusion. The author claims that mixing a possible minor with a permanent major is sterile.
Sterility here means no conclusion follows necessarily.
2
Analyze the author's counterexample
Consider: 'Every Roman is black possibly' (minor) and 'No Roman is black permanently' (major). The author says this leads to 'the impossibility of negating the thing from itself'. This means that if a particular Roman is possibly black, it cannot simultaneously be permanently not black for the same subject. The conflict shows that the premises cannot both be true without contradiction, thus no valid conclusion can be drawn.
The example demonstrates modal incompatibility.
3
Extend to customary premises
The author argues that since the permanent is more specific than the general customary, and the more specific is sterile, the more general (general customary) must also be sterile. For the special customary, it is a compound proposition; if one part fails, the whole fails.
This is an argument by a fortiori and composition.
4
Formalize the logical structure
Represent the syllogism: Minor: Every R is possibly B (affirmative possible). Major: No R is B permanently (universal negative permanent). In the first figure, the minor is in subject position, major in predicate. The modal mismatch prevents a necessary conclusion. Contrast with a valid pairing: Minor: Every R is possibly B. Major: Every R is necessarily A (absolute necessary). Then a conclusion follows.
This prepares for understanding modal compatibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : عدم استعمال الممكنة مع الضرورية المطلقة أو مع الكبريين المشروطتين؛ ومحصله أنّ الممكنة إن كانت صغرى لم تستعمل إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة أو المشروطتين، وإن كانت كبرى لم تستعمل إلا مع الضرورية المطلقة. أما الأول فلأنه قد ظهر من الشرط الأوّل أن الممكنة الصغرى لا تنتج مع السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب لعدم صدق الدوام على الصغرى وعدم كون الكبرى من الست المنعكسة السوالب، فلو استعمل الممكنة الصغرى مع غير الضروريات الثلاث لكان اختلاطها مع الدوائم الثلاث - التي هي الدائمة والعرفيتان - لكن اختلاطها مع الدائمة عقيم، لجواز أن يكون الثابت لشيء بإمكان مسلوباً عنه دائماً، كقولنا : «كل رومي فهو أسود بالإمكان ولاشيء من الرومي بأسود دائماً مع امتناع سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ولوبدلنا الكبرى بقولنا : «لاشيء من التركي بأسود دائما امتنع الإيجاب. ويلزم من عقم هذا الاختلاط عقم اختلاط الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفيتين : أما مع العرفية العامة : فلأن الدائمة أخص وعقم الأخص يوجب عقم الأعم. وأما مع العرفية الخاصة فلعدم إنتاج العرفية العامة الممكنة إنتاج اللادوام أيضاً، لأن الأصل لما كان مخالفاً للممكنة في الكيف كان اللادوام موافقاً لها في الكيف، ولا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عن متفقين في الكيف، ومتى لم تنتج العرفية الخاصة مع الممكنة بجزأيها تكون العرفية الخاصة معها عقيمة، إذ المعني بإنتاج القضية المركبة مع قضية أخرى إنتاج أحد جزأيها معها، وبعدم إنتاجها عدم إنتاج جزأيها معها.
The second [condition]: not using the possible [premise] with the absolute necessary or with the two conditional major premises; and its summary is that if the possible is a minor premise, it is not used except with the absolute necessary or the two conditional ones; and if it is a major premise, it is not used except with the absolute necessary. As for the first [part]: because it has appeared from the first condition that the possible minor does not produce with the seven non-converse negative [propositions] because permanence does not apply to the minor and because the major is not among the six converse negatives. So if the possible minor is used with other than the three necessary [propositions], then its mixture would be with the three permanent [propositions]—which are the permanent and the two customary ones. But its mixture with the permanent is sterile, because it is possible that what is affirmed of a thing as possible is negated of it permanently, as in our saying: 'Every Roman is black possibly and no Roman is black permanently' with the impossibility of negating the thing from itself. And if we change the major to our saying: 'No Turk is black permanently', affirmation is prevented. And from the sterility of this mixture follows the sterility of the mixture of the possible minor with the two customary [propositions]: As for the general customary: because the permanent is more specific and the sterility of the more specific necessitates the sterility of the more general. And as for the special customary: because the general customary does not produce with the possible the production of the non-permanent also, because when the original [premise] opposed the possible in quality, the non-permanent agreed with it in quality, and there is no production in this figure from two [premises] agreeing in quality. And when the special customary does not produce with the possible in its two parts, the special customary is sterile with it, since the meaning of the production of a compound proposition with another proposition is the production of one of its parts with it, and by its non-production, the non-production of its two parts with it [follows].
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage discusses the second condition for mixing modal propositions in syllogisms, specifically regarding the possible (mumkina) premise. The author first states the condition: a possible minor premise can only be used with an absolute necessary premise or the two conditional major premises. Similarly, a possible major premise can only be used with an absolute necessary premise. Then the author explains why the possible minor fails with other premises. The key reason is that the possible minor cannot combine with permanent (da'ima) premises because it is possible for something that is possibly true to be permanently false. The author provides an example: 'Every Roman is black possibly' and 'No Roman is black permanently' leads to a contradiction when the same subject is used. Changing the major to 'No Turk is black permanently' also fails. From this sterility, the author deduces that the possible minor also fails with the two customary ('urfiyya) premises—general and special. The general customary is broader and inherits the sterility from the more specific permanent. The special customary fails because the general customary and the possible are opposite in quality (affirmative vs negative) and cannot produce in this figure. The author clarifies that when a compound premise is used, production means the production of one of its parts. If the compound premise does not produce with the possible, then both its parts fail.

Key Terms
ممكنة
دائمة
عرفية عامة
عرفية خاصة

C) The major premise is false.
D) The minor premise is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A possible minor paired with a permanent major is sterile because the possibility of one attribute can coexist with the permanent negation of that same attribute, preventing a necessary deduction.

Counting Syllogisms from Simple and Compound Premises
Author states the rule for counting syllogisms when premises are simple or compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of syllogisms given simple and compound premises according to the rule of the logicians.

Core Idea
Compound premises multiply syllogisms: 1, 2, or 4.

When premises contain modal or temporal qualifications (compound premises), they effectively split into multiple simple premises, each requiring its own syllogism. This rule helps you quickly determine how many syllogisms a given pair of premises yields and whether the conclusion will be simple or compound.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
Determine whether each premise is simple (basīṭa) or compound (murakkaba). A simple premise is a categorical without modal or temporal qualification; a compound premise includes a condition like 'if...then' or 'always'.
In Islamic logic, temporal or modal qualifications make a premise compound.
2
Apply the counting rule
Use the rule: two simples → 1 syllogism; one simple + one compound → 2 syllogisms; two compounds → 4 syllogisms.
This is a mnemonic anchor for quick calculation.
3
Determine the conclusion type
If only one syllogism is produced (from two simples), the conclusion is a single simple proposition. If multiple syllogisms, the conclusions are combined (compounded) into a single compound conclusion.
The conclusion may be a conjunction or condition depending on the premises.
4
Practice with an example
Take an actual pair of premises from Kalam or Fiqh, classify them as simple or compound, and apply the rule to predict the number of syllogisms and the nature of the conclusion.
This solidifies the rule through application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هاهنا تسمعهم يقولون : القياس من بسيطتين قياس واحد، ومن مركبة وبسيطة قياسان ومن مركبتين أربعة أقيسة، فإن كان المنتج منها قياساً واحداً كان نتيجة القياس بسيطة، وإلا ركبت النتائج وجعلت نتيجة القياس.
And from here you hear them saying: the syllogism from two simple [premises] is one syllogism, and from a compound and a simple are two syllogisms, and from two compounds are four syllogisms. If what is produced from them is one syllogism, then the conclusion of the syllogism is simple; otherwise, the conclusions are compounded and made the conclusion of the syllogism.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This short passage gives a practical rule for counting syllogisms when premises may be compound (i.e., having temporal or modal qualifications). The rule is: if both premises are simple (unmodalized categoricals), the syllogism is one and yields one simple conclusion. If one premise is compound (e.g., a conditional or modal) and the other is simple, then the syllogism effectively counts as two, because the compound premise must be split and each part combined with the simple premise yields a separate syllogism. Similarly, if both premises are compound, we get four syllogisms. The final conclusion is formed by combining the individual conclusions.

Key Terms

مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The number of syllogisms from a pair of premises is determined solely by whether each premise is simple or compound: 1 from two simples, 2 from one compound, 4 from two compounds.

Condition for Using Possible Major Premise with Absolute Necessary
Author explains why possible major premise must combine only with absolute necessary premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a possible major premise can be validly combined in a categorical syllogism and explain why it must be with an absolute necessary premise.

Core Idea
A possible major premise only yields a valid syllogism when paired with an absolute necessary premise.

This lesson explains a crucial restriction in modal syllogistics: if the major premise is a possible proposition, it cannot be combined with just any minor—it requires a minor that is absolutely necessary. Understanding this condition prevents invalid inferences in arguments about possibility and necessity, which are common in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The author states that when the major premise is possible (mumkina), it must be paired with a minor that is absolutely necessary (ḍarūra muṭlaqa). This is the second condition in the discussion.
Absolute necessity means the proposition is true at all times, not just during the subject's existence.
2
Identify what is excluded
The possible major cannot be used with a non-necessary premise, especially a permanent (dāʾima) premise, because permanence is not strong enough to guarantee the required relationship.
The author calls such a combination 'sterile' (ʿaqīma) because it does not produce a valid conclusion.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example: 'Every Roman is white permanently' (permanent minor) and 'No Roman is white possibly' (possible major). The author says this is non-productive because it is possible that what is negated as possible is actually permanently true—i.e., something can be permanently true yet also possibly false? The reasoning is subtle: the minor's permanence does not entail necessity, so the combination fails.
The example uses 'Roman' and 'Indian', but the same logical structure applies to any subject.
4
Generalize the condition
The condition ensures that the minor premise provides the necessity required by the major. If the minor is not absolutely necessary, the syllogism cannot produce a certain conclusion. This is a specific rule in the logic of modal syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني - وهو أن الممكنة إذا كانت كبرى لم تستعمل إلا مع الضرورة المطلقة - فإنه قد تبين من الشرط الأول أن الممكنة الكبرى مع غير الضرورية والدائمة عقيمة، لعدم صدق الدوام على الصغرى وعدم كون الكبرى من القضايا الست فلو استعملت الممكنة الكبرى مع غير الضرورية لكان اختلاطها مع الدائمة، وهو غير منتج، الجواز أن يكون المسلوب عن الشيء بالإمكان ثابتاً له دائماً؛ كقولنا : «كل رومي أبيض دائماً، ولا شيء من الرومي بأبيض بالإمكان» مع امتناع السلب؛ ولوقلنا بدل الكبرى : ولا شيء من الهندي بأبيض بالإمكان امتنع الإيجاب.
As for the second [part]—that if the possible is a major premise, it is not used except with the absolute necessity—it has become clear from the first condition that the possible major with other than the necessary and the permanent is sterile, because permanence does not apply to the minor and because the major is not among the six propositions. So if the possible major is used with other than the necessary, then its mixture would be with the permanent, and it is non-productive, because it is possible that what is negated of a thing as possible is affirmed of it permanently; as in our saying: 'Every Roman is white permanently, and no Roman is white possibly' with the impossibility of negation; and if we change the major to: 'No Indian is white possibly', affirmation is prevented.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion of the second condition by focusing on the case when the possible proposition serves as the major premise. The author reiterates that a possible major can only be used with an absolute necessary premise. The reason is that combining a possible major with a permanent premise leads to sterility. The example given is: 'Every Roman is white permanently' (permanent minor) and 'No Roman is white possibly' (possible major). This combination is problematic because it is possible that something that is permanently true is also possibly false. Changing the major to 'No Indian is white possibly' also fails. Thus, the possible major must be paired with a necessary absolute premise to avoid contradiction.

Key Terms
ممكنة (mumkina): A modal proposition expressing possibility; here as major premise.
ضرورة مطلقة (ḍarūra muṭlaqa): Absolute necessary proposition, true at all times.
دائمة (dāʾima): Permanent proposition, true throughout the subject's existence.

A possible major premise must be paired with an absolute necessary premise; combining it with a permanent or other non-necessary premise renders the syllogism sterile.

Conditions for Productive Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains conditions for productive mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a mixed modal syllogism in the second figure is productive and state the condition for using the possible premise as major.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the possible major requires absolute necessity as its partner.

Mixed modal syllogisms combine premises with different modal qualifiers like 'possible' and 'perpetual'. This lesson focuses on the second figure, where the author explains which combinations yield a conclusion and which are barren. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid arguments in kalam and fiqh, where modal distinctions matter.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish absolute universal from absolute particular
Identify the two types of absolute propositions: universal (al-'urfiyya al-'āmma) and particular (al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa). The author first discusses mixing each with the possible (al-mumkina).
Absolute here means without temporal qualification, i.e., 'at all times' for universal.
2
Analyze why perpetual causes barrenness
The perpetual (al-dā'ima) is more specific than the absolute universal. If the perpetual is barren (unproductive) with a premise, then the more general absolute universal must also be barren. This is a key lemma.
Barren means no conclusion follows.
3
Examine the absolute particular with the possible
The absolute particular does not produce the non-perpetual (al-lā-dawām) with the possible because they agree in quality (both negative/positive?). The author states that if two premises agree in quality in this figure, no conclusion follows.
Check the original: 'agreement in quality' means both affirmative or both negative.
4
Understand the condition for the possible as major
The possible major premise can only be used with absolute necessity (al-ḍarūra al-muṭlaqa). If paired with non-necessary or perpetual, the syllogism is barren. The example with 'Roman' and 'Indian' illustrates why.
The example shows that 'No Roman is white by possibility' contradicts 'Every Roman is white perpetually'.
5
Apply the concept of composite propositions
A premise can be composite (e.g., a disjunction). The author notes that a syllogism with one composite and one simple gives two syllogisms; with two composites, four. This is used to count productive mixtures later.
This anticipates the enumeration in the next lesson.

المختلطات
أما مع العرفية العامة : فلأن الدائمة أخص وعقم الأخص يوجب عقم الأعم.
مع وعدم وأما مع العرفية الخاصة فلعدم إنتاج العرفية العامة الممكنة إنتاج اللادوام أيضاً، لأن الأصل لما كان مخالفاً للممكنة في الكيف كان اللادوام موافقاً لها في الكيف، ولا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عن متفقين في الكيف، ومتى لم تنتج العرفية الخاصة مع الممكنة بجزأيها تكون العرفية الخاصة معها عقيمة، إذ المعني بإنتاج القضية المركبة مع قضية أخرى إنتاج أحد جزأيها معها، وبعدم إنتاجها عدم إنتاج جزأيها معها.
ومن هاهنا تسمعهم يقولون : القياس من بسيطتين قياس واحد، ومن مركبة وبسيطة قياسان ومن مركبتين أربعة أقيسة، فإن كان المنتج منها قياساً واحداً كان نتيجة القياس بسيطة، وإلا ركبت النتائج وجعلت نتيجة القياس.

جهة النتائج في الشكل الثاني]
لخال: والنتيجة دائمة إن صدق الدوام على إحدى مقدمتية، وإلا فكالصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللادوام واللاضرورة والضرورة - آية ضرورة كانت.
القول: الاختلاطات المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب مقتضى الشرطين اختلاطاً - وهي أربعة وثمانون، لأن الشرط الأوّل أسقط سبعة وسبعين اختلاطاً - الحاصلة من ضرب إحدى عشرة صغرى في سبع كبريات - والشرط الثاني أسقط لمانية - الممكنتين الصغرى مع الدائمة والعرفيتين، والكبرى الدائمة.
مع والضابط في إنتاجها أنّ الدوام إما أن يصدق على إحدى مقدمتيه بأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو لا يصدق، فإن صدق الدوام على إحدى المقدمتين فالنتيجة دائمة، وإلا فالنتيجة كالصغرى بشرط حذف قيدي الوجود – أي اللادوام واللاضرورة - منها وحذف الضرورة منها - سواء كانت وصفية أو وقتية.

Mixed Modalities. As for absolute universal: the perpetual is more specific and the barrenness of the more specific necessitates the barrenness of the more general. As for absolute particular: the absolute particular does not produce with the possible the non-perpetual also, because the original, since it differed from the possible in quality, the non-perpetual agrees with it in quality, and there is no production in this figure from two agreeing in quality. Whenever the absolute particular does not produce with the possible in its two parts, the absolute particular with it is barren, for what is meant by the production of a composite proposition with another proposition is the production of one of its two parts with it, and by its non-production is the non-production of its two parts with it. Hence you hear them say: a syllogism from two simple propositions is one syllogism, from a composite and a simple is two syllogisms, and from two composites is four syllogisms. If what is produced from them is one syllogism, then the conclusion of the syllogism is simple; otherwise, the conclusions are combined and made the conclusion of the syllogism. As for the second condition—that when the possible is major, it is only used with absolute necessity—it has been shown from the first condition that the possible major with non-necessary and perpetual is barren, because perpetual does not hold of the minor and because the major is not among the six propositions. If the possible major were used with non-necessary, then its mixture with the perpetual would be unproductive, because it is permissible for what is negated of a thing by possibility to be affirmed of it perpetually; as we say: 'Every Roman is white perpetually, and no Roman is white by possibility,' while the negation is impossible; and if we said instead of the major: 'No Indian is white by possibility,' the affirmation would be impossible.

The Modality of Conclusions in the Second Figure. The conclusion is perpetual if perpetual holds of one of its premises; otherwise, it is like the minor, with the non-perpetual and non-necessary and the necessity removed—whether it is a necessity.
Statement: The productive mixtures in this figure according to the two conditions are eighty-four mixtures, because the first condition eliminates seventy-seven mixtures—resulting from multiplying eleven minors by seven majors—and the second condition eliminates eight—the two possible minors with the perpetual and the two absolute particulars, and the perpetual major. The rule for its production is that perpetual either holds of one of its premises—i.e., it is necessary or perpetual—or it does not hold. If perpetual holds of one of the premises, then the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, the conclusion is like the minor, subject to the removal of the two temporal qualifiers—namely non-perpetual and non-necessary—and removal of necessity from it—whether it is descriptive or temporal. As for whether the conclusion is like the perpetual premise or like the minor, this is by the proofs mentioned in the absolute propositions from contradiction, conversion, and supposition. For example: if 'Every C is B absolutely' holds and 'No B is A necessarily or perpetually', then 'No C is A perpetually'; otherwise, 'Some C is A absolutely' and we make it the minor of the major premise of the syllogism thus: 'Some C is A absolutely' and 'No A is B' [text cut off].
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure, from a commentary on the Shamsiyya. It begins by distinguishing two cases: the absolute universal (العرفية العامة) and the absolute particular (العرفية الخاصة) when mixed with the possible (الممكنة). For the absolute universal, since the perpetual (الدائمة) is more specific, if the perpetual is barren (i.e., unproductive), then the more general absolute universal must also be barren. For the absolute particular, it does not produce the non-perpetual (اللادوام) with the possible because they agree in quality (both negative? Actually the text says the original (the major?) differed from the possible in quality, but the non-perpetual agrees in quality, and no production occurs from two agreeing in quality in this figure). Then it explains the concept of composite propositions: a syllogism with two simple premises is one syllogism; with one composite and one simple, it yields two syllogisms (because a composite proposition has two parts, each combined with the simple); with two composites, it yields four syllogisms. The conclusion is simple if only one of these yields a result; otherwise the conclusions are combined. Next, the second condition: the possible as major premise is only used with absolute necessity (الضرورة المطلقة). The first condition already showed that the possible major with non-necessary or perpetual is barren. The author gives an example to illustrate: 'Every Roman is white perpetually, and no Roman is white by possibility' is impossible because the negation is impossible; replacing 'Roman' with 'Indian' makes the affirmation impossible. Then the text transitions to the modality of conclusions in the second figure. The overall productive mixtures according to the two conditions are eighty-four: the first condition eliminates seventy-seven from the total of eleven minors times seven majors, and the second eliminates eight more (the possible minor with perpetual, the absolute particular with perpetual, and the perpetual major). The rule: if perpetual (i.e., necessity or perpetuity) holds of one premise, the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, the conclusion is like the minor after removing the temporal qualifiers (non-perpetual, non-necessary) and necessity. This is proven by the methods of contradiction, conversion, and supposition, as used for absolute propositions. A concrete example is given: if 'Every C is B absolutely' and 'No B is A necessarily or perpetually', then 'No C is A perpetually'; otherwise, we get 'Some C is A absolutely'. This exemplifies the rule.

الضرورة المطلقة

Modality of Conclusions and Counting Productive Mixtures
Author explains the modality of conclusions and counts productive mixtures in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a second figure mixed syllogism and calculate the number of productive mixtures.

Core Idea
The conclusion in the second figure is perpetual if one premise is perpetual; otherwise it is like the minor stripped of non-perpetual and non-necessary.

Now that we know which modal combinations are productive, we need to know what conclusion they yield. The author gives a clear rule: if one premise is perpetual (either necessary or perpetual), the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, it is like the minor but with temporal qualifiers removed. He also counts exactly eighty-four productive mixtures. This lesson unpacks that rule and count.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for conclusion modality
If perpetual (i.e., necessity or perpetual) holds of one premise, the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, the conclusion is like the minor premise after removing non-perpetual (al-lā-dawām) and non-necessary (al-lā-ḍarūra) and also removing necessity qualifiers.
The conclusion mirrors the minor in type but without temporal restrictions.
2
Understand the removal of qualifiers
The minor premise may have qualifiers like 'non-perpetual' or 'non-necessary'. These are stripped off, and the conclusion becomes absolute in a sense. For example, if the minor is 'Some C is A non-perpetually', the conclusion becomes 'Some C is A' absolutely.
'Non-perpetual' and 'non-necessary' are removed; necessity qualifiers (whether descriptive or temporal) are also removed.
3
Count the productive mixtures
The total possible mixtures are 11 minors × 7 majors = 77. The first condition eliminates 77? No, the text says the first condition eliminates 77, leaving 84? Wait: 'the first condition eliminates seventy-seven mixtures—resulting from multiplying eleven minors by seven majors—and the second condition eliminates eight.' So initially there are 11×7=77 combinations? Actually then it says 84 productive mixtures, which seems contradictory. Let's parse: The total possible mixtures from 11 minors and 7 majors is 77. The first condition eliminates 77, so none left? That can't be. Perhaps the total is 11×7=77, and the first condition eliminates those that are not productive, leaving 84? That is impossible. I think there is a misreading. The text: 'the first condition eliminates seventy-seven mixtures—resulting from multiplying eleven minors by seven majors—and the second condition eliminates eight.' So maybe there are 77+8=85? But then 84 productive? Possibly the total possible mixtures are 11×7=77, and after applying both conditions, 84 are productive? That doesn't add up. I suspect the numbers refer to different things: perhaps the first condition eliminates 77 from some larger set? The passage might be describing that the first condition eliminates 77 out of 11×7=77? That would mean all are eliminated, which is absurd. Actually, it might say that the first condition eliminates 77, and the second eliminates 8, leaving 84 productive? But 77-8=69, not 84. There is likely a misunderstanding. Possibly the total number of possible mixed syllogisms before conditions is larger. For the purpose of this guided path, we can simply state the rule without the count, or mention that the count is given but not fully clear without context. Alternatively, we can interpret: The first condition eliminates 77 from the full set of (something) and the second eliminates 8, leaving 84 productive. We'll just present the rule and example.
The exact count may be debated; focus on the logical rule.
4
Analyze the example with variables
The author gives an example: if 'Every C is B absolutely' and 'No B is A necessarily or perpetually', then 'No C is A perpetually'. This illustrates the rule: since the major is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual. Also, the converse is shown: if the major is not perpetual, the conclusion is like the minor after removing temporal qualifiers.
The variables ج، ب، أ correspond to C, B, A.
5
Apply the proof methods
The rule is proven by the methods of contradiction (khalaf), conversion ('aks), and supposition (iftirāḍ), as used in the earlier section on absolute propositions. These are standard tools in Aristotelian logic.
We won't detail the proofs here, but note they are used.

لخال: والنتيجة دائمة إن صدق الدوام على إحدى مقدمتية، وإلا فكالصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللادوام واللاضرورة والضرورة - آية ضرورة كانت.

Mixed Modalities. As for absolute universal: the perpetual is more specific and the barrenness of the more specific necessitates the barrenness of the more general. As for absolute particular: the absolute particular does not produce with the possible the non-perpetual also, because the original, since it differed from the possible in quality, the non-perpetual agrees with it in quality, and there is no production in this figure from two agreeing in quality. Whenever the absolute particular does not produce with the possible in its two parts, the absolute particular with it is barren, for what is meant by the production of a composite proposition with another proposition is the production of one of its two parts with it, and by its non-production is the non-production of its two parts with it. Hence you hear them say: a syllogism from two simple propositions is one syllogism, from a composite and a simple is two syllogisms, and from two composites is four syllogisms. If what is produced from them is one syllogism, then the conclusion of the syllogism is simple; otherwise, the conclusions are combined and made the conclusion of the syllogism. As for the second condition—that when the possible is major, it is only used with absolute necessity—it has been shown from the first condition that the possible major with non-necessary and perpetual is barren, because perpetual does not hold of the minor and because the major is not among the six propositions. If the possible major were used with non-necessary, then its mixture with the perpetual would be unproductive, because it is permissible for what is negated of a thing by possibility to be affirmed of it perpetually; as we say: 'Every Roman is white perpetually, and no Roman is white by possibility,' while the negation is impossible; and if we said instead of the major: 'No Indian is white by possibility,' the affirmation would be impossible.

The Modality of Conclusions in the Second Figure. The conclusion is perpetual if perpetual holds of one of its premises; otherwise, it is like the minor, with the non-perpetual and non-necessary and the necessity removed—whether it is a necessity.
Statement: The productive mixtures in this figure according to the two conditions are eighty-four mixtures, because the first condition eliminates seventy-seven mixtures—resulting from multiplying eleven minors by seven majors—and the second condition eliminates eight—the two possible minors with the perpetual and the two absolute particulars, and the perpetual major. The rule for its production is that perpetual either holds of one of its premises—i.e., it is necessary or perpetual—or it does not hold. If perpetual holds of one of the premises, then the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, the conclusion is like the minor, subject to the removal of the two temporal qualifiers—namely non-perpetual and non-necessary—and removal of necessity from it—whether it is descriptive or temporal. As for whether the conclusion is like the perpetual premise or like the minor, this is by the proofs mentioned in the absolute propositions from contradiction, conversion, and supposition. For example: if 'Every C is B absolutely' holds and 'No B is A necessarily or perpetually', then 'No C is A perpetually'; otherwise, 'Some C is A absolutely' and we make it the minor of the major premise of the syllogism thus: 'Some C is A absolutely' and 'No A is B' [text cut off].

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure, from a commentary on the Shamsiyya. It begins by distinguishing two cases: the absolute universal (العرفية العامة) and the absolute particular (العرفية الخاصة) when mixed with the possible (الممكنة). For the absolute universal, since the perpetual (الدائمة) is more specific, if the perpetual is barren (i.e., unproductive), then the more general absolute universal must also be barren. For the absolute particular, it does not produce the non-perpetual (اللادوام) with the possible because they agree in quality (both negative? Actually the text says the original (the major?) differed from the possible in quality, but the non-perpetual agrees in quality, and no production occurs from two agreeing in quality in this figure). Then it explains the concept of composite propositions: a syllogism with two simple premises is one syllogism; with one composite and one simple, it yields two syllogisms (because a composite proposition has two parts, each combined with the simple); with two composites, it yields four syllogisms. The conclusion is simple if only one of these yields a result; otherwise the conclusions are combined. Next, the second condition: the possible as major premise is only used with absolute necessity (الضرورة المطلقة). The first condition already showed that the possible major with non-necessary or perpetual is barren. The author gives an example to illustrate: 'Every Roman is white perpetually, and no Roman is white by possibility' is impossible because the negation is impossible; replacing 'Roman' with 'Indian' makes the affirmation impossible. Then the text transitions to the modality of conclusions in the second figure. The overall productive mixtures according to the two conditions are eighty-four: the first condition eliminates seventy-seven from the total of eleven minors times seven majors, and the second eliminates eight more (the possible minor with perpetual, the absolute particular with perpetual, and the perpetual major). The rule: if perpetual (i.e., necessity or perpetuity) holds of one premise, the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, the conclusion is like the minor after removing the temporal qualifiers (non-perpetual, non-necessary) and necessity. This is proven by the methods of contradiction, conversion, and supposition, as used for absolute propositions. A concrete example is given: if 'Every C is B absolutely' and 'No B is A necessarily or perpetually', then 'No C is A perpetually'; otherwise, we get 'Some C is A absolutely'. This exemplifies the rule.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Modality of Conclusions in the Second Figure
Author explains rules for conclusion modality in mixed modal syllogisms of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a second-figure mixed modal syllogism and explain why two necessary premises do not guarantee a necessary conclusion when descriptive predication is involved.

Core Idea
Conclusion modality in the second figure hinges on perpetuity and the distinction between essential and descriptive necessity.

In the second figure, the modality of the conclusion is not simply the weakest or strongest premise; it follows specific rules based on perpetuity and existence conditions. This lesson unpacks the author's rules and the subtle objection about necessity, using the classic example of C, B, A. Pay close attention to how the author proves the rule and why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise modalities
Read the premises and classify their modalities: necessary (ضرورية), perpetual (دائمة), absolute (مطلقة), possible (ممكنة), or customary (عرفية). In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises.
2
Apply the perpetuity rule
If one premise is perpetual (i.e., necessary or perpetual), the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, the conclusion matches the minor premise after removing any existence conditions (non-perpetuity, non-necessity) and necessity.
Perpetuity means the proposition holds at all times.
3
Trace the proof by contradiction
Consider the example: 'Every C is B absolutely' and 'Nothing of A is B necessarily/perpetually'. Assume the opposite of the desired conclusion ('Some C is A absolutely'), combine with the major, and derive a contradiction (some C is not B while all C is B).
Also note the conversion proof that yields a perpetual conclusion.
4
Examine the necessity objection
An objector claims that if both premises are necessary, the conclusion must be necessary. The author refutes this by distinguishing essential necessity from descriptive necessity.
The donkey/horse example shows that a description (ridden by Zayd) can be contingent even when essences are necessarily separate.

The modality of conclusions in the second figure: The conclusion is perpetual if perpetuity applies to one of its premises, otherwise it is like the minor premise with the removal of non-perpetuity and non-necessity and necessity - whether necessity existed. Statement: The productive mixtures in this figure according to the requirement of the two conditions are eighty-four mixtures, because the first condition eliminates seventy-seven mixtures resulting from multiplying eleven minors by seven majors, and the second condition eliminates eight: the possible minor with the perpetual and the two customary, and the perpetual major. The rule for their production is that perpetuity either applies to one of its premises such that it is necessary or perpetual, or does not apply. If perpetuity applies to one of the two premises, the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise the conclusion is like the minor premise with the condition of deleting the two existence conditions – i.e., non-perpetuity and non-necessity – from it and deleting necessity from it, whether it is descriptive or temporal. As for whether the conclusion is like the perpetual premise or like the minor premise, it is by the proofs mentioned in the absolute propositions from contradiction, conversion, and assumption. For example, if 'Every C is B' is true absolutely and 'Nothing of A is B' is true necessarily or perpetually, then 'Nothing of C is A' is perpetual; otherwise, 'Some C is A' is absolute, and we make it a minor premise for a major premise of the syllogism thus: 'Some C is A absolutely and nothing of A is B necessarily or perpetually' yields from the first 'Some C is not B necessarily or perpetually' while it was 'Every C is B absolutely' – this is a contradiction. Or by converting the major to 'Nothing of B is A perpetually' yields the desired conclusion. From here it appears that if the negative necessary converted as itself, it would produce the necessary in this figure as necessary; but since that was not demonstrated, the conclusion is restricted to perpetuity. It should not be said: If both premises are necessary, then the conclusion must be necessary, because the middle term, if it is necessarily affirmative for one extreme and necessarily negative for the other, then one extreme is necessarily negative of the other, so there is necessary separation between the two extremes, so the conclusion of the two extremes is necessary. For we say: The judgment in the two premises is not that the middle term is necessarily affirmative for the essence of one extreme and necessarily negative for the essence of the other; the necessary consequence is that the essence of one extreme is necessarily negative of the essence of the other, but that is not sought; rather what is sought is that a description of one extreme is necessarily negative of the essence of the other. And the necessity of the negation of the essence does not entail the necessity of the negation of the description, due to the truth of our statement in the famous example: 'Nothing of the donkey is a horse by necessity, and every ridden of Zayd is a horse by necessity' while our statement 'Some donkey is not ridden of Zayd by necessity' is false, because every donkey is possibly ridden of Zayd. As for deleting the two existence conditions from the minor: because if it is with a simple major, its existence condition agrees with it in quality; if it is with a composite, it does not produce with its original – as we mentioned – nor with its existence condition, because the two
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for determining the modality of conclusions in the second figure of mixed modal syllogisms. The author begins by stating the general rule: if one premise has perpetual truth (does not change over time), the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise but with the removal of the conditions of existence (i.e., non-perpetuity and non-necessity) and necessity. Then he notes that there are 84 productive mixtures in the second figure, and two conditions eliminate 77+8 = 85? Actually 77 eliminated by first condition, 8 by second, total 85? But the text says the productive mixtures are 84, so the total eliminated must be such that the original number minus eliminated equals 84. The original number from multiplying 11 minors by 7 majors is 77? That seems off; perhaps the text means that after the first condition, 77 are eliminated, leaving some number, then the second condition eliminates 8 more to reach 84 productive. The exact arithmetic is not our focus; the point is that many combinations are invalid. The author then provides the detailed rule about perpetuity and gives an example using letters (ج, ب, أ): If 'Every C is B' is absolute and 'Nothing of A is B' is necessary or perpetual, then 'Nothing of C is A' is perpetual. He proves this by contradiction: assuming the opposite, that some C is A, then combining with the major yields a falsehood. Alternatively, by converting the major, we get the same conclusion. He notes that if the necessary negative converted as itself (i.e., if conversion preserved necessity), then the conclusion would be necessary, but that is not proven, so the conclusion is only perpetual. Then follows an objection: if both premises are necessary, the conclusion should be necessary because the middle term is necessarily related to both extremes, so the extremes must be necessarily separate. The response clarifies that the premises do not state the middle is necessary for the essence of the extremes; rather, the necessary relation is between the middle and a description of one extreme. For example, 'Nothing of the donkey is a horse by necessity' is about the essence of donkey and horse, while 'Every ridden of Zayd is a horse by necessity' is about a description (ridden by Zayd) and horse. The necessary negation between donkey and horse does not entail necessary negation between the description of donkey (being ridden by Zayd) and horse, because donkeys may possibly be ridden by Zayd. Thus, the conclusion need not be necessary. Finally, the author explains why existence conditions are deleted from the minor premise: if the major is simple (unqualified), the existence condition of the minor agrees with it; if the major is composite, the minor with its existence condition does not yield a valid syllogism. This passage illustrates the subtlety of modal logic in distinguishing between essential and descriptive predication.

When Both Premises Are Necessary: The Essence vs. Description Distinction
Author addresses an objection about necessary premises and necessary conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessity of essence (dhat) and necessity of description (wasf) in evaluating whether a modal categorical syllogism yields a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Necessity of essences does not automatically necessitate attributes – always distinguish dhat from wasf.

In logic, a common objection is that if both premises of a syllogism are necessary, then the conclusion must be necessary. The author of this passage counters that claim by drawing a subtle distinction: the necessity may belong to the essence of a thing, not to its accidental descriptions. Using the famous example of donkey, horse, and 'ridden by Zayd', he shows that from necessary premises about essences, a necessary conclusion about a description does not follow. This dialectical exchange sharpens our understanding of modal syllogisms and is a crucial tool for rigorous reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection (لا يقال)
Find the objector's claim in the Arabic: he argues that if the middle term is necessarily affirmed of one extreme and necessarily denied of the other, then the extremes must be necessarily separated. The objector assumes the necessity concerns the essences of the extremes.
The objector appears to follow a simple transfer of necessity across the syllogism.
2
Extract the Author's Response (لانا نقول)
Locate where the author responds by distinguishing between the middle term's relation to the essence (dhat) versus the description (wasf) of the extremes. He says the judgment in the premises is not about essence, so the necessity of essence does not transfer to description.
This is the key logical move: necessity of dhat ≠ necessity of wasf.
3
Analyze the Counterexample
Examine the example: 'No donkey is a horse necessarily' and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse necessarily' are both true and necessary. But 'Some donkey is not ridden by Zayd necessarily' is false because a donkey can be ridden by Zayd possibly. Understand that the description 'ridden by Zayd' is not essential to the donkey.
The conclusion would be necessary only if the description were essential, but it is not.
4
Connect to the Mixed Modal Syllogism Teaching
Earlier, the author stated that for mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion is only 'always' (daim), not 'necessary' (daruri). This passage clarifies why: the conversion of necessary negatives does not preserve necessity, and the essence/description distinction blocks the objection.
This explains the limitation Aristotle noted about modal syllogisms – necessity does not always transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختلطات من (اب) بالضرورة أو دائماً ينتج من الأول بعض (ج) ليس (ب) بالضرورة أو دائماً وقد كان كل (ج) ب) بالإطلاق هذا خلف. أو بعكس الكبرى إلى لاشيء من (ب ا) دائماً ينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، ومن هاهنا يظهر أن السالبة الضرورية لو انعكست كنفسها أنتجت الضرورية في هذا الشكل ضرورية، فلما لم يبين ذلك اقتصر في النتيجة على الدوام. لا يقال: المقدمتان إذا كانتا ضروريتين لم يكن بد من صدق النتيجة ضرورية، لأن الأوسط إذا كان ضروري الثبوت لأحد الطرفين وضروري السلب عن الآخر يكون أحد الطرفين ضروري السلب عن الآخر، فكان بين الطرفين مباينة ضرورية فيكون نتيجة الطرفين ضرورية. لانا نقول: الحكم في المقدمتين ليس الأبان الأوسط ضروري الثبوت لذات أحد الطرفين وضروري السلب عن ذات الآخر، واللازم منه أن ذات أحد الطرفين ضروري السلب عن ذات الآخر وهو ليس بمطلوب بل المطلوب أن وصف أحد الطرفين ضروري السلب عن ذات الآخر، ولا يلزم من ضرورة سلب الذات ضرورة سلب الوصف لصدق قولنا في المثال المشهور «لاشيء من الحمار بفرس بالضرورة، وكل مركوب زيد فرس بالضرورة مع كذب قولنا ليس بعض الحمار مركوب زيـد بالضرورة»، لأن كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان.
The mixed [syllogisms]: From (A B) with necessity or always, there results from the first [premise] 'Some J is not B' with necessity or always, while it was 'Every J is B' absolutely - this is a contradiction. Or by converting the major to 'Nothing of (B A) always', there results the required conclusion. From here it appears that if the necessary negative converted as itself, it would produce a necessary [conclusion] in this figure, but since that was not shown, the conclusion is confined to 'always'. It is not said: if both premises are necessary, the conclusion must necessarily be true, because if the middle term is necessarily affirmed of one extreme and necessarily denied of the other, then one extreme is necessarily denied of the other, so there is necessary separation between the extremes, and thus the conclusion is necessary. We say: the judgment in the two premises is not that the middle is necessarily affirmed of the essence of one extreme and necessarily denied of the essence of the other; the necessary consequent is that the essence of one extreme is necessarily denied of the essence of the other, but that is not what is sought; rather, what is sought is that a description of one extreme is necessarily denied of the essence of the other. And the necessity of denying the essence does not entail the necessity of denying the description, because our statement in the well-known example is true: 'Nothing of the donkey is a horse necessarily', and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse necessarily', while our statement 'Some donkey is not ridden by Zayd necessarily' is false, because every donkey can be ridden by Zayd possibly.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage discusses mixed modal syllogisms, specifically the figure where the major premise is necessary and the minor is absolute. The author first describes a syllogism: from a major premise 'Every A is B necessarily or always' and a minor 'Every J is A absolutely', the conclusion would be 'Some J is not B necessarily or always', but this contradicts the minor. So by converting the major to 'Nothing of B is A always', the desired conclusion is obtained. The author notes that if the necessary negative proposition converted as itself (i.e., preserving necessity), the conclusion would be necessary, but it does not, so the conclusion is restricted to 'always'. Then an objection is raised: if both premises are necessary, the conclusion must be necessary, because the middle term's necessity would necessitate separation of the extremes. The author responds by distinguishing between the essence (dhat) and the description (wasf). The premises do not assert that the middle is necessarily affirmed of the essence of one extreme and denied of the essence of the other; rather, the judgment involves descriptions. From the necessity of the essence, the necessity of the description does not follow. The example of donkey, horse, and 'ridden by Zayd' illustrates: it is necessarily true that no donkey is a horse, and necessarily that everything ridden by Zayd is a horse, but it is not necessarily true that some donkey is not ridden by Zayd, because any donkey could possibly be ridden by Zayd. Thus, the necessity of the conclusion is not sustained.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essence (dhat) and description (wasf) is crucial for evaluating modal syllogisms; necessity of essence does not imply necessity of description.

Reasons for Dropping Existential and Necessity Conditions from the Minor Premise
Author explains why two conditions are dropped from the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical basis for dropping the existential condition and necessity modality from the minor premise in mixed modal syllogisms and identify when such dropping is justified.

Core Idea
The minor premise's existential and necessity conditions are dropped because they would block a valid conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms.

In this passage, the author continues his analysis of mixed modal syllogisms by explaining two important modifications: why the existential condition (qayd al-wujud) is dropped from the minor premise, and why the necessity modality is removed from the minor. These are not arbitrary but follow from the logical behavior of modal propositions in combination. Understanding these reasons is essential for correctly constructing and evaluating modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modifications
Note that the author discusses two separate modifications to the minor premise: dropping the existential condition and dropping the necessity modality. Locate where each is discussed in the passage.
2
Analyze the existential condition
Consider the two cases: when the major is simple (non-modal) and when it is compound (modal). For a simple major, the existential condition agrees in quality. For a compound major, no conclusion follows because the existential condition introduces a temporal qualification that does not align with the major's modality.
3
Analyze the necessity modality
The author assumes perpetuity is not true of the minor, so if it had necessity, it would be one of three types: conditional, temporal, or pervasive. The only relevant mixed cases are two conditionals or a temporal and a conditional.
4
Trace why necessity does not transfer
For two conditionals: necessity applies to combinations of essence and description, not to descriptions alone. For temporal and conditional: the temporal restriction prevents the necessity from transferring to the description of the major. In both cases, the desired necessary conclusion does not follow.
5
Consider the author's note on conversion
The author mentions that if the conditional proposition converted as itself, necessity would transfer, but this has not been shown. This highlights that the failure of transfer is due to the specific properties of modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حذف قيدي الوجود من الصغرى : فلأنها إن كانت مع كبرى بسيطة كان قيد وجودها موافقاً لها في الكيف، وإن كانت مع مركبة لم تنتج مع أصلها - كما ذكرنا - ولا مع قيد وجودها، لأن قيدي الوجود إما مطلقتان أو ممكنتان أو مطلقة وممكنة، ولا إنتاج في هذا الشكل منهما. وأما حذف الضرورة من الصغرى فلأن المقدر أن الدوام لا يصدق على الصغرى، فلو كان فيها ضرورة لكانت إما الضرورة المشروطة أو الضرورة الوقتيّة أو الضرورة المنتشرة، وأخص الاختلاطات من أحدها ومن مقدمة أخرى الاختلاط من مشروطتين أو من وقتية ومشروطة، والضرورة فيهما لم تتعد إلى النتيجة. أما في الاختلاط من المشروطتين فلأن الأوسط فيهما ضروري الثبوت لمجموع ذات أحد الطرفين ووصفه، و ضروري السلب عن مجموع ذات الطرف الآخر ،ووصفه، ولا يلزم منه إلا المنافاة الضرورية بين المجموعين والمطلوب ضرورة منافاة وصف أحد الطرفين لمجموع ذات الطرف الآخر ووصفه، وهو غير لازم. وأما في الاختلاط من الوقتية والمشروطة، فلأن الأوسط إذا كان ضروري الثبوت للأصغر في بعض أوقات ذاته، وضروري السلب عن الأكبر بشرط الوصف لم يلزم منه إلا أن ذات الأكبر مع وصفه ضروري السلب عن الأصغر في بعض الأوقات. وأما أن وصف الأكبر ضروري السلب عن ذات الأصغر فلايلزم، الجواز أن يكون لزوم ضرورة السلب ناشئاً من اقتران الذات بالوصف. نعم لو ظهر انعكاس المشروطة كنفسها تعدت الضرورة من الصغرى، لكنه لم يبين.
And as for dropping the two existential conditions from the minor: because if it is with a simple major, its existential condition agrees with it in quality; and if it is with a compound [major], it does not produce with its original [form] — as we mentioned — nor with its existential condition, because the two existential conditions are either both absolute, both possible, or one absolute and one possible, and no production occurs from them in this figure. And as for dropping necessity from the minor: because it is assumed that perpetuity is not true of the minor; so if it had necessity, it would be either conditional necessity, temporal necessity, or pervasive necessity. The most specific mixed cases from one of these and another premise are the mix of two conditionals, or a temporal and a conditional. Necessity does not transfer to the conclusion in these two. As for the mix of two conditionals: because the middle term in them is necessarily affirmed of the combination of the essence of one extreme and its description, and necessarily denied of the combination of the essence of the other extreme and its description, and nothing follows from this except necessary opposition between the two combinations; but what is sought is the necessity of the opposition of the description of one extreme to the combination of the essence of the other extreme and its description, which is not necessary. And as for the mix of a temporal and a conditional: because if the middle term is necessarily true of the minor in some times of its essence, and necessarily denied of the major with the condition of description, then nothing follows except that the essence of the major with its description is necessarily denied of the minor in some times. But that the description of the major is necessarily denied of the essence of the minor does not follow, because it is possible that the necessity of the denial arises from the conjunction of essence and description. Yes, if the conditional [proposition] converted as itself, necessity would transfer from the minor, but this has not been shown.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed modal syllogisms by explaining two modifications: dropping the existential condition (qayd al-wujud) from the minor premise, and dropping the necessity modality from the minor premise. (1) Dropping the existential condition: If the minor is existential (indicating existence of its subject), and the major is a simple (non-modal) proposition, the existential condition aligns with the major's quality; but if the major is a compound modal proposition (e.g., necessary or possible), no conclusion follows because the existential condition introduces a temporal qualification that does not align with the major's modality. The author notes that the existential conditions are either both absolute, both possible, or one absolute and one possible; none produce a conclusion in this figure. (2) Dropping necessity from the minor: It is assumed that perpetuity (dawam) does not apply to the minor; if it had necessity, it would be of three types: conditional (shartiyya), temporal (waqtiyya), or pervasive (muntashira). The only mixed cases that occur are either two conditionals (both conditional) or a temporal and a conditional. In both cases, necessity does not transfer to the conclusion. For two conditionals: The middle term is necessarily affirmed of the combination of essence and description of one extreme, and necessarily denied of the combination of essence and description of the other. This only yields necessary opposition between the whole combinations, not the required necessity of the description of one against the combination of the other. For a temporal and a conditional: The middle is necessarily true of the minor at some times, and necessarily denied of the major under the condition of description. This yields only that the major with its description is necessarily denied of the minor at some times, but not that the description of the major is necessarily denied of the essence of the minor. The author concludes by noting that if the conditional converted as itself, necessity would transfer, but this has not been established.

The minor premise's existential and necessity conditions are omitted in mixed modal syllogisms because they would prevent a necessary conclusion from following in the first figure.
