Second Figure Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Why Necessity Does Not Transfer
Author explains why certain mixed modal premises in the second figure yield no necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of necessity (conditioned, temporal, diffused) and explain why mixtures of two conditioned premises or a temporal and a conditioned premise in the second figure fail to produce a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Not every modal premise combination in the second figure yields a necessary conclusion.

In this passage, the author examines mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure. He explains that when necessity appears in the minor premise but is of a conditioned, temporal, or diffused type, the necessity does not extend to the conclusion. By analyzing two specific mixtures—two conditioned premises and a temporal with a conditioned—he shows why the required necessary incompatibility fails to follow.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three types of necessity
Recognize conditioned necessity (predicate holds as long as subject has a certain description), temporal necessity (holds at a specific time), and diffused necessity (holds at indefinite times). These are key to understanding the failure of modal transfer.
The author assumes the minor premise does not carry permanence, but if it did, it would be one of these three.
2
Identify the two key mixtures
The author focuses on the mixture of two conditioned premises and the mixture of a temporal and a conditioned premise. These are the most specific mixtures that still fail to transfer necessity.
Other mixtures (absolute-absolute, possible-possible, etc.) are dismissed earlier as non-productive.
3
Analyze the two-conditioned mixture
The middle term is necessarily affirmed of the sum (essence+description) of one extreme and necessarily negated of the sum of the other. This yields only necessary incompatibility between the two sums, not the required necessary incompatibility between the description of one extreme and the sum of the other.
The desired conclusion would be stronger; the premises are insufficient.
4
Analyze the temporal-conditioned mixture
The middle term is necessarily affirmed of the minor at some times, and necessarily negated of the major under its description. This implies only that the major's essence with its description is necessarily negated of the minor at some times. It does not give the necessary negation of the major's description alone from the minor's essence.
The necessity might arise from the conjunction of essence and description, not from the description alone.
5
Consider the possibility of conversion
The author notes that if the conditioned premise could be converted preserving its modal force, necessity would transfer from the minor. However, this conversion has not been established.
This shows the argument depends on the inability to convert conditioned premises as themselves.

الوجود إما مطلقتان أو ممكنتان أو مطلقة وممكنة، ولا إنتاج في هذا الشكل منهما. وأما حذف الضرورة من الصغرى فلأن المقدر أن الدوام لا يصدق على الصغرى، فلو كان فيها ضرورة لكانت إما الضرورة المشروطة أو الضرورة الوقتيّة أو الضرورة المنتشرة، وأخص الاختلاطات من أحدها ومن مقدمة أخرى الاختلاط من مشروطتين أو من وقتية ومشروطة، والضرورة فيهما لم تتعد إلى النتيجة. أما في الاختلاط من المشروطتين فلأن الأوسط فيهما ضروري الثبوت لمجموع ذات أحد الطرفين ووصفه، وضروري السلب عن مجموع ذات الطرف الآخر ووصفه، ولا يلزم منه إلا المنافاة الضرورية بين المجموعين والمطلوب ضرورة منافاة وصف أحد الطرفين لمجموع ذات الطرف الآخر ووصفه، وهو غير لازم. وأما في الاختلاط من الوقتية والمشروطة، فلأن الأوسط إذا كان ضروري الثبوت للأصغر في بعض أوقات ذاته، وضروري السلب عن الأكبر بشرط الوصف لم يلزم منه إلا أن ذات الأكبر مع وصفه ضروري السلب عن الأصغر في بعض الأوقات. وأما أن وصف الأكبر ضروري السلب عن ذات الأصغر فلا يلزم، الجواز أن يكون لزوم ضرورة السلب ناشئاً من اقتران الذات بالوصف. نعم لو ظهر انعكاس المشروطة كنفسها تعدت الضرورة من الصغرى، لكنه لم يتبين.

The existence [of premises] is either two absolute, or two possible, or an absolute and a possible, and there is no production in this figure from them. As for dropping necessity from the minor, it is because it is assumed that permanence does not apply to the minor; so if there were necessity in it, it would be either conditioned necessity, temporal necessity, or diffused necessity. The most specific of the mixtures from one of them and another premise is the mixture of two conditioned [premises] or of a temporal and a conditioned, and the necessity in them does not extend to the conclusion. As for the mixture of two conditioned [premises], because the middle term in them is necessary of affirmation for the sum of the essence of one of the two extremes and its description, and necessary of negation for the sum of the essence of the other extreme and its description, only the necessary incompatibility between the two sums follows, but what is required is the necessity of the incompatibility of the description of one of the extremes for the sum of the essence of the other extreme and its description, which is not entailed. As for the mixture of a temporal and a conditioned, because if the middle term is necessary of affirmation for the minor in some times of its essence, and necessary of negation for the major with the condition of the description, it follows only that the essence of the major together with its description is necessarily negated of the minor in some times. But that the description of the major is necessarily negated of the essence of the minor does not follow; it is possible that the entailment of the necessity of the negation arises from the conjunction of the essence with the description. Yes, if the conversion of the conditioned [premise] as itself were manifest, the necessity would extend from the minor, but it has not been established. Possible general: possible general, possible general, possible general, barren. Diffused absolute: barren. Possible special: possible general, possible general, barren. Barren.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure produce a conclusion. The author begins by stating that when both premises are absolute, both possible, or one absolute and one possible, no conclusion follows in this figure. Then he addresses the case where necessity is dropped from the minor premise. The assumption is that the minor does not carry permanence (i.e., it is not necessary). If it did have necessity, it would be one of three types: conditioned necessity (where the predicate holds for the subject as long as the subject is described by a certain attribute), temporal necessity (holds for a specific time), or diffused necessity (holds at indefinite times). The author focuses on two specific mixtures: both premises being conditioned, and one temporal with one conditioned. He explains why the necessity does not carry to the conclusion in these cases. For two conditioned premises, the middle term is necessarily affirmed of the sum of the essence and description of one extreme, and necessarily negated of the sum of the essence and description of the other. This only yields necessary incompatibility between the two sums, but not the required necessary incompatibility between the description of one extreme and the sum of essence and description of the other. Hence the conclusion is not necessary. For a temporal and conditioned mixture, the middle is necessarily affirmed of the minor at some times, and necessarily negated of the major under the condition of its description. This only implies that the major's essence with its description is necessarily negated of the minor at some times. It does not give the necessary negation of the major's description alone from the minor's essence. The necessity might arise from the conjunction of essence and description. The author notes that if the conditioned premise could be converted as itself (i.e., preserving its modal force), necessity would carry from the minor, but this conversion has not been proven. The table that follows appears to list the results (e.g., absolute general, barren) for various combinations of premise types (general conditioned, special conditioned, general Arabic, general conventional, etc.) in the second figure, but the OCR is too corrupted to read accurately.

Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Condition of the Minor Premise
Author states the condition for the third figure mixed syllogisms and how to derive the conclusion's modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a valid third figure mixed modal syllogism and derive the conclusion's modality based on the major premise type.

Core Idea
Minor must be actual; if possible, middle term fails to subsume the minor.

In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. The author explains a crucial modal condition: the minor premise cannot be possible; it must be actual. He then gives a rule for deriving the conclusion's modality when the major is among four special types. The commentary unpacks why actuality is necessary for the judgment to transfer from the middle to the minor. Let's walk through the logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the third figure
Find in the Arabic the phrase 'فعلية الصغرى'. This is the core condition: the minor premise must assert actual existence, not just possibility.
2
Understand why possibility fails
The commentary argues: if the minor were possible, the middle term (subject of both premises) would only be possibly the minor. Since the major premise predicates something of the middle actually, the minor might not actually fall under the middle, blocking the conclusion.
This is a conditional argument: if minor is possible, then subsumption fails.
3
General rule for conclusion modality
If the major premise is not one of the four special types, the conclusion's modality matches the major premise exactly.
The 'four types' refer to two general (general absolute, general possible) and two special (existential non-permanent, existential non-necessary).
4
Apply special rules for the four types
If the major is one of the two general types, the conclusion is the converse of the minor with non-permanence removed. If the major is one of the two special types, the conclusion is the converse of the minor with non-permanence added.
Non-permanence (اللا دوام) indicates the property does not hold at all times.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Construct a valid third figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major, general absolute); Some intoxicants are nabidh (minor, actual). Therefore some nabidh is forbidden.' Since the major is general absolute (one of the two general types), the conclusion should be the converse of the minor (some nabidh is intoxicant) with non-permanence removed (i.e., 'some nabidh is intoxicant' without temporal restriction).
This illustrates how the rule works in a fiqh context.

شرائط انتاج المختلطات وجهة النتائج في الشكل الثالث] :قال: وأمّا الشكل الثالث فشرطه فعليّة الصغرى والنتيجة كالكبرى إن كانت الكبرى غير الأربع، وإلا فكعكس الصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللا دوام إن كانت الكبرى إحدى العامتين ومضموماً إليها إن كانت إحدى الخاصتين.
القول: شرط إنتاج الشكل الثالث بحسب الجهة أن تكون الصغرى فعلية، لأنها لو كانت ممكنة لم يلزم تعدّى الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، لأن الحكم في الكبرى على ما هو أوسط بالفعل، والأوسط ليس بأصغر بالفعل - بل بالإمكان - فجاز أن لا يصدق الأصغر بالفعل على الأوسط، فلم يندرج الأصغر تحته، فلايلزم من الحكم بالأكبر على الأوسط.
Conditions for producing mixed [syllogisms] and the modality of results in the third figure. He said: As for the third figure, its condition is the actuality of the minor, and the conclusion is like the major if the major is not among the four [types]; otherwise, it is like the converse of the minor, removing non-permanence from it if the major is one of the two general types, and adding it if it is one of the two special types. The commentary: The condition for production of the third figure according to modality is that the minor be actual, because if it were possible, the judgment would not necessarily transfer from the middle to the minor; for the judgment in the major is about what is actually middle, and the middle is not actually minor—rather possibly—so it is possible that the minor is not actually true of the middle, thus the minor is not subsumed under it, so the judgment of the major about the middle does not entail.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents the rules for mixed modal syllogisms in the third figure. It begins with an explicit statement: the condition for a valid third figure syllogism in modal terms is that the minor premise must be actual (i.e., not merely possible). The conclusion's modality mirrors that of the major premise except when the major falls into one of four special types. If the major is one of the two 'general' types (likely absolute general or possible general), the conclusion becomes the converse of the minor with non-permanence removed. If the major is one of the two 'special' types (perhaps existential non-permanent or existential non-necessary), the conclusion is the converse of the minor with non-permanence added. The commentary explains why the minor must be actual: if it were possible, the middle term in the major premise is taken actually, but the middle is only possibly the minor, so the minor might not actually apply to the middle. Therefore the minor is not subsumed under the middle, and the judgment about the middle does not transfer to the minor. This ensures the syllogism's validity. The 'four' types likely refer to the standard classifications of modal premises in the tradition.

Checkpoint
In the third figure, what is the condition regarding the minor premise for a valid modal mixed syllogism?
A) It must be necessary.
B) It must be possible.
C) It must be actual.
D) It must be absolute.

Key Takeaway
The minor premise in a third figure mixed syllogism must be actual to ensure the middle term actually subsumes the minor, allowing the conclusion to follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third figure modal syllogism about a fiqh ruling: major premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (general absolute), minor premise 'Some intoxicants are nabidh' (actual). Determine the conclusion's modality following the author's rule.
?
Given a major premise that is a special type (e.g., existential non-necessary: 'Some scholars are wealthy at some time'), and a minor that is actual (e.g., 'Some scholars are poets'), derive the conclusion's modality and explain the addition of non-permanence.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Conditions for the Third Figure in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author details conditions for third figure's productivity and resulting modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a third-figure mixed modal categorical syllogism and verify the condition that the minor premise must be actual.

Core Idea
The minor premise must be actual, not possible, for the third figure to yield a conclusion.

In mixed modal syllogisms, the third figure imposes a special requirement: the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya). This lesson unpacks why this condition is necessary and how the modality of the conclusion is determined based on the type of the major premise. We will work through the author's rule, its justification, and the adjustments required for the descriptive propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Recognize that in the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Note the modality of each premise: actual, possible, necessary, etc.
The minor premise must be actual; if it is possible, no conclusion follows.
2
Check the actuality condition
Examine whether the minor premise is actual. If it is possible, the middle term may not actually apply to the minor, so the subsumption fails and no conclusion can be drawn.
The Zayd and 'Amr example illustrates this: a possible minor prevents a factual conclusion.
3
Classify the major premise
Determine whether the major premise is one of the four descriptive propositions (al-wasfiyyāt al-arba') or from the remaining nine types. This classification dictates the rule for the conclusion's modality.
The four descriptive propositions are: two general (masruṭa 'āmma, 'urfiyya 'āmma) and two special (masruṭa khāṣṣa, 'urfiyya khāṣṣa).
4
Apply the conclusion rule
If the major is not among the four, the conclusion has the same modality as the major. If it is among the four, the conclusion is like the converse of the minor, with non-permanence removed (if major is general) or permanence added (if major is special).
The proof uses methods like reductio ad absurdum, conversion, and supposition.

قال: وأمّا الشكل الثالث فشرطه فعليّة الصغرى والنتيجة كالكبرى إن كانت الكبرى غير الأربع، وإلا فكعكس الصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللادوام إن كانت الكبرى إحدى العامتين ومضموماً إليها إن كانت إحدى الخاصتين.
القول: شرط إنتاج الشكل الثالث بحسب الجهة أن تكون الصغرى فعلية، لأنها لو كانت ممكنة لم يلزم تعدّى الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر، لأن الحكم في الكبرى على ما هو أوسط بالفعل، والأوسط ليس بأصغر بالفعل - بل بالإمكان - فجاز أن لا يصدق الأصغر بالفعل على الأوسط، فلم يندرج الأصغر تحته، فلايلزم من الحكـم بـالأكبر على الأوسط الحكم به على الأصغر ؛ كما إذا فرضنا أن زيداً يركب الفرس ولم يركب الحمار، وعمراً يركب الحمار دون الفرس، يصدق قولنا : كل ما هو مركوب زيد مركوب عمرو بالإمكان، وكل مركوب زيد فرس بالفعل مع كذب قولنا : بعض ما هو مركوب عمرو فرس بالفعل بل بالإمكان العام، لأن كل ما هو مركوب عمرو حمار بالضرورة، فلما لم يصدق مركوب عمرو بالفعل على مركوب زيد لم يندرج الأصغر تحته حتى يتعدى الحكم منه إليه.
وباعتبار هذا الشرط سقط من الاختلاطات الممكنة الانعقاد سنّة وعشرون اختلاطاً وبقيت الاختلاطات المنتجة مائة وثلاثة وأربعين.
والكبرى فيها إما أن تكون إحدى الوصفيات الأربع أو لا تكون، فإن تكن إحدى الوصفيات الأربع - بل إحدى التسع الباقية – كانت جهة النتيجة جهة الكبرى بعينها ) ، وإن كانت إحدى الأربع فالنتيجة كعكس الصغرى محذوفاً. عنه اللادوام - إن كان العكس مقيداً به - ومضموماً إليه لادوام الكبرى - إن كانت إحدى الخاصتين.
أما أن النتيجة كالكبرى أو كعكس الصغرى فبالطرق المذكورة من الخلف والعكس والافتراض على ماسبق بيانها.
وأما حذف اللادوام من عكس الصغرى فلأن عكس الصغرى
(١) فيه بحث، لأن الصغرى إن كانت إحدى الدائمتين والكبري مطلقة عامة فعلى الضابط المذكور تكون النتيجة مطلقة عامة، والحق أن النتيجة مطلقة حينية، وتفصيله يطلب من شرح المطالع (شريف).
104 - Conditions for the production of mixed [syllogisms] and the modality of the results in the third figure.
He said: As for the third figure, its condition is the actuality of the minor premise, and the result is like the major if the major is not one of the four; otherwise, it is like the converse of the minor, with the non-permanence removed from it if the major is one of the two general [types], and with the permanence of the major added to it if it is one of the two special [types].
The commentary: The condition for the production of the third figure with respect to modality is that the minor premise be actual, because if it were possible, the transfer of the judgment from the middle term to the minor term would not be necessary, because the judgment in the major is about what is actually the middle, and the middle is not actually the minor—only possibly—so it is possible that the minor does not actually apply to the middle, so the minor is not subsumed under it, so it is not necessary from the judgment of the major term about the middle to judge it about the minor; as if we assume that Zayd rides the horse and not the donkey, and 'Amr rides the donkey and not the horse, our statement is true: 'Everything that is ridden by Zayd is ridden by 'Amr possibly', and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse actually', while our statement 'Some of what is ridden by 'Amr is a horse actually' is false, but rather [true] with general possibility, because everything that is ridden by 'Amr is a donkey necessarily. Since what is ridden by 'Amr actually does not apply to what is ridden by Zayd, the minor is not subsumed under it so that the judgment transfers from it to it.
And by considering this condition, twenty-six possible mixed [syllogisms] fall away, and the productive mixed [syllogisms] remain one hundred and forty-three.
The major in them is either one of the four descriptive [propositions] or not. If it is one of the four descriptive—rather one of the remaining nine—then the modality of the result is exactly the modality of the major. And if it is one of the four, then the result is like the converse of the minor, with the non-permanence removed from it—if the converse is restricted by it—and the permanence of the major added to it—if it is one of the two special [types].
As for the result being like the major or like the converse of the minor, [it is determined] by the mentioned methods of reductio ad absurdum, conversion, and supposition, according to what was previously explained.
As for removing the non-permanence from the converse of the minor, [there is a discussion], because if the minor is one of the two permanent [propositions] and the major is an absolute general [proposition], then according to the mentioned rule the result would be an absolute general, but the truth is that the result is a temporal absolute. Its detail is sought from the commentary on al-Matali' (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the third figure of the categorical syllogism when the premises have different modalities (mixed modal syllogisms). The author first states the rule in a compressed form (the matn): "As for the third figure, its condition is the actuality of the minor premise, and the result is like the major if the major is not one of the four; otherwise, it is like the converse of the minor, with the non-permanence removed from it if the major is one of the two general [types], and with the permanence of the major added to it if it is one of the two special [types]." Then the commentary (al-qawl) expands this.

First, the requirement that the minor premise be actual (fi'liyya) is justified: if the minor were possible (mumkina), the middle term might not actually apply to the minor, so the transfer of judgment from the middle to the minor would not be guaranteed. A concrete example is given: suppose Zayd rides a horse only, and 'Amr rides a donkey only. The statement "Everything ridden by Zayd is ridden by 'Amr" is true with possibility (since what Zayd rides could be ridden by 'Amr in some possible world), and "Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse" is true actually. But "Some of what is ridden by 'Amr is a horse" is false actually, because everything 'Amr rides is necessarily a donkey. Since "what is ridden by 'Amr actually" does not apply to Zayd's ride, the minor term ('Amr) is not subsumed under the middle, so no conclusion follows about 'Amr.

Due to this condition, 26 possible mixed syllogisms in the third figure are excluded, leaving 143 productive ones.

Next, the rule for the conclusion's modality: if the major premise is not one of the four descriptive propositions (i.e., it is from the remaining nine types of propositions in the standard classification), then the conclusion has the same modality as the major. If the major is one of the four descriptive types (two general: conditional general and customary general; two special: conditional special and customary special), then the conclusion is like the converse of the minor, but with adjustments: if the major is one of the two general descriptive types, the conclusion removes the non-permanence (lā dawām) from the converse of the minor; if the major is one of the two special descriptive types, the conclusion adds the permanence (dawām) of the major to the converse of the minor. The proof of these rules is by known methods: reductio ad absurdum (khalaf), conversion ('aks), and supposition (iftirad).

Finally, a note (baḥth) is added: if the minor is one of the two permanent propositions (dā'imatān) and the major is absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma), the rule would give an absolute general conclusion, but the correct conclusion is a temporal absolute (muṭlaqa ḥīniyya). The reader is referred to the commentary on al-Matali' by al-Sharif for details.

لادوام

Checkpoint
In the third figure, if the minor premise is possible and the major is actual, can a conclusion be drawn?
A) Yes, because the mood is valid.
B) No, because the minor must be actual for the middle to actually apply to the minor.
C) Yes, but only if the conclusion is possible.
D) No, because the third figure requires both premises actual.

Key Takeaway
The condition for productivity in the third figure of mixed modal syllogisms is that the minor premise must be actual, and the modality of the conclusion depends on the type of the major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a third-figure syllogism with premises: 'Every B is C necessarily' (major) and 'Every A is B possibly' (minor). Determine whether a conclusion follows and, if so, its modality according to the rules in this passage.
?
Using the Zayd and 'Amr example from the text, explain in your own words why the minor premise must be actual and why the attempted conclusion fails.
